The author of this book says: When I have completed for you, O my sons, this book, which is a part of medical science in its entirety, and have reached the utmost clarity and explanation in it, I saw fit to complete it for you with this chapter, which is a part of the practical work.
Because practical work is completely lacking in our country and in our time, so much so that its knowledge is almost extinct and its influence has ceased. And only a few outlines remain of it in the books of the ancients, which have been copied by hands and affected by error and confusion, until its meanings have become obscure and its benefit has been lost.
Therefore, I saw fit to revive it and to compose this chapter on it in a way that is explanatory, clear, and concise, and to include pictures of cauterizing irons and other surgical instruments, since this increases clarity and is very necessary.
The reason why there is no skilled craftsman in our time is that the craft of medicine is lengthy, and its practitioner must beforehand practice in the science of anatomy, as described by Galen. Until he understands the functions, forms, temperaments, connections, and separations of the organs, and knows the bones, nerves, muscles, their number, and their origins and the pulsating and non-pulsating vessels, and the places of their origins.
Therefore, Hippocrates said that physicians are many in name but few in deed, especially in the craft of surgery, and we have mentioned a part of this in the introduction to this book.
For he who is not knowledgeable in the anatomy we have mentioned will inevitably make mistakes that kill people, as I have often witnessed among those who have imagined themselves learned in this science and claimed it without knowledge or experience.
And that is because I saw an ignorant physician who incised a swine erysipelas in a woman's neck, and severed some of the neck's arteries, so the woman bled until she fell dead in his hands.
And I saw another physician who proceeded to remove a stone from a man who had been stabbed in the bladder; the stone was large, so he acted rashly and extracted it with a piece of the bladder's substance. So the man died after about three days, and I had been called to remove it; and from the size of the stone and the condition of the patient, I had estimated that.
And I saw another physician who earned his living practicing medicine among some of the procurers of our city.
Then a black boy who had a fracture in his leg near the heel, along with a wound, came to him.  In his ignorance, the physician quickly bound the fracture over the wound with bandages and splints very tightly, and left no room for the wound to breathe.
Then he indulged his desires, and left him for days, ordering him not to untie the bandage until his leg and foot swelled and he was near death.
Then I was called to him, and I quickly untied the bandage; so he found relief and was freed from his pains, except that the corruption had become firmly established in the limb, and I could not prevent it.
Then the corruption continued to spread in the limb until he died.
And I saw another physician cauterize a cancerous tumor, which ulcerated after days until his patient's affliction became great.  That is because if a cancer is purely of a black humor, then it should not be touched with an iron instrument at all.
Except it be in a limb that can be entirely excised.
Therefore, my son, you should know that manual operations are divided into two parts: an operation accompanied by safety, and an operation accompanied by harm in most cases.
And I have warned in every place where it occurs in this book about the operation which involves risk and fear, therefore you should avoid and reject it.
Lest the ignorant find a way to criticize and attack, therefore take precautions and circumspection for yourselves, and gentleness and deliberation for your patients.
And use the best method leading to safety and a favorable outcome, and avoid dangerous diseases that are difficult to cure.
And protect yourselves from that which you fear might cast suspicion on your religion and your worldly affairs, for this is more lasting for your reputation and higher for your standing in this world and the next.
For Galen said in some of his precepts, "Do not treat a bad disease, lest you become a bad physician.
This chapter is divided into three sections.
The first section.
On cauterization with fire and with strong medicine, arranged from head to foot, with illustrations of the instruments and cautery irons, and everything needed for the work by hand.
And the second section.
On incision, perforation, bloodletting, cupping, wounds, and the extraction of arrows and the like, all arranged systematically with illustrations of the instruments.
And the third section.
On setting, traction, and the treatment of dislocations and the like, arranged systematically from head to foot, with illustrations of the instruments.
The first section.
On cauterization.
And before we describe its use, it is necessary to mention its benefits and harms, and in what temperament it is used.
And I say that the discussion of the benefits and harms of cauterization is lengthy and a precise science and a hidden secret, and a number of wise men have spoken about it.
And they differed in it, and I have shortened from their speech a little for fear of lengthening.
And I say that cauterization generally benefits every bad temperament that is with a substance and without a substance, except for two temperaments, which are the hot temperament without a substance and the dry temperament without a substance.
As for the dry temperament with a substance, they differed concerning it. Some of them said that cauterization is beneficial in it, and others said the opposite, that cauterization is not suitable in a disease that is from heat and dryness, because the nature of fire is heat and dryness.
And it is impossible to be cured of a hot dry disease with a hot dry medicine. And he who says the opposite said that cauterization with fire may benefit from a hot dry disease that occurs in the bodies of people. Because when you add the body of man and its moisture to the temperament of fire, you hit the body of man with cold.
And I say with his saying, because experience has revealed that to me many times, except that one should not imagine that matter except one who has trained and practiced in the chapter of cauterization a thorough practice. And he stood upon the difference of the temperaments of people and the state of diseases in themselves, and their causes, and their symptoms, and the length of their time.
But as for the other temperaments, there is no fear for you from them, especially the cold and moist diseases, for all the physicians have agreed upon them and have not differed in the benefit of cauterization in them.
And know, O my sons, that of the secret of treatment, cauterization with fire and its advantage over cauterization with burning medicine, is because fire is a simple element, its action does not exceed the member which is cauterized, nor does it harm another member connected to it except a little harm.
And cauterization with burning medicine, its effect may exceed to what is beyond of the members, and perhaps it causes in the member a disease difficult to treat, and perhaps it kills, and fire, for its nobility and the excellence of its substance, does not do that except if one exaggerates.
And that has become clear to us by experience, through long service and care in the craft and standing upon the realities of the matter.
For this reason I have dispensed with lengthy discussion, and were it not that it is not befitting to this book of mine, I would have presented to you concerning fire a hidden secret and its manner of action in bodies and its expulsion of diseases with philosophical, demonstrative discourse too subtle for your understanding.
And know, O my sons, that they have differed concerning the time which is suitable for cauterization, and they have made the best time the time of spring.
And I say that cauterization is suitable at every time, because the harm occurring from the time is consumed in the benefit which is brought about by cauterization. Especially if the cauterization is for strong, necessary, urgent pains which do not tolerate delay, because of what is feared from them that it will be followed by a calamity which is greater than the slight harm entering from the time.
And let it not occur to you, O my sons, what the common people and ignorant physicians imagine, that cauterization which cures a certain disease will never return, and they make it a blame.
And the matter is not as they suppose, because cauterization is only in the position of medicine which changes the temperament and dries the humors which are the cause of the occurrence of pains.
Except that cauterization is preferred to medicine in the speed of its success, the strength of its action, and the intensity of its power.
And it is possible that the disease may return at some time, according to the temperament of the patient, the power of his disease, and its strength, and what is prepared in his body of the gathering of impurities in it, and his neglect of himself in acquiring them from foods and similar causes.
Except that the disease in which cauterization is used is a mild disease and in a member with few impurities and humors. Like cauterizing a tooth for pain and the like, for it is possible that that pain may not return, and that is in the least.
And as for the common people's saying also that cauterization is the last of medicine, it is a correct saying, not to what they go to, for they believe that no treatment is beneficial with medicine or without it after the occurrence of cauterization, and the matter is otherwise.
And the meaning of cauterization being the last of medicine is only that when we use types of treatment in a disease and those medicines do not work, then we use cauterization as the last thing, and it works; from here it came to be that cauterization is the last of medicine. Not in the sense to which the common people and many ignorant physicians went.
The ancients mentioned that cauterization with gold is better than cauterization with iron, and they said that because of the moderation of gold and the nobility of its essence, and they said that the place of cauterization does not suppurate; and that is not absolutely the case.
Because I have tried it and found that it only does that in some bodies and not others, and cauterization with it is better and superior to iron, as they said, except that if you heat the cautery iron in the fire of gold, you will not know when it is heated to the degree you want because of the redness of the gold.
And because it quickly becomes cold, and if you increase its heat in the fire, it melts and is wasted; therefore, the craftsman is troubled by that.  Therefore, cauterization with iron is quicker and closer to the truth for us to work with, God willing.
And I have arranged this chapter on cauterization into sections that I have organized from head to foot, so that it may be easy for the student what he wants from it, God willing.
Chapter One.
Cauterization of the head, one cauterization.
This cauterization benefits from the excess of moisture and coldness in the brain, which are the cause of headaches and frequent colds from the head to the region of the eyes and ears, excessive sleep, toothache, and throat pain. And in short, for every illness that results from cold, such as paralysis, epilepsy, apoplexy, and similar illnesses.
The method of this cauterization is that you first order the patient to vomit using a purgative medicine that cleanses the head for three or four nights, according to the patient's strength, age, and habit.
Then you order him to shave his head with a razor, then you seat him before you cross-legged, placing his hands on his chest.
Then place the base of your palm on the root of his nose between his eyes; where your middle finger reaches, mark that spot with ink.
Then heat the olive-shaped cautery iron, which is of this form.
Expose the bone the size of a toothpick head or the size of a carob seed; then lift your hand, or else, if it has cooled, replace your hand with the same instrument or another. Until you see the amount of bone I mentioned to you. Then take some salt, dissolve it in water, and soak a cotton swab in it, place it on the area and leave it for three days.
Then apply a cotton swab soaked in butter to it and leave it there until the eschar from the cautery separates. Then treat it with the four-ingredient ointment until it heals, God willing.
And they have said that the longer a wound remains open, the more pus it discharges, and this is better and more beneficial.
Some have mentioned cauterizing the skin to the bone, holding the cautery until some of the thickness of the bone is burned, then removing what was burned from the bone, and then treating it.
And others said that it is necessary to exaggerate the cauterization so that it strongly affects the bone, until it falls from the bone like a small carat or disc.  They claimed that this vents the vapors of the head from that area and leaves the wound open for a long time.
Then it is treated until it heals.  I do not see these two types of cauterization at all except in some people and by way of risk.  In my opinion, leaving it is better and safer, for the head weakens whenever its natural connection is separated, as we have witnessed in other members.
Especially if the patient's head is naturally weak. The first type of cauterization is safer and better in my opinion, and I use it.  Act accordingly and you will be safe, God willing.
Chapter Two.
On cauterizing the head also.
If a chronic pain occurs in the head and it persists in the patient, and he has used purgatives, clysters, snuffs, ointments, and dressings, especially if he has been cauterized with the single cauterization that we described and none of that has benefited him, then look. If the patient's head is naturally strong and not weak, and he feels intense cold, cauterize him again a little above the previous cauterization. Then cauterize each temple of his head until the thickness of the skin is gone and the amount of bone that we described is revealed. And cauterize him at the back of his head in the place known as the hatchet, and lighten your hand in this and do not uncover the bone, for the patient will feel a severe pain from it, unlike the pain of all the other cauterizations of the head, and I will mention this cauterization in its place.
And the cautery with which the temples and the back of the head are cauterized should be gentler than the cautery with which the middle of the head is cauterized, and this is its image.
Chapter Three.
On cauterizing non-chronic hemicrania.
If pain occurs in a fissure of the head with headache, and the pain extends to the eye, then let the patient vomit with purgative medicines for the head, and use all the treatment which I mentioned in the divisions of diseases; and if that does not work, then cauterization is done in two ways. Either cauterization with a sharp, burning medicine or with iron.
As for cauterization with a burning medicine, take a single clove of garlic, peel it, and cut its ends on both sides, then incise the place of pain in the temple with a wide scalpel until there is a space under the skin that will accommodate the clove.
Then insert it under the skin until it disappears, then bind it tightly with bandages, and leave it for fifteen hours, then unbind it, remove the garlic, and leave the wound for two or three days, then dress it.
Apply a cotton swab soaked in butter until the area suppurates, then treat it with ointment until it heals, God willing.  If you wish, do this with some of the burning medicines which I gave in the eighteenth chapter on burning medicines.
As for cauterization with iron, it is in this manner: heat the cautery, the image of which is this:
And it is called the nail cautery because its head is like the head of a nail, with some perforation and a small projection in its middle.
Then place it on the painful area and hold your hand while you rotate the iron little by little, and the amount you burn should be about half the thickness of the skin. Then lift your hand so as not to burn the artery beneath and cause bleeding. Then soak cotton in salt water and apply it to the area and leave it for three days. Then dress the cotton with butter, then treat it with ointment until it heals, God willing.
And if you wish, cauterize this headache with the knife-like end of the cautery, and be careful not to cut the artery in this chronic headache, especially.
Chapter Four.
Cauterization of the chronic headache.
If you treat the headache with what we have mentioned of the preceding treatment and what we mentioned in the divisions of diseases, and the treatment does not work, and you see from the ailment something that what we mentioned of the first cauterization with medicine or cauterization with fire cannot overcome. Then you should heat the knife-like cautery until it turns white, after you have drawn a line on the painful area, its length half a finger or so, and you should withdraw your hand once, while you are pulling it, until you cut the artery and reach the bone.
Except that you should be careful about the contact of the jaw which moves during chewing, lest you burn the muscle or its motor nerve, and spasms result.
And be cautious and watchful of the bleeding from the artery which you have cut, for there is danger in cutting it. Especially for one who is ignorant of what he does and has not been a practiced expert; and leaving the work is preferable.
And the treatment of bleeding occurring from an artery will be mentioned in its place in the book, God willing; if you see a condition for which this cauterization is not sufficient.
And you saw the patient's body able to withstand cauterization in the middle of the head as we described, and treat the wound until it heals, God willing.
And if you wish, use the cauterization that we mentioned in the chapter on the incision of the artery with the double-bladed cautery, for it is a better and more effective cauterization than this.
Chapter Five.
In cauterizing the pains of the ears.
If pain occurs in the ear due to cold and it is treated with laxatives and all the treatment that we mentioned in the classification, and the pain does not go away, then heat the cautery which is called the point, whose image is this.
Then, after heating it, apply it with a point around the entire ear, as it goes around, or around both if the pain is in both. And keep the cautery a little away from the root of the ear after marking the place with ink, and the cautery should be about ten points in each ear or thereabouts, then treat the places until it heals, God willing.
Chapter Six.
On cauterizing the paralysis.
The paralysis treated by cautery is that type which arises from phlegm, as I mentioned in the classification of diseases.
And avoid cauterizing the type which arises from dryness and spasm of the nerve.
When you have treated this type of paralysis with ointments, snuffs, and gargles and your treatment has been ineffective, then the patient should be cauterized with three cauterizations. One at the root of the ear, the second a little below his temple, and the third at the junction of the lips.
And make your cauterization on the opposite side from the diseased side, because the relaxation occurs on the side which appears healthy.
And the image of the cauterization is that you cauterize with a cauterization opposite the upper tip of the ear, a little below the horn of the head, and another in the temple, and its length should be the length of the thumb.
Lower your hand with the cautery until you burn about half the thickness of the skin.  This is the image of the cautery; it is a type of scalpel, the image of which has preceded, but it is a little gentler, as you see. The knife should be somewhat thicker than usual. Then treat the area as previously mentioned until it heals, God willing.
Chapter Seven.
Cauterization of chronic apoplexy.
If the apoplexy becomes chronic and you treat it with what we have mentioned, and your treatment is not effective, and the patient does not have a fever, cauterize him in four places. One cauterization on each temple, one in the middle of the head as previously mentioned, and one at the back of the head as previously described.
The application of the cautery is as previously described.  It may also be cauterized at the mouth of the stomach, which is more effective, then treat as previously described.
Chapter Eight.
On cauterization for forgetfulness.
If it is due to phlegm, the patient should first be given strong purgatives and brain-cleansing pills.
Then his whole head should be shaved and a mustard plaster, as described in the chapter on plasters, should be applied to the back of his head repeatedly; for this is a kind of cauterization.
Do this in the exact order I have described there. If he is cured by this, well and good; otherwise, cauterize him three times at the back of his head, the cauterizations being arranged from the top of the head to the nape of the neck.
Make the space between each cauterization the width of a finger. Then treat the cauterization as described above. If you wish to increase [the number of cauterizations] and the patient can tolerate it, cauterize the middle again.
If you wish to increase [the number of cauterizations], cauterize the temples, then treat it until it heals.  The cautery should be olive-shaped, as described above.
Chapter Nine.
On cauterizing paralysis and general body relaxation.
It is necessary that you first cleanse the head with purgatives and as we have mentioned. Then shave the patient's head, then cauterize: one cauterization in the middle of the head, one on each temple, and one on the back of the head.
And three on the vertebrae of the neck. If, in a case of general body relaxation, you need more than that, and the patient can tolerate it, and the disease is strong and persistent, then cauterize with four cauterizations on the vertebrae of his back. And reach with the cautery until you burn most of the skin, then lift your hand, then treat him as previously mentioned until he is healed, God willing.  The cautery should be olive-shaped.
Chapter Ten.
On cauterizing epilepsy.
Only the epileptic whose epilepsy is caused by phlegm is cauterized.  It is necessary to first cleanse his brain with strong purgatives and with all the other treatments that we mentioned in the classification, if the patient is an adult and able to tolerate taking medicine.
But if he is a child who cannot tolerate medicines, then use gargles and mouthwashes that cleanse the brain many days beforehand, along with improving his diet.
Then shave his head, then cauterize him with one cautery in the middle of the head as previously described, and another cautery at the back of the head. And on each temple one cautery. If the patient is strong and able to tolerate it, cauterize him with the cauteries mentioned for the patient with paralysis and body relaxation, on the vertebrae of the neck and the vertebrae of the back; and let the cautery be olive-shaped as previously described.
If the patient is a child, make the cautery gentle in this manner.
Chapter Eleven.
On cauterizing melancholia.
If the cause of melancholia is corrupt humors and thick phlegm, cauterize with the cauteries we mentioned for the patient with paralysis.
If the cause of melancholia is an excess inclined to black bile and the patient's body is moist, purge him with that which cleanses his brain, as was mentioned previously in the classification.
Then shave the patient's head, then make a tight cake of linen like a circle, then place it in the middle of his head, with the patient sitting cross-legged holding it from every side, then take one pound of old sheep fat.
Then heat it over the fire to a moderate heat, as much as the finger can withstand when inserted into it, then pour it into the middle of his head in the circle and leave it until it cools. Do this for the patient once every week along with all other good treatment until he is cured, God willing.
And if you wish, cauterize it with many small punctures without holding your hand to the cautery, but rather let it be a fumigation, for this type of cautery moistens the brain moderately.
Then apply to it cotton soaked in butter or chicken fat.
Chapter Twelve.
On cauterizing the watery discharge in the eye.
If the watery discharge in the eye becomes apparent to you with the signs which I mentioned in the classification, then act quickly, and purge the patient of whatever cleanses his head and protect him from all humors and sweat him in the bath on an empty stomach for several days.
Then shave his head and cauterize him in the middle of the head, then cauterize him twice on the temples if the onset of the watery discharge is in both eyes, or on one side if it is in one eye.
Then cut with the cautery all the veins and arteries which are under the skin, and let the cauterizations be lengthwise across the temples, and guard against the bleeding of blood.
If you see something of it, cut it off immediately with any treatment possible, and we will discuss the wisdom in cauterizing and cutting the arteries and guarding against bleeding.
And it may be cauterized in the nape of the neck under the two bones with two extensive cauterizations.
Chapter Thirteen.
Concerning the cauterization of chronic weeping sores.
If the weeping sores of the eye are chronic and persistent, and are from the veins and arteries on the surface of the head, outside the head, and you are certain that it is from a cold, thick, phlegmatic excess, then cauterize it with the cautery that I described for the beginning of the descending water. A cauterization in the middle of the head and two cauterizations on the temples and two cauterizations in the nape of the neck under the two bones. And if you need more, then cauterize one cauterization on each side of the tail of the eye at the edge of the eyebrow with a small cautery iron.
Chapter Fourteen.
Concerning the cauterization of the foul smell of the nose.
If you treat it with what we mentioned in the division and the treatment is not effective, then immediately purge the patient with colocynth for three nights, then shave his head and cauterize the middle with the olive-shaped cautery iron. Then cauterize with the nail-shaped cautery iron, two cauterizations above the eyebrows slightly below the hair, and be careful of the artery, do not cut it. And this is the image of the nail-shaped cautery iron.
Chapter Fifteen.
Concerning the cauterization of the relaxation of the eyelid.
If the eyelid relaxes from illness or moisture, cauterize the eyelid with one cauterization using this crescent-shaped cautery iron.
And if you wish, cauterize above the eyebrows slightly, two cauterizations on each side, and keep a distance from the temples, and let the length of each cauterization be the length of the eyebrow.
And do not exaggerate with the cauterization, but only to the extent that one-third of the skin is burned, and the shape of the cautery iron is as follows.
Chapter Sixteen.
On cauterizing the eyelid.
If its margins turn inward, so that the eye is pricked, then cauterization in it is of two types. Either cauterization with fire or cauterization with a burning medicine. As for cauterization with fire, you order the patient beforehand to leave his eyelashes alone, if he is one who plucks them, until they grow long and become even.
If he pricks it while they are growing, then tie his eyes with a bandage lest they move until they grow. Then, when they have grown and become even, place the patient's head in your lap, then mark on the eyelid with ink a sign in the shape of an acacia leaf.
The beginning of the mark should be near the margins. Then, place under the eyelid cotton soaked in egg white or in the saliva of a lizard. Then, heat a cautery iron, its form is this.
Then cauterize according to the shape you marked, little by little, in many times, until the surface of the skin, which is in the shape of the acacia leaf, is completely burned, especially its surface. A sign of the correctness of your work is that you see the eyelid has curled up and the hair has risen from the flesh of the eye.
Then lift your hand and leave it for three days. Then apply cotton with butter to it until the scab is removed, then treat it with ointment until it heals, God willing. If some of the hair returns after a time and the eyelid relaxes,
repeat the cauterization in that place as you did before. If the hair is in the lower eyelid, cauterize it until it returns to its natural position and does not prick the eye. As for cauterization with a burning medicine, it is that you order the patient to leave the margins until they grow long and even.
Then make from paper the shape of an acacia leaf. Then take from the usual soap and from unslaked lime, of each one a drachm weight or so, and crush them together well. Do this quickly lest it dry. Then spread some of it on the paper which you made in the shape of an acacia leaf, and place it on one or both eyelids. Place cotton soaked in egg white under the eye, and the patient's head in your lap.
Place your index finger on top of the medicine and hold it down slightly, while moving it whenever the patient feels the sting of the medicine, because he will feel a burning sensation like fire. As long as he feels the burning, leave the medicine and move it with your finger. When the burning ceases, remove the medicine and wash the eye with water.
And look: if you see the eyelid has risen as it rises when cauterized with fire or by cutting, then leave it. Otherwise, repeat the medicine on the place where the medicine did not take effect and did not turn black, until you complete your work and the eye is cauterized. Then place cotton with fat on it until the burning subsides.
Then treat it with palm-tree ointment or other until it heals.  It is necessary for you during the procedure to be extremely careful that nothing of the medicine falls into the eye. If the eyelid relaxes after some days, and something like a hair is suspected in the eye,
then repeat the medicine on that place of the eyelid which has relaxed, specifically as you did initially, then treat it until it heals. This is the image of the acacia leaf.
Know that people's eyes differ in size, so your cauterization should be according to that. The correct method is not hidden from one who is experienced in this craft.
Chapter Seventeen.
On cauterizing the fistula which appears in the canthus of the eye.
If you treat the fistula with what we mentioned in the divisions of diseases and your treatment is not effective, then it should be cauterized in this manner.
You should order the patient to place his head in your lap, and a servant should hold his head in front of you in such a way that it does not move or shake.
Then you place cotton soaked in egg white or in the saliva of a frog on his eyes, then you heat the cautery which is of this shape.
It should be hollow like the quill of an eagle's feather at one end, which is the cauterizing end; and if you wish, it can be perforated at the other end, and if you wish, it can be solid like an awl, although the hollow one is better for your work, God willing.
Then, squeeze the fistula if it is open and remove its contents and dry it; or if it is not open, open it and remove its pus. Then, place the cautery on it while it is very hot, holding it with your hand until it reaches the bone.
And move your hand slightly away during cauterization, from the eye towards the nose, lest your hand should slip or the patient should move, and the cautery fall into the conjunctiva and damage it.  If you reach the bone with the first cauterization, well and good; otherwise, repeat the cauterization a second time if necessary.
Leave it for three days, then apply cotton with butter and treat it with drying ointment until it heals. If forty days pass and it does not heal, then apply a strong, corrosive medicine until the bone is exposed, and treat it as will be mentioned in its chapter, God willing.
Another method of cauterizing a fistula, mentioned by some of the ancients:  You should approach the site of the fistula, incise it, and then place a thin cone in the incision itself; this is its image.
Pour into it the weight of a dirham of molten lead, and hold your hand on the cone firmly and steadily, and the patient must not move at all, lest the molten lead flow into his eye. It is advisable to place on the patient's eye cotton soaked in egg white or water.
For the lead burns the site of the fistula and heals it wonderfully. If the fistula heals with the cauterization and treatment we have mentioned, well and good; otherwise, it is necessary to use nasal perforation and return the fistula to the nasal passage, as will be detailed in its appropriate place.
Chapter Eighteen.
On cauterizing a cleft lip.
A cleft often occurs in the lip, called a harelip, especially in the lips of children; it occurs frequently. If you treat this cleft with what we mentioned in the section and the treatment is not effective, then heat a small, knife-shaped cautery in this manner.
Its cavity should be as fine as a knife. Then apply it, hot and quickly, to the cleft itself until the cauterization reaches the depth of the cleft; then treat it with the cautery until it heals, God willing.
Chapter Nineteen.
On cauterizing fistulas occurring in the mouth.
If a swelling appears at the root of the gums, or in the palate, or at the roots of the molars, then it suppurates and bursts, and a fistula results from the flow of pus, and you treat it but the treatment is not effective, then you should heat a cautery to the extent that it fits into the fistula.
Then insert it, hot, into the opening of the fistula, and hold your hand until the heated iron reaches its depth and end. Do this once or twice, then treat it afterwards with what we have mentioned in the treatment until it heals, God willing.
If the discharge ceases and it heals, well and good; otherwise, it is necessary to examine the area, and to remove the diseased bone, according to what will come in its chapter, God willing.
Chapter Twenty.
Concerning the cauterization of loose molars and gums.
If the gums are loose due to moisture and the molars are mobile, and you have treated them with medication without success, then place the patient's head in your lap, then heat the cautery iron, whose image comes after this, after you have placed the tube on the tooth.
And insert the hot cautery iron quickly, and hold your hand for a little while until the patient feels the heat of the fire has reached the root of the tooth, then lift your hand, then return the cautery iron repeatedly as you wish, then let the patient fill his mouth with salt water.
And let him hold it for an hour and then spit it out; for the mobile tooth will become firm, and the loose gum will tighten, and the putrid moisture will dry.
Chapter twenty-one.
On cauterizing toothache.
If the toothache is from cold, or if there are worms in it, and treatment with medication has not been effective, then cauterization is done in two ways: either cauterization with fat, or cauterization with fire.
As for cauterization with fat, you take cow’s fat and boil it in an iron spoon or in a shell, then you take cotton and wrap it around the end of a probe, then dip it in the boiled fat and quickly place it on the aching tooth and hold it until it cools.
Then repeat this several times until the strength of the heat reaches the root of the tooth.  If you wish, you can dip wool or cotton in cold fat and place it on the aching tooth and place a heated iron on top of it until the heat reaches the bottom of the tooth.
As for cauterization with fire, take a brass or iron tube, and let it be somewhat thick in its body, so that the heat of the fire does not reach the patient’s mouth.  Then heat the cautery iron which is depicted and place it on the tooth itself and hold your hand until the cautery iron cools.  Do this repeatedly.
Then the pain will go away, either that same day or the next day. After this cauterization, it is advisable that the patient fill his mouth with good fat and hold it for an hour, then spit it out. This is the image of the cautery iron.
Cauterize with whichever end you wish, and as much as is possible. This is the image of the tube.
Chapter Twenty-Two.
On cauterizing fistulas.
If fistulas are caused by phlegm and cold humors and do not respond to ripening with medication, and you desire rapid ripening, then heat a hollow cautery iron, which has this image, perforated at both ends.
So that smoke exits from the other end during cauterization, place it carefully on the fistula itself, once and twice if needed, until you reach the depth of the fistula. If the fistula is small, then make the cautery iron to the size of the fistula.
Then leave it for three days and apply cotton soaked in butter to it until what was burned by the fire is gone. Then treat it with ointment and tents until it heals, God willing.
Chapter Twenty-Three.
On cauterization for hoarseness and shortness of breath.
If humors overwhelm the trachea, especially if this is accompanied by a cold temperament, then the patient should first be purged with laxative medicines, then cauterize with a cautery iron in the pit of the neck at the root of the throat in the lower position.
Be careful not to reach the throat with the cautery and do not burn more than half of the skin.  Then cauterize again at the joint of the neck at the end of its last vertebra, and let the cautery be nail-shaped in the manner previously described.
Then treat him with what has been previously mentioned until he recovers, God willing.
Chapter Twenty-Four.
On cauterizing lung disease and cough.
If the cough and lung disease are due to cold humors, and the patient has no fever or consumption, and the disease is chronic, then cauterize two places above the clavicles in the low, soft, empty places, and another cautery in the middle of the chest between the breasts.
And the cautery should be a nail-shaped instrument, in the manner previously described.  And if you wish, let your cautery be by puncturing with the instrument called the "nuqta," the form of which has been previously shown in puncturing earache, and let the punctures be from thirty points or thereabouts.
Then treat him with what has been previously mentioned until he recovers.  You may also make a cautery with three branches in this form, and it hastens the cauterization because you cauterize three places at once with it. And this is the form of the cautery.
Chapter Twenty-Five.
On cauterizing the armpit.
If the head of the humerus is dislocated because of slippery humors, or if it does not remain in place when it is reduced from its dislocation, so that it becomes habitual to relapse and then dislocate at the slightest movement as we have witnessed, then it is necessary to reduce the joint first. Then the patient lies on his back or on his healthy side, then the skin inside the armpit is raised upwards with the fingers of your left hand if the joint has dislocated inwards.
Then heat the cautery iron with two heads, whose image is this.
Then cauterize the skin with it until it penetrates to the other side, and the shape of the cauterization will be four cauterizations.  It may be cauterized with a cautery iron with three heads; then the shape of the cauterization will be six cauterizations, and the heads should be fine.
And this is the image of the cautery iron with three heads.
And one may be added to this number, making the cauterizations eight. Then, apply to the cauterization balls pounded with salt, then keep the patient still and do not move the limb for a time until it strengthens. If the dislocation is upwards, which is rarely the case,
cauterize above the shoulder one good, nail-like cauterization, or many cauterizations in a dotted pattern, for the joint will strengthen, the moisture will leave, and the patient will heal.
Chapter Twenty-Six.
In cauterizing the stomach.
If coldness and excessive moisture occur in the stomach, to the extent that it is deranged and many catarrhs occur in it, and it is treated with various treatments without success, then the patient should lie on his back, extending his legs and arms, then three cauterizations should be performed. One cauterization below the pit of the stomach, the size of a finger, with a nail-like cautery, and two cauterizations below on either side of the first cauterization, so that the shape of the cauterizations is triangular, and further apart so that they do not meet if they suppurate; and the depth of the cauterization should be two-thirds the thickness of the skin, and this is the shape of the cauterizations.
In this manner and to this extent, no more.
And if you wish, cauterize it with one large cauterization in the middle of the stomach, in this manner.
And the cautery with which you cauterize this single cauterization should be circular in shape, in this manner.
And the stomach may be cauterized with dots for one who is afraid of this cauterization; and that is to mark dots on the stomach with ink to the extent that you desire, then cauterize it with a dot-like cautery, then treat it with the treatment that has been mentioned until it heals.
Chapter Twenty-Seven.
In the cold cauterization of the liver.
If there appears from the liver a pain due to coldness and dampness, or due to a subtle wind, so that it has departed from its natural temperament excessively, and the patient has been treated with what we mentioned in the division, and that did not benefit. Then it is necessary that the patient lie on his back, and three cauterizations should be marked with ink in this shape and this exact amount on the liver, below the ribs where the elbow of a person ends.
And after each cauterization, there should be a cauterization the thickness of a finger, and the cauterization should be straight along the body, and do not press down hard with the cautery iron.
And let the amount of thickness of skin that you burn be half, no more. And let the patient stand on his feet; and if he is not standing, then let him be lying down with his legs extended and his arms raised. And this is the image of the cautery iron.
And you may be able to cauterize these cauterizations with a knife-like cautery iron if you have gentleness and skill in the craft, and be careful lest you overdo the cauterization and burn all the skin, and burn the abdomen and reach the intestines, for the skin there is thin, so know this.
Chapter Twenty-Eight.
In cauterizing a swelling of the liver.
If an abscess appears in the liver and you want to know if that swelling is in the flesh of the liver or in its peritoneum, then if it is in the flesh of the liver, the patient will feel heaviness and pain without sharpness.
And if it is in the peritoneum of the liver, there will be intense sharpness with the pain, and you see that the physicians have been exhausted in treating it, then the patient should lie on his back, then mark the swollen area with ink.
Then heat the cautery in the fire; it is the cautery that resembles a awl, and this is its image. And cauterize it with one cauterization until you burn all the skin and the cauterization reaches the peritoneum until all the matter comes out, then treat it with the treatment of abscesses until it heals.
And this type of cauterization should not be used except by one whose training in medicine has been long and who has experienced these diseases many times by practice; then he may undertake such work, but leaving it is better in my opinion.
Chapter twenty-nine.
On cauterizing the fistula.
The ancients mentioned cauterization with the roots of rhubarb for a cold fistula in this way: that you take one long, dry root of rhubarb, the longest you can find, and it should be about the thickness of a finger, then dip it in oil and hold it in the fire. Then apply one cauterization between the connection of the clavicle and the neck, and two small cauterizations a little below the jugular veins, slightly inclined towards the side under the chin, and two large cauterizations above the breasts, between the third and fourth ribs.
And two cauterizations also between the fifth and sixth ribs, slightly inclined backwards, and another cauterization in the middle of the chest and another above the stomach, and three cauterizations on the back: one between the shoulders and two on either side of the spine, below the cauterization which is between the shoulders.
And you should not deepen your hand with the cautery, but it should be a superficial burning of the skin. Some of the ancients mentioned that some people used a hot iron instrument, like a file, heating it and inserting it between the ribs until it reaches the tumor itself.
And it removes the matter, as we mentioned in liver tumors.  In this method, cauterization is dangerous; either the patient dies immediately, or a fistula develops at the site which will not heal.
Chapter Thirty.
On cauterizing the spleen.
If you treat the spleen ailment with the treatments mentioned in the division and your treatment is not effective, then cauterization is done in three ways, all correct. One is to cauterize it three or four times in a row along the length of the spleen.
In the shape of the liver cauterizations whose shape we presented, and between each cauterization and the next is the width of a finger or a little more.  The quality of the cautery is the same as that which we mentioned in cauterizing the liver, and do not deepen your hand with the cautery. The patient's position is lying on his back.
The other method is in the elbow, and you raise the skin opposite the spleen where the patient's left elbow ends, and you raise the skin opposite the spleen where the patient's left elbow ends, and your raising of the skin is across the body so that the cauterizations fall along the length of the body.
Then you insert two cautery irons, very hot, until you pass them through the skin on the other side. Then you remove the cautery irons, and the cauterizations will be four. And if you wish, you cauterize with the other cautery iron with three points.
Then you treat the site of the cauterization after you leave it to drain pus for many days; this is more effective than all the previous treatments.
Chapter Thirty-One.
On cauterizing dropsy.
Cauterization is only beneficial in ascitic dropsy specifically. If you treat the patient with dropsy using the types of treatment we mentioned in the classification and your treatment is not effective, then you should cauterize him with four cauterizations around the navel and one cauterization on the stomach.
Another cauterization on the liver, one on the spleen, and two on his back between the vertebrae—one opposite his chest and another opposite his stomach. The depth of the cauterization should be approximately the thickness of the skin. Then you leave the cauterization open to drain pus for a long time.
And do not deprive the patient of treatment after cauterization with what is necessary to combine the two meanings for him, so that healing may be hastened. The shape of the cauterizing iron with which the back is cauterized is nail-shaped, as its form has preceded. And that with which the abdomen is cauterized is olive-shaped.
Chapter thirty-two.
On cauterizing the feet and legs.
If they swell in dropsy and fill with yellow water, then it is necessary to cauterize on the back of the foot in the hollow between the little finger and the ring finger.
And place your hand with the cautery iron and do not bend it, then raise your hand and do not return it at all, for the yellow water will exude, and the cautery iron is of this form.
Then cauterize on the legs, two cauterizations on each leg, and the cauterization should be with the sharp end of the cautery iron, and the cauterization should be along the length of the leg, one below the knee and another below that towards the middle of the leg, and on each thigh two cauterizations.
And leave the cauterization open without treatment for a long time, so that the water exudes from it, then treat it with all that we have mentioned.
Chapter Thirty-Three.
On cauterizing diarrhea.
If the diarrhea is from cold and dampness, so that it weakens the binding and digestive powers in the stomach and intestines, and this has been treated with various treatments and it has not healed, and you see that the patient can tolerate cauterization and is strong enough, then cauterize him with a large cauterization on his stomach. As was previously mentioned in cauterizing the stomach with the circular cautery iron, and four cauterizations around the navel with a gentle, nail-like cautery iron, and one or two large cauterizations on the cotton above the coccyx.
If you see that the dampness is abundant and the patient can tolerate it, then cauterize him on the pubis and on each flank, and perhaps add two small cauterizations on the stomach near the large one; for this is a successful treatment whose benefit is not missed, God willing.
Chapter thirty-four.
On cauterizing the hemorrhoids of the anus.
If there are chronic, numerous, or single hemorrhoids in the anus, and they are from thick, cold humors or corrupt dampness, and they have been treated with what we mentioned in the classification, but the treatment has not been effective, then cauterize the patient with three cauterizations on the lower part of the sacrum, slightly below the table, in a triangular pattern.
And a cauterization below the navel, about two fingers wide. And if you judge that his stomach is cold and his food is not digested, and you see his face swollen, then cauterize him on the stomach with a large cauterization as previously mentioned, and a cauterization on his liver. And another on his spleen with a nail-shaped cautery.  And leave the cautery open for a time, then treat him until he is healed, God willing.
Chapter Thirty-Five.
On the cauterization of warts.
After cutting it, if the wart is cut, heat the cautery which resembles a needle, then insert it, hot, into the same cut wart, and continue until the cautery reaches the mouth of the vein from which the blood flows. Do this once or twice.
If the warts are numerous, cauterize each one as we have described, then treat them with suitable ointments until they heal. And if you cauterize it with a large cautery on cotton, it will be more effective.
Chapter Thirty-Six.
On the cauterization of fistulas which are in the anus and its surroundings.
If the patient does not respond to the incision and the procedure we described in its place and shrinks from it, then perhaps he will heal by cauterization. If a fistula occurs and pus and foul moisture flow from it for a long time, then the first thing you should do is measure it with a thin probe.
Then know its depth with the probe, then heat the cautery which resembles a needle, then insert it, hot, into the fistula itself, along the course of the fistula and to the depth that the probe entered, and repeat the cauterization until all those corrupt bodies are burned, once or twice or three times, according to your need.
And be careful not to burn a nerve if there is one, or a large vein, and if the fistula leads to the body of the bladder or to the body of the intestine.
Therefore, be careful of all these places.  You only do this if the fistula is in fleshy tissue and you have determined that it is not penetrating. Then treat the place until it heals. If the place closes and the materials cease and it remains so for a time, then know that it has healed completely.
And if the materials do not cease from it, then know that it is penetrating, or that in its depth there is a corrupt bone, or something similar, which will be mentioned in its place.
Chapter Thirty-Seven.
On cauterizing the kidneys.
If pain occurs in the kidneys from cold or a strong wind, and the patient's sexual function is diminished because of that, then it is necessary to cauterize it on the loins, on the kidney itself, one cauterization on each kidney, with the nail-shaped cautery previously mentioned.  Perhaps you may cauterize a third time on the same area, so that there will be three cauterizations in a row, which will be more effective.
Chapter Thirty-Eight.
On cauterizing the bladder.
If weakness and relaxation occur in the bladder from cold and dampness, so that the patient cannot retain urine, then cauterize it with one cauterization below the navel, on the bladder where the pubic hair begins, and one cauterization to the right of the navel and another to the left.
The distance after cauterization on each side should be the width of a thumb joint.  Cauterize one or two times in the lower back if necessary; the cautery should be nail-shaped, as previously mentioned.
Chapter Thirty-Nine.
On cauterizing the womb.
If a substance from cold and dampness occurs in the womb, thereby preventing conception, corrupting its flow, and hindering its function, or if pain occurs in it during menstruation, then three cauterizations should be made around the navel, as was mentioned in cauterizing the bladder. One or two cauterizations on the cotton in the lower back; the cautery should be nail-shaped.
Chapter Forty.
On cauterizing a dislocated hip.
Erroneous, mucous humors may accumulate in the hip joint, causing it to dislocate.  The sign is that one leg is longer than the other when they are measured against each other, and a void is found at the site of the dislocation.  Therefore, a semi-circular cauterization should be performed on the affected area of the hip joint itself.
After you have drawn a circle around the joint with ink, so that the joint itself falls in the middle of the circle, use the cautery whose image is shown in the cauterization of the stomach. If you do not have this cautery, perform three cauterizations with the large olive-shaped cautery.
Make the cauterization as deep as the entire thickness of the skin, then treat it until it heals, God willing.
Chapter forty-one.
On cauterizing sciatica.
If pain occurs in the hip joint, and the cause is cold and dampness, and you have treated the patient as mentioned in the classification, and the treatment has not worked, and the condition has become chronic, then it is necessary to purge the patient of thick humors with fetid acacia or prickly pear fruit, or something similar.
Then, shave it and apply cautery in two ways: either cautery with burning medicines or cautery with fire. Cautery with fire can be done in many ways; one of them is to cauterize the hip joint itself with three triangular cauterizations in this manner.
Make them of a suitable depth, and the distance between each cauterization should be the width of a finger.  The cautery iron should be olive-shaped.  You may also cauterize one in the middle, at the head of the hip itself, making four cauterizations.  Or, if you wish, cauterize it in the circle previously mentioned in the cauterization for hip dislocation, with one cauterization.
So that the circle encloses the entire hip and burns through the entire thickness of the skin. This is the method of cauterization for sciatica if the pain is confined to the hip itself and does not extend to the thigh and leg.
If it extends to the thigh and leg, cauterize two places on the thigh where the patient indicates pain, and one cauterization above the malleolus, four finger-widths towards the lateral side slightly. The cautery iron should be knife-shaped, and the depth of the cauterization should be only the thickness of the skin.
If he indicates that the pain extends towards the toes, cauterize where he indicates with a pointed cautery iron, three or four times, or more if necessary. If he indicates pain below the knee towards the leg, cauterize there with one knife-shaped cauterization.
And be careful in all your cauterizations not to burn a nerve or a large artery, lest you cause the patient a bad or lasting injury.
And I have seen one and another who were cauterized above the malleolus, and the cauterization was excessive, so that the leg became gangrenous, until the gangrene reached the foot and perforated it entirely, and the whole leg was corrupted, and then diarrhea and death followed. If the pain is in both sides, cauterize both in the same way.
Some learned physicians have mentioned a cauterization of the hip of this kind:  A cup-like instrument is made of iron, its diameter half a span, and its mouth the thickness of a date pit, or slightly less, and within this cup another cup, and a third cup.
And the distance between each cup is the width of a thumb joint. The cups are open on both sides, and their height is about one or two finger joints. A handle of iron is firmly attached to the cups. This is its image.
Then it is heated in the fire until it glows red and throws off sparks, then it is placed on the hip joint, the patient lying on his sound side, and three circular cauterizations are performed at once. Then he is left for three days, and the wound is dressed with butter, and the wound is left open for some days.
Many, then treat it with ointment until it heals. The author of this book says: We rarely used this type of cauterization because of its ugliness and the horror of its appearance, and because few are found who can endure it, except that it is a good cauterization for those who endure it and whose condition is suitable for it.
As for cauterization with burning drugs, it is done by making two cup-like instruments similar to the rings through which the door hinges descend, from copper or iron.
Their rims are raised about two finger joints or so, and the distance between them is about the thickness of a finger. They are open at the bottom and open at the top, and are held together in this manner.
Then you place them on the hip joint, the patient lying on his sound side, and you firmly hold your hand, then you pour between the circles some sharp water, which has been warmed a little over the fire, and hold it for the duration of an hourglass. The patient must endure its stinging and burning, for he will feel a burning like fire.
Until the stinging subsides, then you raise your hand with the instrument and wipe the sharp water from the entire hip with fresh water, then you leave it for three days and apply butter to it until the blackness of the burn disappears, and you leave it for days so that pus may drain from it, for this is most effective.
Then you treat it with ointments until it heals. If the pain extends to the thigh or leg, you make for it a mold to hold the water, in the same way that you did for the hip.  This is the description of the sharp water, which I have already described in the chapter on the preparation of drugs.
Take of potash and of unslaked lime, one part of each; pound them and place them in a new pot which has been pierced at its bottom with one small hole, the size that a small probe can enter. Place another glazed pot under the bottom of the pot.
And pour on the potash and lime enough fresh water to cover them by a finger’s breadth, after you have pressed them well with your hand. Leave the pot until the sharp water descends into the glazed pot below. Then collect all that water and pour it onto more lime and potash, freshly prepared.
For then it will be extremely sharp, and serves in many medical procedures and in cauterizing various members, because it acts exactly like fire.  Among the drugs which also cauterize the hip are thapsia, honey of spurge, and lime with soap, well mixed.
Galen recounts, from an ancient man, a treatment for hip pain and sciatica, and greatly magnified its importance, claiming that no other treatment is needed and that it cures with a single application.
To the point that one might enter the bath carried and leave cured.  This is done by taking fresh green  celandine—if fresh is not available, take newly dried—and finely grinding it with a little fat, and applying it to the hip where the pain is, or to the leg, or thigh.
It is bound on and left for three hours, or as long as the patient feels the burning cease. Then he is put in the bath, and when his body is damp, he is put in the tub. The pain will go away and he will be cured, God willing. If he is not cured, repeat the application after ten days.
Then, God willing, he will be cured. Know that no one should use cautery with these drugs except after evacuating the body. Dioscorides mentioned that goat hair, if used to cauterize sciatica, is beneficial.
The cautery is done in this way: wool is taken and soaked in old oil, and placed on the deep place between the thumb of the hand and the forearm, closer to the forearm. Then take dry goat hair.
Heat it in the fire until it becomes a coal, then place it on the wool and leave it until it goes out. Then take another and continue doing this until sensation reaches, through the humerus, to the hip and the pain subsides, God willing. This type of cautery is called goat-hair cautery.
Chapter forty-two.
Concerning the cautery of backache.
Pain may occur in the back from many causes, either from a fall, a blow, excessive vomiting, and the like.  It may be from the effusion of a cold, moist substance. Cautery is only done in this type alone.
That which is from the effusion of a cold substance, it is necessary after the patient has vomited with hellebore and the like, that he be cauterized on his back where the pain is, three rows across the width of the table itself, after marking the place with ink. In each row, five cauterizations or more, according to what you see the patient can tolerate and his strength.
And the cautery is done with a needle cautery, and if you wish, cauterize with three or four medium-sized nail cauteries in this manner.
Chapter forty-three.
On cauterizing the beginning of a hump.
This illness often affects small children, and its initial sign in a child is that he experiences shortness of breath upon standing and movement, and you find at the end of his spinal vertebrae a bead that has protruded, standing out from the other beads.
If you see that and want to stop it, cauterize it with a circular cautery iron in this manner. Let the cautery take hold on each side of the vertebra evenly; and if you wish, cauterize it around the vertebra with a pointed cautery iron, in two or three rows, and let the points be close to one another.  Then treat the area until it heals, as we have described.
And be careful not to use cautery in a hump that is caused by nerve tension.
Chapter forty-four.
On cauterizing gout and joint pains.
If joint pains are due to cold humors that descend to any member of the body, and if the pains occur in the legs, physicians usually call this gout in particular.
If the gout, which is cold in origin, is treated with the types of treatment we mentioned in the classification, and the pains do not go away, then cauterization will remove them.  This is done by cauterizing around the joint of the legs with many cauterizations after evacuation. The cautery should be olive-shaped and medium-sized, in this manner.
And if you need to cauterize the surface of the leg, do so with a pointed cautery. If the pains ascend to the knees or to the other joints—and this often happens—cauterize each knee with three or four cauterizations on each side with the same olive-shaped cautery.
If you need more cauterization than this, cauterize, but do not deepen your cauterization, rather it should be only about the thickness of the skin.  If the pains ascend to the hips or to the back, use what we mentioned concerning cauterization in its chapter.
If the pains are only in the hands, cauterize around the forearms as if circling two rows. If the pains remain in the fingers, cauterize each knuckle with a point, and the metacarpus. If the pains ascend after some days to the elbows or shoulders,
cauterize them on each side.  The patient should not be without good management and the taking of medicines, for if he improves his diet and evacuates the phlegm, he will be cured with this cauterization, God willing.
Chapter Forty-five.
On the cauterization of hernias.
If a hernia appears in the groin and some of the intestine and fat descends to the testicle, and this is recent and beginning, then you should order the patient to abstain from food that day and to use laxatives to evacuate the bowels.
Then he should lie on his back in front of you, and you should tell him to restrain himself until the fat or intestine protrudes, then you should return it with your finger.  Then you should mark with ink below the hernia on the pubic bone a mark resembling a semicircle, its ends towards the upper part of the body.
Then you heat a cautery of this shape.
Until it becomes white and throws off sparks. Then you return the intestine or fat to the abdomen. Then an assistant places his hand on the place so that the intestine does not protrude.  And you have parted the patient's legs and placed a cushion under him, and another assistant ties his legs.
And another on his chest holds his hands. Then you lower the cautery onto the same mark, and your hand with the cautery is upright and straight, and you hold it until it reaches the bone, or repeat it again if it does not reach the bone the first time.
And you should guard against the protrusion of the intestine while cauterizing, lest you burn it, which would cause the patient either death or a great calamity. And know that if you do not reach the bone with the cautery, your work will not succeed. And the cautery should be gentle for children according to their size, and for adults according to their size.
Then you treat the cauterized area after three days with butter, until the scabs from the fire are gone. Then you treat it with other ointments until it heals. And let the patient lie on his back for forty days until the wound is closed.
And you should make his diet during his treatment such that it softens his bowels, lest the intestine protrude during straining and defecation. Then, if he wishes to get up after forty days, you use a tight bandage and keep it on for another forty days. And he should reduce exertion, fullness from food and drink, and loud shouting.
For if this procedure is used in this way, he will heal completely, God willing. And I will mention the treatment of hernias by incision in its chapter, God willing. As for the hernia that occurs in the rest of the abdomen and is incipient, if you wish to prevent it from increasing, cauterize a round area around it. Let what you burn of the skin be about two-thirds of it. Then treat it as we have mentioned, for it will not increase, God willing. And some of the ancients mentioned cauterizing the hernia with a triangular cautery in this manner.
After you mark the hernia with ink, making the horizontal line at the top of the hernia and the other line below, and you put down your hand in the middle with one nail-shaped cautery. And the first cauterization is easier and better, God willing.
Chapter forty-six.
On cauterizing fistulas.
If pain occurs in some member from a fall or a blow, and that pain lasts a long time, and treatment with medicines does not work, then it should be cauterized with a pointed cautery.  And the cauterization of each member should be according to its size, whether small or large, and its weakness or strength.
And if the pain and fistula persist, then if it heals from the first cauterization, that is well and good; otherwise, repeat the cauterization upon it, for it is the nature of these pains to move from one member to what is near it. Therefore, it should be followed by cauterization until the patient is healed.
Chapter forty-seven.
On cauterizing leprosy.
As for lepers, they may benefit greatly from cauterization, especially the type of leprosy that comes from putrefaction of phlegm and black bile.
If you wish to cauterize, observe whether the leprosy is incipient. If you have treated it with what we mentioned in the classification, and it has not lessened nor stopped, and you fear that corruption will take over the entire temperament of the patient, then cauterize the head with five cauterizations. One in the middle of the head as is customary, the second below it towards the forehead at the end of the hair, and two on the temples, and one at the back on the nape of the neck.  Deepen your cauterization slightly so that it affects the bone a little.
And scales will be removed from it, so that the thick vapors may easily escape from it. Cauterize also the spleen in the same manner as mentioned above. But if the leprosy has persisted in the patient and appeared manifestly, then it is necessary to cauterize with these cauterizations which we mentioned on the head. And a cauterization on the tip of the nose, two cauterizations on the cheeks, two cauterizations on the vertebrae of the neck, six on the vertebrae of the back, one large one on the coccyx at the base of the spine, and another above it in the same region, and two on the hips, one on each hip. And two on the knees, one on each knee, and two on the shoulders, and two on the elbows, and two on the breasts, and cauterize each joint of the fingers of his hands and feet with a cauterization, and each heel of his feet and forearms.
And guard against the nerve which is on the back of the heels lest you burn them.  A cauterization may also be applied to the pubic bone, another to the pit of the stomach, and another to the liver. And know that the more cauterizations you add, the more useful and effective it will be.
And know that the patient does not feel the pain of the cauterization as the healthy person does, because his body is numb.  And the cauteries should be large and small according to the limbs and joints, as described above for the types of cautery irons.
Then treat the cauterization with crushed barley flour mixed with honey and other treatments until it heals, God willing.
Chapter forty-eight.
Concerning the cauterization of numbness: When a limb becomes numb and is treated with medications, ointments, and bandages but does not heal, cauterize the numb limb with cauteries according to the size of the limb, whether large or small. Let your cauterization penetrate slightly into the thickness of the skin.
Then treat it with ointment until it heals.  And it may be that some numbness affecting the hand and foot is cauterized in the vertebrae of the back at the exit of the nerve that moves that limb, and the numbness will go away.
And let none undertake this except one who is knowledgeable about the anatomy of the limbs and the exits of the nerves that move the body.
Chapter forty-nine.
Concerning the cauterization of vitiligo.
If vitiligo is longstanding and no medical treatment is effective, cauterize it slightly, to the depth of the thickness of the skin, until the whiteness disappears and its color changes.
Then treat it with lentil flour, rose oil,  plantain leaves, and pigeon's blood or the blood of sparrows; one part of each. Mix them all together, apply to a cloth, and bind it to the place until it heals.
Chapter fifty.
Concerning the cauterization of cancer.
If the cancer is incipient and you wish to stop it, cauterize it with a circular cautery around it, as it extends. Some scholars have mentioned that a deep cauterization should be performed in its center, but I do not see that, because I expect it to ulcerate, and I have witnessed this many times.
Therefore, the correct method is to cauterize around it in a circle, as we said, or with many cauterizations.
Chapter fifty-one.
Concerning the cauterization of the fistula.
If a fistula occurs and is slow to mature, either because of the causative agent, or because of the patient's age if he is old and anemic, or because of time, and you wish to hasten the maturation of the fistula, cauterize around it with many small cauterizations, puncturing it.
Then leave it, for it will hasten maturation.  If you wish to stop it with cauterization, take the cautery whose image is this.
And insert it into the middle of the fistula until it penetrates the skin, and let the cauterization be from what is below, so that the flow of pus is easier, then treat it as is appropriate until it heals.
Chapter fifty-two.
On cauterizing the ulcer.
An ulcer is a corruption that spreads in the member and consumes it, just as fire consumes dry wood. If you see an ulcer in a place that can tolerate cauterization with fire, then take many small and large, nail-shaped cauteries according to what suits that place where the ulcer is.
Then cauterize it from every side until all the corruption is eradicated and nothing at all remains, then leave it for three days and apply to the cauterized areas powdered sulfur with oil until all the eschar and all the corruption is removed.
Then treat it with ointments that grow flesh. If you see after three days that the flesh grows healthy and without corruption, otherwise repeat the cauterization on what remains of the corrupt areas.  An ulcer may also be treated with a sharp medicine.
For it acts in place of fire, more quickly beneficial. And I have mentioned its treatment with sharp medicine in the section; take it from there when you need it.
Chapter fifty-three.
On cauterizing inverted and non-inverted nails.
This ailment frequently occurs on the soles of the feet; it is a rough thing that pains the person.  Cauterization is done in two ways: either cauterization with fire, or cauterization with a sharp liquid.
As for cauterization with fire, you heat a hollow cautery iron, similar to an eagle's feather, made of iron to the extent that it surrounds the nail on all sides, and it should be thin-edged. Then you lower it, hot, onto the nail, and then you rotate your hand with the cautery iron around the nail until the cautery iron reaches the depth of the nail.
And you leave it for three days, it will suppurate, then you dress it with pounded wild bread (خبازى البرّيّة) mixed with salt, and you leave the dressing on it overnight. Then it will be removed from its roots.  Then you treat the wound site with a flesh-growing ointment until it heals, if the nails are not inverted.
This frequently occurs on the surface of the body, especially in the hands and feet.  Therefore you should take a tube of copper, iron, or eagle's feather, and lower it onto the nail or wart, then pour a little of the sharp liquid into the tube.
And hold your hand as you rotate it, with a slight twitch of your hand, so that the edge of the tube affects the root of the nail, and the sharp liquid finds a way to penetrate to the root of the nail. The patient should endure the stinging of the sharp liquid for a short while.
Then leave it; the nail will be removed from its roots.  Do this one by one until you have dealt with all of them on the body. Then treat the areas after they are removed with ointments that grow flesh.
Chapter fifty-four.
On cauterizing scabies.
If someone develops scabies from a cold in the nerve or from a quartan fever or something else, then you should cauterize it four or five times on the vertebrae of the back, with one cauterization between each vertebra, and one cauterization on his chest. And one cauterization on his stomach with an olive-shaped cautery iron, for scabies will subside and the cold disease will quickly mature.
Chapter fifty-five.
On cauterizing pustules that occur on the body.
Ugly pustules may erupt on the body, arising from cold, thick, corrupt substances.  At their first appearance, each pustule should be gently cauterized with a slender cautery iron, the end of which has been heated in the fire, or with a long cautery iron shaped like a reed, or with a lentil-shaped cautery iron.
And boils may be cauterized at their first eruption in this manner, so that the active cause does not increase and the harmful substance dissipates and the patient recovers. However, this should be done after the patient has been bled.
Chapter fifty-six.
On cauterizing hemorrhage that occurs when an artery is severed.
Hemorrhage often occurs from a severed artery during a wound inflicted from the outside, or during the incision of a tumor, or the cauterization of a member, and so forth, and its arrest is difficult.
If this happens to someone, quickly place your index finger on the mouth of the artery and press firmly so that the blood is contained beneath your finger and nothing emerges. Then place several small and large olive-sized cauteries in the fire and heat them until they are extremely hot.
Then take one, either small or large, depending on the wound and the location where the artery is severed.  Quickly remove your finger and apply the cautery to the same vessel and hold it until the bleeding stops.
If, upon removing your finger from the mouth of the artery, the bleeding resumes and the cautery goes out, quickly take another cautery from those prepared in the fire, and continue doing this, one after another, until the bleeding stops.
Be careful not to burn a nerve that is there, lest another affliction befall the patient.  Know that if an artery bleeds, it cannot be stopped, especially if it is a large artery, except in one of four ways: either by cauterization, as we have said; or by amputation if it has not already been severed; for if it is severed, its ends contract and the bleeding stops; or by tightly tying it with threads; or by applying medications that stop bleeding and tightening with bandages firmly.
And as for one who attempts to stop it with a ligature, or by tightening with cloths, or by applying burning substances, and the like, it will not be stopped in this way at all, except rarely. If this happens to someone and a physician or medicine is not at hand, he should immediately place his index finger on the mouth of the wound itself, as we have described.
And he should tighten it very hard until the blood is constricted and flows from above the wound and onto the artery. The finger should not be removed from it, constantly applying very cold water until the blood clots, thickens, and stops.  In the meantime, look for whatever is needed in the way of cautery or medicine.
Chapter Two.
Concerning incisions, punctures, bloodletting, wounds, and the like.
Khalaf said: In the first chapter we mentioned every ailment for which cauterization with fire and burning medicine is suitable, and its causes, reasons, instruments, and types of cautery instruments, arranging that from head to foot in sections.
And I follow in this chapter the same method, so that the student may easily obtain what he seeks.
Before I begin with that, you should know, my sons, that this chapter contains more subtleties than the first chapter on cauterization.
And because of that, caution should be greater, for in this chapter, the practice often involves the loss of blood which sustains life, when opening a vein or making an incision on a tumor or lancing an abscess, or treating a wound, or removing an arrow, or incising a stone, and the like, all of which are accompanied by danger and fear, and in most of them, death occurs.
And I advise you against undertaking what is doubtful, for in this art, various kinds of people with various kinds of illnesses may come to you; among them are those who have grown weary of their illness and have come to disregard death.
