Because of the severity of their illness and the length of their suffering, and the illness has reached a stage which indicates death; and there are some who will give you their money and enrich you with it in hope of health, and their illness is mortal; therefore, you should absolutely not cooperate with anyone who is in this condition.
Let your caution be greater than your desire and eagerness, and do not undertake anything of this kind unless you have certain knowledge of what will lead to a favorable outcome, and in all the treatment of your patients, use foresight and warning of what will lead to safety.
For in that you will have help in acquiring praise, glory, mention and thanks. May God inspire you, my sons, with His guidance and may He not deprive you of correctness and success; surely that is in His hand, there is no god but He.
And I have arranged the chapters of this section in the same manner as in the section on cauterization, from head to foot, so that what you want from it may be easier for you, God willing.
Chapter One.
In the treatment of the water which gathers in the heads of infants.
This ailment often occurs to infants at birth if the midwife presses the infant's head without gentleness, and it may also occur from a hidden cause which is not known.
And I have not seen this ailment in anyone except infants, and all those whom I have seen afflicted by it have quickly died; therefore I have considered abandoning treatment for it. And I have seen an infant whose head was filled with water, and the head grew larger each day. Until the infant could not sit up on his own because of the size of his head, and the moisture increased until he perished.
And this moisture either gathers between the skin and the bone, or it gathers under the bone on the membrane.  The treatment, if the moisture is between the skin and the bone and the swelling is small, is that one should make a single horizontal incision in the middle of the head, the length of the incision being about two finger-breadths, so that the moisture flows out. This is a picture of the lancet.
If the moisture is greater and the swelling is larger, then make two intersecting incisions in this manner:
And if the moisture is under the bone—and its sign is that you see the sutures of the head open on every side and the water subsides if you press it with your hand inwards, and this is not hidden from you—then one should make three incisions in the middle of the head in this manner.
After the incision, all the moisture comes out, then you bind the incisions with rags and bandages, then you bathe it from above with syrup and oil until the fifth day. Then you untie the bandage and treat the wound with lint and ointments. Do not neglect to bind the head moderately, and feed the patient with any dry food that contains little moisture.
Until the member strengthens and heals. Another description of the incision is that you look where the bone of the swelling and the gathering of the water appear, for it may be more in the back of the head, or in its front, or on the right, or on the left.
Then aim the incision where the swelling and the fullness of the water appear to you, and incise it as you are able, and be careful not to cut an artery, lest bleeding occur and the patient die from that bleeding along with the evacuation of the moisture.
Chapter Two.
Cutting the two arteries behind the ears, known as the mastoid arteries.
When someone is afflicted with severe colds in the eyes or chest, and this has persisted and medication has not been effective, then the most effective treatment is to cut these two arteries.
Then, if you wish to cut them, you should shave the patient's head with a razor, then rub the area with a rough cloth so that the artery appears, then tie the patient's neck with the edge of his garment, then look where the pulse beats and their location, the two hidden places behind the ears, and they are rarely hidden except in some people.
Then mark them with ink, then cut them with a sharp scalpel, cutting to the bone. This should be done across the head; and if you wish, you may insert the scalpel from under the artery and cut upwards. The length of the cut should be about two fingers' breadth.
If the artery is cut, the blood will spurt forth in a pulsating flow, leaping forward repeatedly. If the artery is not palpable, then you should measure three fingerbreadths from the ear, then mark it with ink and incise to the bone.  What should be...
Let six ounces of blood flow out on average; and perhaps more or less will flow, all depending on the strength of the patient and the fullness of his arteries. Then look at the wound; if there remains any peritoneum on the bone, cut it away.
Lest a hot inflammation occur. Then bind the wound with a linen thread, then treat it with ointments until it heals.
Chapter Three.
In ligating the two arteries in the temples.
If a person suffers from chronic migraine or acute attacks caused by sharp humors and heat in the muscles of the temples, or severe chronic headache, and so on, and he has been treated with various medical treatments without success, then we have tried in these diseases ligating the arteries of the temples or cauterizing them as we described.
The method of ligating them is to shave the hair from the temples of the patient and to locate the artery visible in the temple, for it will be apparent to you from its pulsation, and it rarely hides except in a few people.
Or, in severe cold, if it is hidden from you, let the patient tighten his neck with the excess of his garment, then you rub the area with a cloth or compress the area with hot water until the artery appears clearly and obviously. Then take the scalpel, whose image is this. Then you incise the skin gently with it until you reach the artery, then you place a hook in it and pull it upwards until you bring it out of the skin and free it from the membranes beneath it on all sides; if the artery is thin, then twist it with the end of the hook. Then cut off a portion of it to the extent that its ends are separated and contract, and do not cause bleeding; for if it is not severed and cut, the blood will not flow at all; then it is evacuated from blood from six ounces to three.
If the artery is large, then it is necessary to tie it in two places with a strong doubled thread; and let the thread be either of silk or of lute strings, lest corruption quickly reach it before the wound heals and bleeding occurs.
Then cut what is between the ligatures; do this at that time or after another time; and if you wish to cauterize it with a cautery to the bone with a knife-cautery until its ends are severed, this will serve the same purpose or better, as we said.
Unless the patient has a fever or a hot temperament, for cauterization is something that helps in eliminating humors and is thus surer in benefit.  After ligating the arteries, the area should be packed with old cotton and firm bandages applied; after healing, treatment with dry medications that generate flesh and with sutures should continue until it is healed, God willing.
If bleeding from the artery occurs during your work, quickly cut it, either by cautery, or by filling the area with vitriol and pressing firmly until the bleeding stops. If you do not have any of that at hand, place your finger there until the blood clots.
Then wash the area with very cold water until the sharpness subsides and bind it as necessary. What is easier and simpler than ligating the artery is to cauterize the vein with this double-bladed cautery after marking the two areas with ink.
Apply it very hot until it reaches the bone and the vein is severed in two places so that the ends are separated, for it will not reunite with this cautery at all.  This is an image of the cautery.
The blades should be sharp, resembling small scalpels, except that they should be much less sharp than a scalpel; for if they are as sharp as a scalpel, they will cool down more quickly and the flesh will not be severed quickly. If there is some thickness to them, the heat of the fire will remain in them.
And the flesh is cut quickly. This procedure is better than any other, and is lighter and easier. Let the distance between the blades be the width of a finger.
Chapter Four.
In the treatment of the constant flow of hot tears into the eyes.
If the tears are constant and their flow is from the veins on the cranium from the outside, and nothing from medical treatment with drugs has benefited, and you see the patient's face is red. And he feels in his forehead a crawling like the crawling of ants, and his eyes are sunken and moist, their lids are eroded and their eyelids are drawn together from the intensity of the tears.  Therefore, the ancients in treating it resorted to this procedure, and they called it treatment with the scalpel. It is that you order the patient to shave the hair from his forehead.
Then you make three parallel incisions in the forehead along the length of the forehead. The length of each incision is about two fingers, one incision parallel to the length of the nose in the middle of the forehead, the second a little distance from the movement of the muscle in the temple, and the third on the other side.
And be careful not to cut the two arteries on the sides, and keep your hand away from the junction of the jaws.  The distance between each incision should be about three fingers, held together. And have with you prepared pieces of sponge or many dry cloths to wipe the blood.
Then insert the double-edged scalpel, whose image is this. From the incision next to the temple to the middle incision, and with it, you dissect all the skin between the two incisions along with the periosteum on the bone. Then you also do that from the middle incision to the third incision. Then remove this scalpel. And also insert into the first incision another instrument called a scalpel, sharp on one side and smooth, not sharp, on the other; and this is its image:
And place its sharp side upwards towards the flesh attached to the skin, and its smooth side towards the bone, and push it until it reaches the middle incision, and cut with it all the vessels that descend from the head to the eyes without the cut reaching the surface of the skin.
Then do that in the middle incision to the other incision.  After a moderate amount of blood flows, squeeze the places of the clotted blood. Then place in each incision a cotton wick, and put on it a dressing moistened with syrup and oil, or vinegar and oil.
So that a hot swelling does not occur. On the third day, remove the bandage and use much cleansing with lukewarm water, then treat it with basilicon ointment with rose oil and whatever else you treat wounds with until it heals, God willing.
Chapter Five.
In the treatment of tears and discharges to the eyes from the interior of the head.
When someone experiences frequent, sharp, severe, constant discharges, and you see his eyes weak, small, his vision weakened, his eyelids ulcerated, and the eyelashes falling out. And there is severe, painful pain in the depth of the head and continuous sneezing. Know from these symptoms that those materials and discharges come from many deep veins. The best treatment is this: order the patient to have his forehead shaved, then make one incision in the middle of the forehead.
And there is severe, painful pain in the depth of the head and continuous sneezing. Know from these symptoms that those materials and discharges come from many deep veins. The best treatment is this: order the patient to have his forehead shaved, then make one incision in the middle of the forehead. Or slightly higher across, and begin the incision from the left temple to the right temple, making the incision to the bone, and keep your hand away from the temporal muscles that move when chewing, until the bone is exposed and all the blood is dried with a sponge.
Then separate the edges of the incision with old cotton or linen threads, and then bind it from above with bandages, and soak the bandages with syrup and oil, lest a hot swelling occur. And when you remove them and see that the hot swelling has subsided, then you should scrape the bone until the growth of flesh begins.
Then treat it with a drying preparation that grows flesh, such as taking two parts of wheat flour and four parts of colophony, and preparing an ointment from them, and using it in the growth of flesh in such wounds.
Chapter Six.
In the treatment of what falls into the ear.
Everything that falls into the ear is one of four types: either a metallic stone or a semi-stone like iron and glass, or a plant seed like chickpeas and kernels and the like, or a flowing substance like water and vinegar and the like.
Or an animal.  If a stone or a kind of stone falls into the ear that does not grow in the ear, then expose the ear to the sun.  If you see the stone, then instill some violet oil or cerate into it, then try to remove it by moving the head or sneezing with a handkerchief. And close the nostrils when sneezing comes, after placing a ring of rags or wool around the ear and extending the ear upwards. Often it comes out with this treatment. If it does not come out otherwise, then try to remove it with the gentle scoop, whose image is this.
If it comes out with the scoop, otherwise try to remove it with a gentle, slightly curved, blind probe. If it does not come out with that, then make a tube of copper and insert the end of the tube into the ear hole gently.
And seal around the tube with pitch softened with oil, lest there be a path for the air other than the tube, then draw it with your breath strongly, and often it comes out with that. If it does not come out with what we described, then take a little of the Nabataean mastic or of the prepared mastic which is used by birds, and place it on the end of the probe.
After wrapping it tightly with cotton, then insert it into the ear hole gently after drying the ear from moisture. If it does not come out by all that we have described, then proceed to incision before the hot swelling or tetanus occurs.
The description of the incision is that you first bleed the patient in the cephalic vein and let out blood according to his strength. Then you seat the patient before you, and turn his ear upwards, and make a small incision at the base of the ear near its lobe in the lowest part of it.
And the incision should be crescent-shaped until you reach the stone, then remove it with whatever instruments you can, then quickly stitch the incision at once and treat it until it heals.
And if the thing that has fallen into the ear is one of the seeds that grow and swell, then try to remove it as we have mentioned. If it does not respond to removal, then take a thin, delicate scalpel of this shape.
And try with it to cut that type of seed that has fallen into the ear.  You only do this if you are certain that the seed has become moistened by the vapor of the ear until it becomes many small pieces, then remove them with a blind probe or with a fine sponge or by suction.
As we mentioned, then it is easy to remove them. As for water entering the ear, the patient should first use sneezing with hellebore, having filled his ear with some worn cotton while lying on the ear that contains the water. If it comes out with that,
otherwise, take many small, smooth, flat stones the length of the fingers, warm them slightly by fire, and the patient inserts one into the opening of his ear and hops on one foot on that side and strikes the stone in the ear with another stone.
He continues to do this with one stone after another until all the water comes out. The water may also come out by taking a piece of papyrus or a feather, inserting one end into the ear, and applying the other end to fire until most of it burns, then repeating with another feather.
Do this repeatedly until all the water comes out or draw it out with a tube as previously described for the stone. As for removing an animal that has entered, see if it is small, like a flea or similar. Treat it as mentioned in the section, and as for...
If its body is large and perceptible to the touch, try removing it with forceps and hooks. This is easier than anything else that lodges in the ear. As for removing worms that develop in the ear, if you treat it with what we mentioned in the section on instillations and your treatment is not effective,
you should look into the ear in the sun. If you see something of the worms, remove them with forceps or fine hooks. If you do not see anything, take a tube of this shape.
narrow at the bottom and wide at the top, and insert the thin end into the ear as far as the patient can tolerate. Then suck strongly. Do this repeatedly until all the worms come out. If it does not come out, seal around the tube with wax as I mentioned to you for the stone.
If it does not come out with what we have mentioned, use the instillations that I have mentioned. The ancients have tried them in killing worms, and you will find that in the section on instillations.  Pouring oils and medicines into the ear is done with this instrument, and this is its image:
As you see, make it from silver or copper, narrow at the bottom with a small hole, and wide at the top. If you wish, the nozzle inside the tube can be made of tightly fitting copper. If you wish, take a reed and wrap cotton tightly around one end.
Then pour the oil or juice or whatever medicine you want into the tube while it is in the ear. Then insert the reed with the cotton from above and squeeze it with your hand moderately until the oil is driven into the ear cavity and it is felt.
by the patient inside. Let what you pour into the ear be warmed slightly in the fire. Be careful that what is dropped into it is neither very cold nor very hot, for the ear cannot tolerate that.
Chapter Seven.
In the treatment of the obstruction affecting the ear.
Some children may be born with their ear canals not perforated.
It may also happen that some people experience an obstruction in the ear canal due to a wound or a growth. This obstruction may be deep within the ear canal, invisible to the eye.
It may also present slightly outside the opening and be visible. That which presents deep within the ear and is not visible is, in most cases, difficult to see.  That which is visible, however, should have the patient's ear placed in the sun. And examine it; if you see the obstruction clearly, open it with a delicate scalpel, which is of this form:
Its tip should be slightly broad, and the rest of the scalpel smooth on both sides so as not to injure the ear. If the obstruction is due to flesh that has grown in the ear canal, grasp it with a delicate hook and cut it gently but firmly until all the flesh is removed.
If the obstruction is deep within the ear, take a smooth, slender probe, warm it slightly in the fire, and insert it into the ear canal. If you feel the obstruction, and the patient feels heaviness in hearing, remove it gently.
Take care not to injure the membrane of the ear. Then insert into the ear a tent corresponding to the size of the opening, having smeared it with Egyptian ointment. Do this for several days until you are sure the wound has healed. Or, take a tent, moisten it with water, and sprinkle powdered vitriol on it.
Use this. If you see that the ear has swollen with a hot inflammation, it is necessary to remove the tent and replace it with another tent smeared with a poultice made with rose oil, so that the hot inflammation subsides. Then treat it until it heals.
If bleeding occurs, dip a sponge or cloth in cold water and apply it to the ear. Use the other treatments for stopping bleeding mentioned in the chapter on haemostatics.
Chapter Eight.
On the treatment of warts which appear on the eyelids.
These warts which appear on the eyelids may be moist or dry.  It is necessary to grasp them with forceps or a hook and cut them from their roots with a scalpel, then apply powdered verdigris to the area.
If they bleed, cauterize them with a mild lentil-shaped cautery. Cauterization is better after cutting. They often return if any of their roots remain; if cauterized with fire, these roots are burned and do not return.
Chapter Nine.
On the treatment of the cold which affects the eyelids.
Often something resembling a cold in its intensity and hardness appears in the eyelids.
Therefore it is called a cold, and it is a gathering of thick moisture in the upper and lower eyelids. The treatment is to look: if the cold is prominent on the surface of the eyelid and moves in every direction, then the matter is easy, so make a transverse incision on it.
Then carefully peel it from every side until it is released, then hang it with a hook and cut it. If you cannot cut it except after the eyelid has been penetrated by the cut, that will not harm the patient. If the incision is large, then bring it together with sutures and treat it until it heals.
And if it is small, then there is no harm from it, for ointment will heal and join it. If the cold is inclined towards the inside of the eyelid, towards the inner surface, then turn back the eyelid and hang the cold with a hook without needing an incision, and excise it from every side.
If it penetrates the eyelid with the cut, that will not harm anything. Then wash the eye after cutting the cold with salt water and treat the area with something that joins until the patient heals.
Chapter Ten.
In the treatment of the pterygium which appears in the eyelid.
Pterygium is a fleshy growth in the layers of the upper eyelid, and this mostly happens to children. It weighs down their eyes, causing them to suffer from colds at night, and they cannot look at sunlight. Because the tears flow quickly to them.
Therefore, you see them always leaning on their faces and sleeping on them, and their eyelids under the eyebrows are moist, with a swelling upon them, and the pterygium is clearly visible. When you press the area with your fingers, you feel the pterygium between them. The procedure is to have the patient place his head in your lap.
Then you take a linen cloth and prepare a wick from it, and make a circle from that wick to the extent that it surrounds the pterygium on every side, then you place it on it and press with your fingers from every side to gather the pterygium in the middle of the circle.
Then you make a transverse incision in the middle of that moisture with a lancet, and the incision should not be larger than the incision made in bloodletting.  As for the depth, you should cut all the skin until you reach the pterygium.
Then, in most cases, the pterygium protrudes from the incision in the form of a white fatty piece. You pull it out with a cloth wrapped between your fingers, while you turn your hand right and left until it is cured. Beware of increasing the incision, lest the lancet reach the eye and harm it.
If the pterygium does not appear to you in the first incision, you should gently increase the incision a little until the pterygium protrudes, then pull it as we said. Then, dip a cloth in vinegar and water and place it on the area and bandage it. Some people crush salt.
And they place it inside the incision to dissolve what remains of that moisture, then treat it until it heals. If a hot swelling occurs in the area, treat it with soothing compresses until it heals.
Chapter Eleven.
On the types of eyelid surgery.
If extra eyelids grow on the eyelid, contrary to the natural course, under the natural eyelids and eyelashes, then they harm the eye and cause various diseases, such as constant tearing, eyelid relaxation, whiteness, and thickening, until it becomes a cause of blindness.
Eyelid surgery is performed in four ways: either by cauterization with fire, or with a strong medicine as previously described in the chapter on cauterization, or by cutting and suturing, or with a reed, as I will describe.
You should place the patient's head in your lap, then turn the eyelid with your left hand. If it does not turn, then insert a needle with a thread from under the eyelid, and pass the needle with the thread from above, near the hair itself, and pull the thread upwards with the eyelid.
Turn it with a probe, then incise the inside of the eyelid below the excess hair with a sharp scalpel, from the larger canthus to the smaller canthus, then remove the thread and place a small pledget of cotton or cloth under the eyelid, then mark the eyelid with ink.
Like the shape of a laurel leaf, except that the shape should be according to how much you want to raise the eyelid, because this varies among people; some need you to cut a suitable amount from the eyelid according to how much the eyelid has relaxed.
And some need less cutting, all of this according to the relaxation of the eyelid. Then incise with the scalpel along the two lines that you have marked, beginning from the larger canthus to the smaller canthus, and each incision should be near the natural hair, about the thickness of a probe.
Then insert the hook into one corner of the skin, then peel it all off, then bring together the edges with sutures using a fine woolen needle and thread, wipe away the blood, and stick the remaining threads to the eyebrows with some adhesive substance.
If you wish to do that, otherwise it does not matter. Then leave the sutures and threads for about three or four days, then treat it.  If you wish, you can leave the wound without suturing and treat it with something that dries and contracts, for the eyelid will rise when the wound closes and heals; however, suturing is better.
This method of blepharoplasty was mentioned by the ancients, although it is burdensome for the patient; it is, however, a good procedure and without danger.  Another method of blepharoplasty is also to mark on the eyelid a shape like the shape of a laurel leaf, as we described. Then raise the eyelid with three hooks, either separate or grouped in this manner:.
Then cut off the excess eyelid with small scissors, evenly, in this manner:
If you cannot hold the hooks and they do not straighten out for you, then take a needle with thread and insert it into the middle of the shape, and insert another thread near the larger canthus and a third thread near the smaller canthus.  Then gather the threads evenly between your fingers and raise them with a moderate lift.
Raise all of the marked skin as we described, then join the edges of the wound with sutures and treat it until it heals. If a hot inflammation occurs at the time of your cutting or incision, then calm that inflammation with  kyrioti and similar soothing ointments.
It may also happen that the lower eyelid inverts its edges; then you should use what we mentioned of cutting, suturing, cauterization with fire, and with a caustic medicine. Cauterization with fire or with a caustic medicine is more effective and gentler on the patient than cutting and suturing; and cutting and suturing in both [upper and lower eyelids] is what you should do.
Cauterization with a reed is done in this manner: you invert the eyelid and make the incision we described on the inside; then make two thin reeds or pieces of wood, their length equal to the length of the eyelid and their width less than the width of a scalpel.
And you have trimmed their ends on both sides where the threads are fastened. Then gather them gently over the excess of the eyelid, and firmly tie the two reeds on both sides.  Leave it for several days; then the stretched skin will die, turn black, and fester.
Until it falls off by itself. If it is slow, cut it off with scissors, then treat it until it heals. When it has healed, the eyelid will be raised and the edges of the eyelid will not be inverted. This is the image of the two reeds which you make in this shape and this exact size in length and width:
Chapter Twelve.
On raising an ingrown eyelash from the eye with a needle.
If it is one or two hairs, the procedure is to take a fine needle, thread it with a fine, smooth silk thread, then gather its ends and tie them in a very gentle knot, the length of the thread being about a span.
Then attach another fine thread to the needle, shorter than the first, and tie its ends if you wish. Then let the patient put his head in your lap, and let it be near the sun so that the work may be clearly seen, for it is delicate work. Then insert the needle with the thread into the root of the ingrown hair.
And quickly pass it through the eyelid until you bring it out above the natural hair, then pull the thread upwards by the needle and draw into the needle that hair, whether it is one, two, or three, no more.
Then pull it with your hand until the hair comes out with the needle in the eyelid along with the natural hair. If you pull the needle and the hair does not come out with it, pull the needle downwards by the thread which you attached to it until the needle comes out from the upper opening.
Then return the hair into it and pull them both. Do this until it rises to the upper opening and you see it clearly. Then remove the needle and thread and take them out, and bandage the eye and leave it bandaged for two or three days until the hair settles in its place and flesh grows over it.
If the hair is short, either add a long hair from the natural hair to it so that it rises with it, or leave it until it grows longer, then raise it.
Chapter Thirteen.
In the treatment of the stye which occurs in the upper eyelid of the eye.
That which this stye is exposed to is called a harelip, and this stye is either natural or accidental. The accidental one is from the healing of a wound or a cut or cauterization, and so on.
And the method of treatment is to incise that adhesion and separate its lips and place between them a linen wick and bind it until it heals.  And it is not advisable to use in its treatment things which dry and constrict.
For if you do that, the stye will return more severely than it was. Rather, use in it things which relax, such as fenugreek and lint with water in which marshmallow and flaxseed have been boiled, and diachylon ointment melted with something of the fats, and smear it on the wick and treat it with that.
And the key to its treatment is that you strive by every means that it not adhere in the way it was at first. And this treatment which we have mentioned is only some improvement of the stye, not that the appearance of the place returns to exactly as it was at all.
Chapter Fourteen.
On the treatment of the stye which occurs in the lower eyelid.
This stye which forms in the lower eyelid is that which is truly called a stye, and it is natural and it is accidental.  The accidental one is from a wound or incision or cauterization and the like.
And the method of working in it is that you take a needle with doubled thread and pierce it into the flesh and pass it from the left canthus to the right canthus until the thread is at the edges of the flesh, then you stretch the flesh upwards with the needle and cut it with a wide scalpel.
If the shape of the eyelid returns to what is appropriate, otherwise you take a probe and place it at the site of the incision and turn the eyelid with it and make two incisions on the inner side of the eyelid, and the ends of the incisions are from the corners of the cut which you cut until they meet, and from them is formed an angle. Until, when they meet, its shape becomes similar to this shape:
And it is the Greek letter lambda, then you remove that flesh to the extent that its sharp side is lower than what is next to the eyelid, then you gather the scattered parts with two stitches, sewing it with a wool thread, then you treat it with what we mentioned of relaxing and astringent medicines until it heals.
And if the fistula has resulted from an incision, suture, or cauterization, it is necessary to make a simple incision without the hair of the labia as well, as previously mentioned, then separate the lips with a probe as we mentioned.
In short, in the treatment of fistula, whether from above or below, you should act according to what is apparent to you of the form of the fistula, for it may differ greatly in appearance, and the skilled surgeon devises a solution in whatever way is possible for him.
Until the shape returns to its natural form or approximates it, and the disease itself indicates what treatment and instrument are suitable for it in most cases.
Chapter Fifteen.
On the adhesion of the eyelid to the conjunctiva or cornea.
This adhesion may occur to many people, such that the upper eyelid adheres to the white of the eye, preventing its easy movement and hindering its natural functions; it is necessary to insert the tip of a probe under the eyelid.
And raise it upwards or stretch it with a hook, then cut the adhesion with a gentle scalpel, not sharp like cutting scalpels, but rather somewhat blunt, lest the patient be disturbed during the procedure and the scalpel slip, injuring the eye with its sharpness; and your cutting should be as if you are peeling off that adhesion.
And wait until the eyelid returns to its natural state and the adhesion is completely healed, then pour into the eye salty water to wash it, or instill into it some solution of vitriol dissolved in water, then separate the eyelid from the eye with a linen wick.
And place on top of the eye wool moistened with egg white, and after the third day use the astringent eye-salves until it heals.
Chapter Sixteen.
On cutting the pterygium and fleshy excrescences.
The pterygium is of two types: either nervous, resembling a firm, thin membrane, or non-nervous, resembling a hard white moisture; if touched by iron or attempted to be removed with a hook, it will be torn.
And a hook has not been fixed in it. Both types begin from the greater canthus and gradually extend until they cover the pupil and prevent light and movement of the eye. The procedure for cutting it is that the patient should place his head in your lap.
Then he opens his eyes and you lift the eyelid with your hand, then you grasp the pterygium with a slightly curved hook and stretch it upwards, then you take a needle and insert into it a horsehair or oxhair and strong thread, and bend the end of the needle slightly and pierce it into the middle of the pterygium.
And you pass it through with the needle and tie the pterygium with the thread and stretch it upwards and dissect with the hair the side of the pterygium next to the pupil, as if you are spreading it with the hair to its end, then you cut the remainder at the root of the canthus with a fine scalpel or small scissors and leave the natural flesh of the canthus.
Lest there result from its cutting a constant flow of tears.  The pterygium may be stretched with the hook alone or with the thread as we said, then the smooth scalpel is used to dissect the end, whose image is this:
And be careful that the scalpel does not touch the cornea, lest it cause a wound in it and the pupil is injured. Then, after cutting, put in the eye some powdered salt or vitriol, and leave it until the next day, then treat it as is appropriate until it heals.
If the pterygium is not fibrous and you cannot insert a needle into it or fix a hook in it, this type of pterygium is often treated with medicines and heals as described in the classification.  If you wish to cut it, open the patient's eye.
And take a fine, smooth scalpel with a weak edge, in this image: And dissect the pterygium from above gently with it. If you see that it is loosening and fragmenting and the dissection has affected it, then instill in the eye from your container vitriol or red vitriol or powdered salt, and bandage the eye until the next day.
Then repeat the procedure until it is all gone, unless a hot inflammation interferes in the eye; then leave it and treat the hot inflammation until it heals, then resume the procedure of dissection until it heals. As for the fleshy outgrowth of the caruncle...
If the outgrowth harms the eye excessively, then hook that outgrowth with a hook and cut some of it, but do not overdo the cutting lest it cause a flow of tears, then instill in the canthi red vitriol or vitriol until it heals.
Chapter Seventeen.
On cutting the pterygium and what grows of excess flesh in the eye.
In the eyes of some people, a red, superimposed flesh may grow until it covers the pupil or approaches it or overflows onto the eyelids, and sometimes the eyelids are inverted outwards and it resembles the rose of the pomegranate.
The procedure is that the patient places his head in your lap, then he opens his eyes, and you grasp all that red flesh with hooks suitable for that.
Or you grasp it with a rasp or forceps, then you cut the flesh, one piece at a time, until you remove it all by cutting, and you must be careful of the eye lest you injure it during the procedure. Your cutting may be done either with the scalpel that we described in cutting the pterygium, or with small scissors. Like those with which one plucks the hairs, as its image comes in the chapter following this chapter.
When you have finished cutting and all the growth is gone, fill the eye with finely ground salt or instill into it some red vitriol or similar caustic medicine, and apply externally to the eye a cotton pad with egg white to prevent hot inflammation.
If some of the growth remains and the bleeding overwhelms you and you fear hot inflammation, then leave the eye and treat it with things that calm inflammation, then return to working on it until it heals.  Do likewise with the excess flesh that appears in the eye of this type.
However, you should avoid treating any eye weak by nature or by circumstance with instruments of iron, and do not undertake any of these operations until you see that the organ can tolerate the procedure.
Chapter Eighteen.
On removing pterygia from the eye.
Pterygia are red vessels that grow over the eye, hindering its function and weakening it over time.  First you should look; if the eye which has the pterygia is strong and has no other disease besides the pterygia,
then the removal is performed as follows: you should have the patient place his head in your lap, then you should hook those vessels with one or two hooks, according to your skill, and the hooks should be finely curved in this manner:.
Or they may be two hooks joined in one body, in this manner:
Then, with fine scissors, gently remove those vessels, wiping away the blood from time to time until you see that the vessels have been removed from the eye and the bleeding has stopped.  Be careful not to injure the eye with the tips of the scissors, and let your work be done at midday facing the sun.
Be very careful in your work, so as not to cut anything besides those vessels. When you have finished, instill into the eye green or red vitriol to cauterize any remaining pterygium. If you cannot remove it all at that time, bandage the eye with something that will soothe its pain.
Leave it for some days until the pain subsides and the inflammation is safe, then repeat the operation in the same manner until it is cured. This is the image of the scissors.
Leave it [and let the patient's head be on the pupil's lap, and another wipes away the tears and adjusts the gaze at the front and back of the eye, and you] the principle in this operation is to open the eye with fixed or triangular speculums held by an assistant.
Sharp, and let the patient's head be on the pupil's lap, and another wipes away the tears and adjusts the gaze at the front and back of the eye, and you have the choice of beginning to remove it from the top of the eye or from the bottom. It is preferable to remove two strands at the upper side, one at the end of the corona and the other at its beginning.
Grasp it between the middle finger and the index finger, then hook the strands from below opposite them, then grasp it between the little finger and the ring finger, and lift your hand gently until you see that all the pterygium has risen; cut it from the smaller side if you wish with scissors, or if you wish with a fine scalpel.
And insert a thread on a needle and lift all the pterygium until it rises above the conjunctiva, and do not cut the strands, for tears and blood will obscure your view.
Then cut with scissors what extends beyond the cornea from above and below, so that nothing remains attached except to the cornea. If you can lift it from the cornea by pulling it with the thread, that is best, and this may be possible in some people.
And when you have cut it, cauterize its location for several days to alleviate pain and prevent inflammation, then resume treatment.
Chapter Nineteen.
On restoring a deviated nose.
Physicians call a deviated nose a polypus. If you treat it with cautery or with a sharp, burning medication as previously described, and it does not heal, then there is no recourse except to incise the swelling when it is mature and to extract all the moisture or pus within it.
Until the bone is exposed.  If the bone is exposed and you see corruption or blackness in it, then scrape it with an instrument of this shape:
The rasp, which is made of Indian iron, is called *khushnah*. Its head is round like a button, finely engraved with the pattern of a file or rasp. You place it on the corrupted part of the bone, then turn it between your fingers, applying slight pressure, until you know that the corruption has been scraped away.
Do this several times, then dress the wound with drying, astringent medications. If the wound closes and flesh grows over it, and the discharge stops, and it remains for forty days without recurring, suppurating, or developing any complications,
know that it has healed. Otherwise, there is no recourse except to return the polypus to the nasal cavity in this manner:  Expose the bone again with an iron instrument or with a sharp medication. When the bone is exposed, take a probe of this shape:
The end of the iron instrument is triangular, and its shaft is conical, tapering towards the tip, as you see. Then place it on the bone itself, near the lacrimal duct, but slightly away from the eye. Then rotate your hand with the probe until it penetrates the bone and the patient feels it.
He will feel air exiting from it if he holds his hand to his nose.  Then dress the wound with the drying astringent medications as I have taught you. If flesh grows and the area hardens, then the matter that was flowing outwards will return to the nose, which will be easier for the patient, God willing.
Chapter Twenty.
In returning a protrusion of the eye.
If the whole eye protrudes, and no damage or impairment occurs in the vision, then the patient should be relaxed, and then bled in the cephalic vein. Then apply a cupping glass to the nape of the neck without any stipulation, and gently suck.
Then apply a poultice made from acacia, aloe, frankincense, and bdellium.  Then bind the eye over the poultice with many soft bandages.  The method for the bandages is to take many soft rags, folding each rag into four layers to make many of them.
Then place them one on top of another as much as the eye needs, then bind it firmly with a strong cloth. Repeat the cupping with suction without any stipulation, then remove the cupping glass and leave the eye bandaged for a day and a night.
If the bandage loosens during that time, it should be tightened gently. Then remove the bandage after a day and a night, as we said. If you see that the eye has returned, otherwise repeat the poultice, bandages, binding, and cupping until it returns.
Chapter twenty-one.
On the excision of the uvula.
If a hernia occurs in the uvular membrane, then it protrudes and appears outside the eyelids like a grape, and the appearance of the person is made ugly by it, and you wish to excise it, then the procedure is as I describe.
It is to insert a needle into the base of the uvula from below upwards, then insert another needle with doubled thread from the side of the uvula, and pass it through, leaving the first needle in place.
Then cut the doubled thread where it bends and tie some of the uvula around the needle with it and pull it firmly, then remove the needle and place moistened wool on the eye with egg white and leave it until the threads fall off with the uvula, then treat the eye with whatever strengthens it.
Until the wound heals.  You may also tie the uvula from each side with a circle of cloth so that the eye is further drawn together firmly, then incise with a thin scalpel reaching to the depth of the eye, so that the whitish moisture flows, and the eye descends and shrinks in place, then pull it until it heals.
Chapter twenty-two.
In the treatment of the uvula.
This ailment called kimana is a swelling that gathers in the eye resembling descending water, though it is not. The method of treatment is that you seat the patient on a chair upright, then take his head in your hands from both sides.
And move it until you see the swelling descend to the lower part with your eye, then it settles and does not move, and the light departs, and the patient sees things as he used to see them. And if it does not descend to the lower part, we know that it is water. If the descent of the swelling is not achieved by what we have mentioned,
otherwise, seat the patient before you, then take a thin lancet and incise it slightly above the cornea at the point of contact of the conjunctiva with the cornea in the iris, until the matter comes out.
Then, when it has come out, instill in the eye warm water in which honey has been mixed, or water in which fenugreek and honey have been boiled, then treat it with the rest of the treatment until it heals.
Chapter Twenty-Three.
We have mentioned the types of water in the classification and which of them is suitable for cupping, in a detailed and explained statement, so take it from there correctly. Then it is necessary that you seat the patient before you, sitting squarely facing the light near the sun.
And you bind his healthy eye and bind it tightly, then you lift the eyelid of his eye with your left hand if the eye in which the water is the left eye, or with your right hand if it is the right eye.
Then you place the tip of the cupping instrument near the cornea, with the thickness of a probe, in the same white of the eye on the side of the smaller canthus, then you push the cupping instrument forcefully while turning it with your hand until it penetrates the white of the eye and you feel with the cupping instrument that it has reached something empty.
And it is necessary that the extent of the cupping instrument's penetration into the depth should be the distance that is from the pupil to the end of the black, which is the cornea; for the copper is seen in the same viewer's sight, clearly because of the clarity of the corneal membrane, then you move the cupping instrument upwards to the place in which the water is.
Then press it downwards repeatedly; if the water descends immediately, then the patient will see what his sight has been opened to immediately, and the cupping instrument is in his eye, then it rests a little; if the water rises, then lower it again without removing the cupping instrument.
Then if it settles and does not rise, remove the cupping instrument gently while you twist it with your hand little by little, then dissolve in the water a little clean Andarani salt and wash the eye with it from the inside, then place on the outside of the eye completely a compress or wet wool.
With rose oil and egg white, and bind it with the healthy eye; but as for us and the people of our time, we place on it crushed cumin with egg white. If the cupping instrument does not respond to entering the eye because of its hardness, because some people have very hard eyes, Then it is necessary that you take the scalpel which is called al-barīd, whose image is this:
Then pierce with it only the adherent part and do not exaggerate in the piercing; it is only that you should strike the cupping instrument a delicate place, then introduce the cupping instrument as we have mentioned.  And when your work is complete, prepare for the patient a firm recumbent position in which he sleeps on his back in a dark room.
And he abstains from all movements and from coughing, and you make his food that which softens his constitution, and he does not move his head to the right or left at all, and the bandage remains in place until the third day, then you untie it in that dark room and test his sight.
And you show him things, then return the bandage until the seventh day. And it is not appropriate to do that during treatment or after cupping at your time, but it is necessary that you avoid that before the water ascends quickly with intense looking.
If a hot swelling appears, then it is necessary to untie the eye before the seventh [day] and treat that with what calms the swelling until, when it calms, then at that time you release his sight and place on his face a kerchief that trains his sight from under it for several days while he is in that dark room, then…
he leaves the house gradually and manages its causes. And know that the student in cupping cannot dispense with observation many times, then after that he proceeds to the work. And it has reached me from some Iraqis that he mentioned that he makes in Iraq a cupping instrument that is perforated, with which he draws out the water.
And I have not seen anyone in our country make that, nor read it in a book of the books of the ancients. And it is possible that this is something recent. And this is an image of types of cupping instruments for him who is ignorant of them to stand upon:
It is made of copper specifically, and its tip is with this same thinness, triangular-tipped, sharp.
Chapter twenty-four.
In the treatment of the flesh that grows in the nose.
There may grow in the nose various kinds of excessive flesh, of which there is something resembling a scorpion with many legs, and of which there is that which is cancerous flesh, hardened, dark in color, and of which there is that which is soft flesh, not dark in color.
As for that which of these fleshes is soft, not malignant nor cancerous, it should be that you seat the patient before you facing the sun, and open his nostril, and cast the snare into that flesh, then pull it outwards, then cut what you have grasped of it with a delicate, sharp scalpel from one side.
Until you know that all the flesh has gone. If some remains that you could not cut, then remove it with one of the delicate instruments gently, until nothing of it remains. If the blood overcomes you or a hot swelling appears,
then counteract it with what is necessary. Or if it were of the malignant tumors, then hasten to cauterize it until the bleeding stops and all the flesh is gone. Then place in the nose after the cutting vinegar and water or a drink. If the nose opens and moisture passes from it to the throat,
know that he has healed. If the moisture does not pass as it should, then know that inside there is flesh growing on the top of the weakened bones that the instrument did not reach with the cutting. Then it should be that you take a thread of linen of some thickness.
and tie many knots in it, and make between each knot the space of a finger or less.  The patient should skillfully insert one end of the thread into his nose with a probe or with whatever he is able to after making it like a button, and pull his breath until it reaches the pharynx.
and it comes out at his throat. And often boys in school do this action, and it is a matter easy for whomever wants it. Then gather the two ends of the thread, the one end that came out at the mouth and the other that remained in the nose.
Then use the knots in the thread to saw the flesh, doing that until you know that the flesh has been cut by the knots of the thread. Then remove the thread and place in the nose, after wiping away the blood, a wick that has soaked in Egyptian ointment. Do this for three days or more.
until the ointment eats away all that remains of the flesh. Then, as a final thing, place a lead tube in the nose for some days until it heals. If it needs a drying treatment, use that.
And this is an image of the syringe with which ointments and medicines are dropped into the nose:
It is made of silver or copper, similar to a small, open oil lamp, and its passage is likewise.
And if you wish, make the tube closed like a reed, and the nozzle of the syringe flat.
It has a handle at its end, as you see, to hold it when the ointment or whatever you wish of juices or flowing things is heated in it.
Chapter Twenty-five.
On warts growing on the tip of the nose.
Often a wart grows on the tip of the nose and increases and grows larger with the days until its appearance becomes ugly.
Therefore, it should be cut off at its first appearance and completely excised, then cauterize the area or apply a burning medication that takes the place of cautery. If cutting it off is missed until it grows large, then look: if it is hardened, solid, dark in color, and insensitive,
do not use an instrument on it, for it is a cancerous tumor, and I have often seen those who cut this tumor suffer great harm from it.  If the tumor is soft to the touch, not dark in color, and you see that it can be completely cut, empty the patient's bowels and cut it without hesitation or delay, and treat the area with what dries and contracts until it heals.
Chapter Twenty-Six.
On suturing the nose, lip, and ear. If their connection is separated by a wound or something similar, know that when a separation of connection occurs in one of these cartilages, the treatment is rarely effective except in some people.
So if something like that happens to someone, see if the wound is fresh with its blood, then bring the edges of the wound together with sutures, and then treat it until it heals.  And if the separation of the connection has separated its edges and each part is whole, then you should scrape each part to the thickness of its outer skin until it bleeds.
Then bring the edges together with sutures and tighten them, and sprinkle on them mastic and frankincense, powdered, and place over the sutures a plaster of palm-tree ointment or another of the adhesive ointments, and leave it bound for two or three days, then unbind it.
And change the medication and leave it until the threads separate by themselves, then treat it with ointment until it heals.  The method of suturing is to bring together the separated connection either with needles, as we described in suturing the abdomen, or with thread, as I have shown you there.
Chapter twenty-seven.
On removing the knots which appear in the lips.
Many people develop small, hard tumors inside their lips; some resemble a barleycorn, some are smaller, and some larger.  It is necessary to evert the lip and incise each nodule and suspend it with a hook.
And cut it from each side, then pack the area after cutting with powdered alum until the bleeding stops, then rinse with vinegar and salt, and treat the area with a styptic until the wounds heal.
Chapter twenty-eight.
On cutting away excess flesh in the gums.
Often, excess flesh grows on the gums, which the ancients called apoulos. It should be hooked with a hook or grasped with forceps and cut at its base, and leave the moisture or blood to flow, then apply powdered alum to the area.
Or one of the astringent drying powders. If that flesh returns after treatment—for it often returns—cut it again and cauterize it, for it will not return after cauterization.
Chapter twenty-nine.
On extracting teeth with iron.
Rough, unsightly crusts may gather on the surfaces of the teeth, inside and out, and between the gums.  They may blacken, yellow, and green, even causing damage to the gums and disfiguring the teeth.
Then you should seat the patient before you, his head in your lap, and clean the tooth or teeth in which you see the crusts and sandy substance until nothing remains.  You should do the same with the black, green, yellow, and other discolorations until you have cleaned them.
If the discoloration is removed at the first cleaning, well and good. Otherwise, repeat the cleaning on another day, and then again, until you achieve your goal. Know that different shaped and many forms of scrapers are needed for the teeth, depending on what suits your work.
Because the instrument used to scrape the inside of a tooth is different from that used to scrape the outside, and that used between the teeth is of yet another form.  These are several forms of scrapers that you should have prepared:
Chapter Thirty.
On the Extraction of Teeth.
You should treat the aching tooth by every means and delay its extraction, for there is no replacement for it once extracted, because it is a noble substance, except when its extraction is unavoidable.
If the patient is determined on its extraction, you should ascertain that it is the aching tooth. For often the patient is deceived by the pain and thinks that it is in the sound tooth, and extracts it, then the pain does not go away until the diseased tooth is extracted.  We have seen this done repeatedly by barbers.
If you are certain which tooth is aching, then you should incise around the tooth with a moderately strong scalpel, so as to loosen the gum from all sides, then move it slightly with your fingers or with delicate forceps, little by little, until you loosen it.
Then firmly grasp it with the large forceps, and hold the patient's head between your knees, having steadied him so that he does not move; then pull the tooth straight out, so as not to break it. If it does not come out otherwise, take one of those instruments and carefully insert it under it from each side, gently rocking it as you did before.
And if the tooth is perforated or decayed, you should fill the hole with a piece of cloth and pack it firmly with the end of a thin probe, lest it crumble when you pull it with the forceps; and you should make sure to thoroughly incise around the gum on every side.
And take care not to break it, for if some of it remains, it will cause the patient a greater affliction than his initial pain. And beware of doing what ignorant barbers do, in their haste and recklessness in extraction without using what we have described.
For they often inflict great harm on people, the least of which is that the tooth breaks and all or part of its roots remain. Or it may be extracted with part of the jawbone, as we have often witnessed. Then, after extraction, gargle with a drink or with vinegar and salt. If bleeding occurs from the site—and this often happens—then crush some alum and pack it into the site.
Otherwise, cauterize it if the alum does not help. The image of the delicate forceps with which you initially move the tooth: they should have long ends, short handles, and be thick, lest they bend when you grasp the tooth with them. And this is the image of the large forceps:
They should be, as you see, thick in the handles, so that when you grasp them, they do not yield or bend; short in the ends; and made of Indian iron or of well-tempered steel, sharpened at the ends, and at the tip, teeth that interlock, so that they grasp firmly and tightly.
And the ends may be made in the shape of a file, which will also provide a strong grip.
Chapter Thirty-One.
On extracting the roots of molars and removing fractured jawbones.
If, when extracting a tooth, a root remains broken, you should apply a cotton pledget soaked in butter to the area for a day or two, until the area relaxes, then insert the forceps or hooks. whose ends resemble the beak of a bird called a *bulruja*.  And this is an image of the hooks:
They should be made like a file on the inside, or like a rasp. If you are unable to extract it with these hooks, you should excavate around the root and expose all the flesh with a scalpel, then insert an instrument resembling a small lever, which is depicted thus:
short at the end, somewhat thick, and not pointed, lest it break. If the root is extracted by this means, well and good; otherwise, use these other instruments, which are depicted thus: the first has a triangular end and is somewhat thick.
Another fine triangular image:
And we may also use this instrument with two branches, which is also depicted thus:
and other instruments and tools previously mentioned in the extraction of teeth.  We may also use this instrument which resembles a large hook, which is depicted thus:
Triangular at the bent end, somewhat thick, lest it break; it should not be pointed.  Know that instruments for teeth are numerous, as are all other instruments; they are almost innumerable.  A skillful and clever craftsman may invent his own instruments according to what the work and the diseases themselves indicate.
For there are diseases for which the ancients did not mention instruments, because of the diversity of their types. If a bone of the jaw or one of the bones of the mouth is fractured or becomes rotten, then investigate its location with one of these instruments and hooks mentioned for the extraction of roots.
And we use a forceps, its image is this:
It should be somewhat thick to grasp the bone so that it does not slip until the bone is removed.  We treat the area with appropriate medicines. If the bone has slight decay, scrape away its decay and blackness until it is clean, then treat it until it heals.
Chapter Thirty-Two
On the extraction of molars growing on top of others.
If molars grow in a place other than their natural position, their appearance is marred, especially if this happens in women and slaves.  It should be examined; if the molar has grown behind another tooth...
and its extraction or pushing back is not possible, then extract it. If it is attached to another tooth, cut it with this instrument, whose image is this:
It resembles a small beak and should be made of sharp Indian iron.  Cut it in several sessions because of the tooth’s hardness, lest you disturb other teeth. But if it is firmly rooted, file it down with an Indian file, whose image is this.
It should be entirely of Indian [steel], its cutting edge very fine, like the file used to make needles. File the tooth little by little over many days gently, lest you loosen the tooth and it falls out. Then file it again and remove it with some forceps.
If a tooth is broken and hurts the tongue when speaking, it should also be filed down until the roughness of the fracture is gone, it is even, and smooth, so it does not hurt the tongue or impair speech.
Chapter Thirty-Three.
On binding loose teeth with silver or gold threads.
If the front teeth become loose and mobile from a blow or fall, and the patient cannot bite on anything to eat for fear they will fall out, and you have treated them with astringent medicines but the treatment has not worked.
The solution is to tie them with a gold or silver thread; gold is better because silver tarnishes and rots after a few days, while gold remains unchanged forever and is not subject to that. The thread should be of a medium thickness, appropriate for the space between the teeth.
The method of binding is to take the thread and insert its loop between the healthy teeth, then weave the ends of the thread between the loose teeth, whether one or more, until you reach a healthy tooth on the other side by weaving.
Then weave back to the side where you began, and pull gently and carefully so that they do not move at all.  Tighten the thread at the roots of the teeth so that it does not come loose. Then cut off the excess thread with scissors, gather it together, twist it, and hide it between the healthy and loose tooth.
So that it does not injure the tongue. Then leave it tied as long as it remains. If it becomes loose or breaks, tie it with another thread.  In this way, he will enjoy it for his whole life. This is an image of the teeth and how to bind them, showing two healthy teeth and two loose teeth, as you see.
It may be that a single tooth or two, after falling out, can be returned to its place and bound as we described, and it will remain. This is done by a skillful dentist.  He may carve a bone from cattle and shape it like a tooth, and place it in the place from which the tooth has fallen. And it is tightened as we said, and it remains, and he enjoys it.
Chapter Thirty-Four.
Concerning the cutting of the ligament which appears under the tongue and prevents speech.
This ligament which appears under the tongue may be either natural, with which a person is born, or accidental, from a wound which has healed.
And the procedure is that you open the mouth of the patient and place his head in your lap and raise his tongue, then cut that ligament transversely until the tongue is released from its restraint. If there is some stiffness and complexity, and this is from the healing of a wound, then place a hook in it and split it transversely until the ligament is severed and the knot is loosened.
And beware that the incision be not deep into the flesh, lest you cut an artery there and bleeding results. Then let him rinse the mouth after the cutting with rose water or with vinegar and cold water, then place under the tongue a pledget of lint which the patient holds in place each night.
Lest it unite again.  If bleeding occurs, place powdered alum on the place. If the bleeding overcomes you, cauterize the place with a lentil-shaped cautery suitable for this. Then treat it with other treatments until it heals.
Chapter Thirty-Five.
On removing a frog which has formed under the tongue.
A swelling resembling a small frog may occur under the tongue, preventing the tongue from its natural function, and it may even grow large enough to fill the mouth. The treatment is to open the patient's mouth towards the sun and look at the swelling.
If you see it dark colored or black, hard and insensitive to the patient, do not interfere with it, for it is a cancer. But if it is whitish and moist, insert a hook into it and dissect it with a fine scalpel, freeing it from every side.
If bleeding overwhelms you during the operation, apply powdered vitriol until the bleeding stops, then resume your work until you remove it completely. Then let the patient rinse the mouth with vinegar and salt.
Then treat it with other suitable remedies until it heals.
Chapter Thirty-Six.
In the treatment of tonsil tumors.
And what grows in the throat from other tumors, glands may appear inside the throat resembling those that appear outside, called tonsils. If you treat them with what I mentioned in the section and they do not heal,
then look: if the tumor is hard, dark in color, and insensitive, do not approach it with an instrument; and if it is red in color and its base is thick, do not approach it with an instrument either, for fear of hemorrhage, but leave it until it matures, then either lance it or it will burst of its own accord.
And if it is white in color, round, and its base is thin, this is what should be cut.  The procedure is that you should look before operating whether its hot inflammation has completely subsided, or lessened somewhat.
Then seat the patient facing the sun, with his head in your lap, and open his mouth; and take an assistant with you who will press his tongue downward with an instrument of this form:
It is made of silver or copper, thin like a knife.  When you press the tongue down with it and the tumor is visible and you see it, take a hook and pierce it into the tonsil and pull it outward as much as possible without pulling any of the membranes with it.
Then cut it with an instrument of this form, resembling scissors except that its two ends are curved, each one next to the other, very sharp; it is made of Indian iron or Damascene steel:
If this instrument is not available, then cut it with a scalpel of this form:
Sharp on one side and not sharp on the other. After you cut one tonsil, cut the other in the same way. Then, after cutting, gargle the patient with cold water or vinegar and water.
If bleeding occurs, gargle with water in which pomegranate peel or myrtle leaves or similar astringents have been boiled until the bleeding stops, then treat him until he is healed. Other tumors may grow in the throat besides the tonsils; cut them as described for cutting the tonsils.
I treated a woman who had a tumor that had grown inside her throat, resembling a polyp, somewhat insensitive, which almost blocked the throat. The woman was breathing through a narrow passage and was prevented from eating and drinking water.
She was on the verge of death if she remained for a day or two.  Two branches extended from the tumor, even emerging from her nostrils. I acted quickly, inserted a hook into one of them and pulled; a suitable piece was extracted.
Then I cut it where I reached it through the nostril. Then I did the same with what protruded from the other nostril.  Then I opened her mouth, depressed her tongue, inserted the hook into the tumor itself, and cut some of it; only a little blood flowed.
The woman's throat opened, and she immediately began to drink water and then eat.  We continued to cut away portions of the tumor repeatedly over a long time, the tumor replacing what was cut, until it lasted a long time for both of us.
Then I devised a plan and cauterized the tumor inside the throat.  It stopped growing, and she traveled away; I do not know what God did with her afterward.
Chapter Thirty-Seven.
On cutting a tumor of the uvula.
That which is called ‘an-‘unabah’ if it descends as a growth onto the uvula and swells, and is white and oblong, it is called ‘amood’; and if it is thicker at the bottom and round, it is called ‘an-‘unabah’. If it is treated as mentioned in the classification and the treatment is not effective…
And I saw that the hot tumor had subsided from her, and it was thin; so it should be cut, and whatever part of it was clustered and round, and did not have length, and was bloody or the color of pus, or black, or insensitive, then it should be avoided cutting.
For in it there is danger to the patient. So if you see it in the manner I described, of its whiteness and length, then you should seat the patient next to the sun and compress his tongue with the instrument whose description I presented earlier, then pierce the uvula with the hook.
And pull it downwards and cut it with one of the two instruments which I mentioned in cutting the tonsils. And it is necessary that you not cut from it except that which exceeds the natural state, without excess, because if you cut more from it, you will harm the voice and speech.
Then after cutting, use what we described in cutting the tonsils, and treat it until it heals. If the patient is afraid of cutting it, then it is necessary to use a method in cauterizing it without fear or caution. The method of cauterization in it is only with a sharp medicine, and that is that you place the patient’s head in your lap.
Then compress his tongue with the instrument we mentioned, then take from the sharp water which I mentioned in the chapter on cauterization, and mix it with unquenched lime, and make it neither thick nor thin, and fill the concavity of this instrument with it. And this is its image:
Its tip, in which you place the medicine, has a concavity like the concavity of a spoon, and you place the instrument with the medicine on the uvula itself, and the patient lying on his side so that the saliva flows from his mouth from inside his throat, lest something descend from it into his throat and harm him.
Then hold your hand with the medicine while you press it on the uvula for about half an hour until you see that it has blackened and the sting of the medicine has subsided. And if you wish, take cotton, wrap it around the end of a probe, and moisten the cotton in the medicine.
And insert the probe with the cotton into the tube from above until the cotton adheres to the uvula. Do this repeatedly until you achieve what you want from cauterizing the uvula, then leave it. It will wither and fall after three or four days. If you need to reapply the medicine, reapply it.
After cauterization, wipe around the uvula with cotton soaked in butter and wipe away the medicine around it. Then gargle with cold water and treat from the outside with ointments and from the inside with gargles until it heals. This type of treatment is safer than cutting and further from fear.
The uvula may also be treated with something gentler than cauterization and cutting; it is treated with fumigation in this manner:  Take fumitory, hyssop, thyme, rue, wormwood, chamomile, and costmary, and similar herbs. Gather them all, or some of them, in a pot and submerge them in vinegar.
Boil it, the pot being plastered with medicinal clay, and there should be a hole in the middle of the lid onto which a hollow instrument is fitted, made in this manner, of silver or copper:
Insert the end with the pomegranate into the patient's mouth so that the steam rises to the uvula through the tube until the uvula is well steamed. Then repeat this several times until it withers. And beware of performing this treatment at the first onset of the swelling.
For it often increases the swelling.  Rather, it should be done when its hot swelling subsides. If you do not have this instrument, take a reed and fit a piece of eggshell to its end so that the patient's mouth is not burned.
Because the eggshell prevents the heat of the steam from burning the mouth, and this is among the good treatments and is safe.
Chapter Thirty-Eight.
On removing thorns and similar things that lodge in the throat.
Often a fish bone or thorn or something similar lodges in the throat.  What is visible should be removed with the eye, after the tongue has been pressed down with an instrument, near the uvula.
So that you may see what is in the throat.  And if it does not appear to you and is hidden in the throat, then the patient should vomit before his food is digested in his stomach, for perhaps the lodged thing will come out with the vomit. Or the patient should swallow a piece of turnip or a head of lettuce.
Or he should swallow a mouthful of dry bread, or you may take a piece of soft sea sponge and tie it to a string, then he should swallow it. When it reaches the place of the thorn, pull the string quickly; do this several times.
Often the thorn or bone will stick to it and come out. If it does not come out by the methods mentioned, then use a lead instrument of this shape:
It should be slightly thicker than a probe, and at its end a hook. The patient inserts it gently into his throat, raising his head upwards and being careful not to touch his windpipe, lest he cough and thereby push the bone or thorn further in. Or the physician may insert it with his hand.
And it is better for the patient to insert it himself, because he knows the location of the lodged object, and he pushes it downwards or pulls it upwards with the instrument, all this as much as he is able, until it comes out.
Chapter Thirty-Nine.
On removing leeches lodged in the throat.
If you treat the leech with what we mentioned in the section on medicinal treatment and it is ineffective, then look into the patient's throat in the sunlight after you have pressed down his tongue with the instrument I described to you.
If you see the leech, pull it out with a small hook or a fine, tightly woven swab.  If you cannot do this, then take a hollow tube and insert it into the patient's throat near the leech, then insert a hot iron into the tube's cavity.
Do this repeatedly, and the patient should abstain from water for the whole day. Then he should take a jug full of cold water, open his mouth into it, and rinse his mouth with it, without swallowing a drop, moving the water around from time to time with his hand.
The leech will fall out when it senses the water. If it does not come out as described, fumigate the throat with urine and with asafoetida using the instrument described for fumigating the tonsils; do this repeatedly, for it will fall out.
The procedure for fumigation is as follows: take a pot containing hot coals, the pot being covered with a lid having a hole in its middle.  Fit the end of the instrument into this hole, then add the fumigant. The patient places his mouth over the end of the tube and closes his mouth so that the fumigant does not escape, until he knows that the fumigant has reached his throat.
Then the leech will fall out. If it does not, repeat the fumigation.  The patient should endure thirst, eat salty things and garlic, and not drink water; the leech must come out with this treatment. This is an image of an instrument for extracting the leech from the throat when it is visible.
It resembles hooks, as you see, except that it has this curve which enters the throat, and its ends resemble a bird's beak; they are rough like a file, so that when they grasp something, they do not let go.
And another instrument, with which the tongue is compressed; it is a wide iron ring, large enough to fit in the mouth over the tongue. It is inserted into the mouth standing on its side, so that the mouth remains open without the patient's will, and he is completely unable to remove it until it is taken away.
The description of the instrument with which the mouth is opened in this same way.
Chapter Forty.
It will deal with the cauterization and incision of tumors.
Tumors are numerous and varied in kind, as will be described one by one in this book.  They differ in their cauterization and incision in two respects: one, from the type of tumor itself and what it contains of humors.
The second type is due to the locations in the body where they occur, for a tumor occurring in the head is not the same as a tumor occurring in the anus, and a tumor occurring in a fleshy location is not the same as a tumor occurring in a joint; each has its own rule of treatment.
Some tumors should not be cauterized except after the pus in them has matured and is complete, while others should be cauterized while they are raw and have not fully matured, such as tumors that are near the joints.
For if a tumor occurs near a joint and its condition persists until it corrupts what is around it, it may damage the ligaments or nerves of that joint, thus causing the crippling of that limb; or the tumor may be near a principal organ.
Because if you delay its cauterization until it matures, you will harm that principal organ; or it may be near the anus, so cauterize it raw.  It is necessary to cauterize a raw, not fully mature tumor that is near the anus, lest it become putrid and penetrate into the interior of the anus, becoming a fistula.
And it becomes to a point where it does not heal. You should know the time for cauterizing tumors that have fully matured, which is when the pain of the tumor subsides, the fever is gone, the redness and throbbing decrease, the head of the tumor softens, and all other signs [of maturity] appear.
And you should apply the cautery to the lowest possible part of the tumor, if that is possible, so that the flow [of pus] is easier downwards; or in the thinnest and most prominent part of the tumor.  The cautery should be applied lengthwise along the limb if the tumors are in the hands or feet.
And the places of muscles, tendons, nerves, and arteries; in short, in all smooth places that do not bend. As for those that bend, the cautery should be applied according to that location.  If the tumor is in fleshy places, it is best to leave it uncauterized.
Until its maturation is complete, as we have said, for if you cauterize it before that, the discharge of pus will be prolonged, it will be very harmful and dirty, and perhaps its lips and base will harden. Some tumors may be cauterized across the body, when necessary, or according to what the member requires.
