You should use one cautery for small tumors, and for large tumors a wide cautery or many incisions, according to the size of the tumor.  There may be tumors where it is necessary to scrape away the skin and cut it if it has become like a rag.
And it has reached the point where what has died is like what occurs in many abscesses and hidden collections. Some of them are incised with a three-cornered incision, and some are cut like an ivy leaf, like the inguinal tumor.
Some use a circular incision, a crescent-shaped incision, and similar incisions.  If it does not have a head, like smooth, flat tumors, then it should only be cauterized simply. If the tumor is large and has been present for a long time and you cauterize it, you should not rush to remove all the pus at that time.
Rather, remove some of it, then dress the tumor until another day, then remove some more of the pus; do this repeatedly gradually until you remove all of it.
Especially if the patient is weak, or a pregnant woman, or a small child, or an old man, for the vital spirit often dissolves with the sudden exit of pus, so the patient may die and you do not notice, so be very careful in this matter.
After cauterizing these tumors, you should wipe the wound and look at it. If the wound of the tumor is small or the incision is single and simple, use lint or old cotton for packing. If the tumor is large and the cauterization incisions are numerous,
then you should insert a wick into each incision until some of them reach each other.  And if some of the skin of the tumor has been cut or scraped away, then you should pack it with old cotton or linen lint without moisture and dress it until the third day.
Then remove it and treat it with the appropriate ointments until it heals. If bleeding occurs while you are working, use cold water and vinegar, after soaking a linen cloth in them, and apply it to the bleeding site repeatedly.
If the bleeding persists, you should use the measures, treatments, and styptics that we have described in many places in this book and in the division. If it is winter and the location of the tumor is very sensitive, you should moisten the pledgets with a warm potion and oil.
And place them on the site. If it is summer and the areas are fleshy, you should apply pledgets soaked in water and oil or in a potion and oil, all of it cold, until the third day, as we said. Then you should remove the dressing from the tumor and wipe it.
And use similar treatments until it heals. This is what you need to know about the general treatment of tumors. As for the detailed method, I have described how to treat each tumor in a summarized, categorized manner.
Chapter forty-one.
Concerning incisions on tumors that appear on the scalp.
Small tumors appear on the scalp; they are a type of boil contained within sacs, similar in appearance to a chicken’s pouch.  There are many types; some are fatty, and some contain a moisture resembling pus.
Some contain a moisture resembling whey and broth, and so on. Some are hard and calcified.  There is no danger in incising and removing all of them, unless you encounter an artery during the incision.
The procedure for incising them is to first probe them with the instrument, the image of which follows, called the “muddass”, so that you know what it contains. If its contents are fluid, incise it lengthwise with a simple incision, in this manner:
Begin the incision from point B to point C. When the fluid has been evacuated, remove the sac that contained the fluid and cut it away completely, leaving nothing at all, for it often recurs if any part remains.
Then dip a cotton swab in yellow ointment, if available; otherwise, in salt water, and fill the wound with it. Leave it until the next day, for it will consume what remains of the sac. Then reapply the cotton swab with the yellow ointment a second and third time, if necessary.
Until you are certain that nothing of the sac remains. Then treat the wound with ointments until it heals. If the swelling contains fatty material, incise it in this manner:
Make a firm incision, and place hooks in the edges of the wound, and strip it from all sides.  Do your best to remove the membrane containing it. If an artery obstructs you, do as I have described to you. Do likewise in the swelling if it is hardened from the incision.
The treatment is the same as I have described.  The incision on a hardened swelling is easier because it contains little blood and fluid. Some of these swellings in the head in some people contain no fluid at all. For I incised a swelling in...
the head of an old woman, and I found the swelling like hard, rough, white stone, impossible to break; if someone threw it, it would wound him.  It was unlike other swellings occurring in the head, except for those appearing in the heads of children.
And at the roots of the ears, incise them all with a simple incision, and make their base from below, so that the material may easily flow downwards.  Then treat them with what suits them of treatment.
Chapter Forty-two.
Concerning the incision on the swellings which frequently appear in the neck. These swellings frequently appear in the neck and under the armpits and in the groins; they may appear in the rest of the body. That which appears in the neck may be single, or it may be multiple, and some develop from others.
And each swelling of them is within a special membrane, as is the case with the boils and the swellings of the head as we have described. And the types of these swellings are many; some are hardened, some contain fluids, and some are malignant and do not respond to treatment.
So I saw one of them which was good in condition to the touch, and its appearance was close to the color of the skin; it moved in every direction and was not attached to a nerve of the neck, nor to a vein, nor to an artery, nor was it deep-seated. Then it should be incised with a simple incision from the top to the bottom of the body in this manner, from line J to line B.
And peel it from every side, and stretch the lips of the skin with a hook or with two hooks or with many hooks, if that is needed, as we said in the case of the swellings of the head; and remove it little by little, and be careful not to cut a vein or a nerve, and let the scalpel not be very sharp.
Lest your hand increase the cutting, or the patient be troubled, and you cut what you do not need to cut.  If you cut a vein or an artery and it hinders your work, then place in the wound powdered verdigris or some of the styptics which stop blood.
And tighten the wound and leave it until the acuteness of the swelling subsides and the wound relaxes and begins to suppurate, for the bleeding will then stop; then return to your work until you finish.  Then examine with your index finger if there remain any other small tumors; then cut them and cleanse them.
If there is a large vein at the root of the tumor, then it should not be cut from its root, but it should be tied with a doubled tight thread and left until it falls off by itself without harm; then stuff the wound with old cotton which has been dipped in Egyptian ointment, then treat it.
And if you cut the whole tumor, then it is necessary to bring together the lips of the wound and stitch it immediately after you know that no residue remains at all. If you see that there remains a residue of the bone of the tumor.
Then it is necessary that you aim with the cutting at its origin and base, and use the stitching and what we have mentioned. And as for the tumors containing fluids, apply a simple cautery where the place of their ripening appears to you, and make the cautery from the lower side as we said.
Then after cauterization, use a dressing with Egyptian ointment and the like to consume what remains of the corruption, until when you see the wound has been cleansed, then treat it with ointments that grow flesh until it heals.
Chapter forty-three.
Concerning the incision of the throat for a tumor that occurs inside the throat.
The ancients mentioned this incision in the throat, but I have not seen anyone in our country perform it. This is the account of their words. As for those with angina, they should avoid the incision of the throat, for they will not benefit from it.
Because all the veins and the lung will be weak, but those with a hot tumor in the mouth or throat or tonsils, if there is no illness in the trachea, then it is necessary to use an incision of the throat to escape the harm that results from suffocation.
If we intend to do this, then we should incise the throat below three or four rings of the trachea, a small transverse incision between two rings, to the extent of the incision in the peritoneum, not in the cartilage. This location is suitable for the incision.
Because it is devoid of flesh and its blood vessels are distant. If the physician is timid, then he should stretch the skin of the throat with a hook, then incise the skin until he reaches the trachea, avoiding the blood vessels if he sees any.
Then he should incise the peritoneum which we have described.  The incision of the trachea is indicated by the phlegm that exits from it, along with what is excised, and by the cessation of the voice. The wound is left open for a time, and when the time that the suffocation was feared has passed, the edges of the wound are brought together from the skin.
And its incision alone, without the cartilage, then use medicines that cause the flesh to grow until it heals. The author of this book said: The explanation of all this which we have narrated is only if they see that the patient's throat is obstructed by one of these tumors that were mentioned.
And the patient is close to death, and his life is about to end, they proceed to incise the throat so that the patient breathes through the wound, and is saved from death.  They were ordered to leave the wound open until the severity of the illness subsides, and its severity is about three days or so.
Then they are ordered to suture the wound and treat it until it heals. What I witnessed myself is that a maidservant took a knife and thrust it into her throat, cutting some of the windpipe. I was called to treat her, and I found her weakening like one who is slaughtered.  So I uncovered the wound.
Then I found the blood that came from the wound to be little, so I knew that she had not cut a vein nor a jugular vein, and the air was exiting from the wound.  I proceeded, incised the wound, and treated it until it healed. Nothing happened to the maidservant except hoarseness in her voice, and no more.
And after days she returned to her best condition.  From this we say that incising the throat is not dangerous.
Chapter forty-four.
Concerning the incision on the tumor which appears in the throat from the outside and is called the throat’s elephantiasis.
This tumor which is called the throat’s elephantiasis is a large tumor, the color of the body. It is frequent in women and is of two types.
Either it is natural or accidental. As for the natural, there is no remedy for it; as for the accidental, it is of two kinds: one resembling fatty swellings, and the other resembling the tumor which results from the obstruction of an artery, and its incision is dangerous. Therefore, one should not at all use the iron on it, except what is small of it; if you probe and examine it with a probe and find it resembles a fatty swelling and is not connected to anything of the veins, then incise it as you incise fatty swellings. And remove it with its containing sac, if it is in a sac; otherwise, remove it all completely, then treat the place with the appropriate treatment.
Chapter forty-five.
Concerning the incision of various swellings.
Swellings are of many types, and I have mentioned all their types in the classification. It is necessary to explain here the difference between a swelling and an abscess, since it is difficult. I say that with an abscess there is heat, fever, and frightening pain until the boiling of the matter subsides. And the putrefaction is completed, then the fever and intensity subside.  A swelling is not accompanied by heat, fever, or pain, and it is contained within a membranous sac, which is its special covering. It is the color of the body, and its beginning is like a lentil, and it becomes like a blister, larger or smaller.
And it is of two types: either fatty, or it contains moisture. The colors of the moisture are many, as I have mentioned in the classification. Therefore, when you come to treat a swelling, you should first probe and examine it with the instrument called the probe, whose image will come in the chapter after this.
Assess the size of the swelling, then remove the probe and look at what comes out after it. If fluid of any color comes out, then incise it with a simple incision, as I have mentioned in the case of other swellings. If no fluid comes out after the probe, then know that it is fatty.
Then make a firm incision on it in this manner, as I have taught you, and attach it with hooks. Carefully peel the skin from every side, and preserve the sac, if you are able, so that you may remove it whole with the swelling.
If the sac is ruptured during the procedure and you are unable to remove it whole—and this often happens—then remove it piece by piece until nothing remains. For if any part of it remains, however small or large, the swelling will return in most cases.
If it overcomes you and a little remains, cauterize the wound at its completion with some sharp cauterizing instruments.  Tighten the wound and place on top of it something that soothes hot swelling and treat it with other treatments until it heals. If the swelling is large, suture its lips and treat it with that which heals.
If you encounter a superficial or deep vein and bleeding occurs, quickly cauterize the area with powdered vitriol and leave it bound for two or three days until the wound becomes infected and the flow of blood stops. Then return to removing what remains of the swelling.

On the forms of instruments.
Those which are used in incision and cauterization; among them are the forms of probes, and they are of three types: large, medium, and small.

Image of a small probe.
They are suitable for exploring tumors, wounds, fistulas, and cavities for what is inside them of bone and the like. They are made round, polished, and smooth like stylets, of Chinese copper, or of *ispadruwiyya*, or of copper, or of iron, or of silver; and the best of them are those made of *ispadruwiyya*.
Probes may also be made of black lead.  They are suitable for probing fistulas which have a curve in their depth, so that they flex gently with that curve.  They are also of three types, for there are long ones, medium ones, and short ones.
According to the need of the depth of each fistula, and its thickness is made according to the width and narrowness of the fistula. Image of a large lead probe.

But they are not used to draw blood from every part of the body, but rather from fleshy areas such as the muscles of the leg and thigh, the muscles of the arm, the breast, the abdomen, the groin, and similar moist fleshy parts.

Chapter Forty-Seven.
On the treatment of men's breasts.
The breasts of some people may swell at the age of puberty until they resemble women's breasts, remaining swollen and unsightly. If this is disliked, then it is necessary to incise the breast with a crescent-shaped incision in this manner.
From line B to line C. Then remove all the fat, then fill the wound with a cauterizing medicine, then bring the edges of the wound together with sutures and treat it until it heals.  If the breast inclines downwards and becomes flaccid due to its size, as happens with women,
it is necessary to make two crescent-shaped incisions in its upper sides, each joining the other at their ends so that the larger line encircles the smaller one, in this manner: From line B to line C, then remove the skin between the incisions and remove the fat, and use what we mentioned of sutures and dressings and whatever treatment is needed until it heals.
If you cannot remove all that needs to be removed because of the patient's anxiety or because of bleeding, then you should pack the wound with cotton soaked in caustic ointment and leave it until it eats away the remaining fat, then treat it until it heals.
Chapter forty-eight.
On the treatment of tumors that appear under the armpit.
These tumors that appear under the armpit are a kind of hard, cystic swellings, and some contain fluids.  Those that contain fluids should be incised with a crescent-shaped incision in this manner.
From line B to line C.  If all the fluid in the tumor is evacuated, pack it with old cotton and leave it until the next day, then remove the cotton.
And treat it with a suitable ointment until it heals. If the tumor is a kind of nodular swelling, incise it as we mentioned in the incision of swellings. If the wound progresses to the point where pus forms, cauterize it as we previously mentioned.
Chapter forty-nine.
On the incision of a tumor that appears near an artery or vein, called anorisma.
If an artery is injured and the skin over it heals, a tumor often results from this; similarly, a vein may also develop a swelling and tumor.
The signs by which one knows whether the tumor and swelling are from an artery or from a vein are: if the tumor is from an artery, it is oblong and deep-seated in the body; and if you press the tumor with your finger, you feel as if it has a whirring sound.
That which is from a vein, the tumor is round on the surface of the body, and incision of these tumors is dangerous, especially those in the armpit, groin, and neck, and in many other places of the body where they are very large; therefore, one should avoid treating them with iron.
And those that are also in the extremities or in the head should be avoided.  As for those that are a swelling of the mouth of an artery, incise it in the skin with a longitudinal incision, then open the incision with hooks, then peel away the artery and free it from the membranes.
Until it is exposed, then insert a needle under it and pass it to the other side, and tie the artery with a doubled thread in two places, as I have taught you in the ligation of the two arteries in the temples; then incise with a scalpel the area between the ligatures until all the blood in it comes out.
Then the tumor will resolve, and then use treatment that produces pus until the ligatures fall off, then treat it with appropriate ointments until it heals.
If the tumor is from a severed vein, you should grasp with your hand as much of the tumor as possible together with the skin, then insert a needle below the place you grasped with your hand and pass it through with a doubled thread until you bring it out on the other side. Then tie the tumor tightly with it, according to what I have described to you in tying the uvula from both sides with the ends of the thread. If you fear that the threads will slip, then insert another needle with another thread under the entire tumor at the intersection of the first needle.
And tighten your threads in four places, then incise the tumor in its middle until what is in it comes out, then cut off the excess skin and leave what was tied, then apply a dressing that you have soaked in a potion and oil. Then use treatment with cauterization and ointments until it heals.
Chapter Fifty.
On the tumor that occurs from the twisting of a nerve.
Just as a tumor occurs in an artery and vein, so too does a tumor occur in a nerve if a blow occurs in it or it occurs from excessive fatigue or the like.  This is most common in the wrists and heels.
And in every place where the joints move, it is a soft tumor, its color resembling the rest of the body. It is, in most cases, painless.  If pressed hard, the patient feels something like numbness. The tumor is not concentrated deep in the body.
Rather, it is under the skin and moves in every direction, but not forward or backward. As for those that are in the joints, they should not be approached with iron, for it may cause gangrene. As for those that are in the head or forehead,
Incise the skin with a scalpel. If the tumor is small, grasp it with forceps and cut it from the root. If it is large, hang it with a hook and flay it, then remove it, and close the wound with sutures and treat it until it heals.
Chapter Fifty-One.
On the excision of warts which appear in the abdomen.
Many people develop warts on their abdomens and other parts of their bodies; these are called fungal warts because of their resemblance to mushrooms. Their base is thin and their head is thick, their edges sometimes turn inwards; some are small and some grow very large.
I once saw a man who had two warts on his abdomen; they resembled mushrooms, white and thin at the base, their edges turned inwards and cracked, and moisture constantly flowed from them. I cut them off; one weighed about eighteen ounces, and the other about six.
In cutting them, you should observe that if the patient is moist and the wart is white, moist, and thin at the base, cut it with a wide scalpel, and have cautery irons ready in the fire, for often much blood flows when they are cut.
If the bleeding is excessive, cauterize it immediately. If you find the patient cowardly and afraid of cutting with an iron, take a strong lead thread and tie it tightly around the wart of this kind, and leave it for two days, then increase the tightness of the lead, continuing to tighten it.
Tighten it repeatedly until it affects the wart, so that it is severed and falls off by itself without effort. If the wart has a thick base, there is some danger and uncertainty in cutting it, especially if it is in the abdomen; however, it is possible to cut off half of it or some of it.
Then cauterize it so that it does not return, and then treat it until the wound heals. Beware of cutting a wart that is dark in color, insensitive, and foul-looking, for it is a cancerous tumor, and we shall discuss cancer later.
Chapter fifty-two.
In the treatment of umbilical hernia.
An umbilical hernia may result from many causes: either from a rupture of the peritoneum of the abdomen, from which fat or intestine protrudes, as occurs in other hernias; or from blood emitted from a vein or artery, as previously mentioned; or from wind that is contained within it.
If it is due to a rupture of the peritoneum and protrusion of fat, then the color of the swelling will resemble that of the body, it will be soft without pain, and it will appear in various positions. And if it is due to the protrusion of the intestine, its position will be more variable than what we have described.  If you press it with your fingers, it disappears and then returns, and there may be gurgling. It increases greatly upon entering a bath and with severe exertion. If it is due to moisture, then it will be soft.
It will not disappear if you press it with your hand, nor will it increase or decrease. If it is due to blood, then along with these signs, the swelling will appear dark. If it is due to growing flesh, the swelling will be firm, hard, and remain at a constant size.
If it is due to wind, it will be soft to the touch. The procedure in this case is that you should observe: if the umbilical hernia is due to blood from an artery or vein or to wind, then you should refrain from treating it, for there is danger and deception in that, as I have informed you in the chapter where I mentioned the swellings that occur from arteries and veins.
If the umbilical hernia is due to the intestine or fat, then you should instruct the patient to restrain himself and stand upright and extended, then you should draw a line with ink around the entire umbilicus. Then you should order him to lie on his back before you. Then you should incise with a wide scalpel around the umbilicus at the place marked with ink. Then you extend the center of the swelling upwards with a large probe, and then you tie the incised area with a strong thread or silk string, tightly.
The knot of the ligature should be a slip-knot. Then open the center of the constricted swelling above the ligature and insert your index finger. Search for the intestine; if you find that the ligature has seized it, loosen the slip-knot and push the intestine into the abdomen. If you find fat, extend it with a probe.
And cut away its excess. If an artery or vein obstructs you, ligate it carefully and return to your work. Take two needles and thread them with strong threads, and insert the needles into the incision that you made around the swelling, crosswise, passing them through. Then tighten the swelling in four places on the needles.
And if you wish, remove the needles and leave the area until the constricted flesh decays and falls off by itself, or cut it if it softens and decays. Then treat it with the appropriate ointments until it heals. If the umbilical hernia is from flesh that has grown there,
or from moisture, then it is necessary to incise the swelling as we said and evacuate the blood or moisture that you find in it, then treat it with what will close the wound.
Chapter Fifty-Three.
On the treatment of cancer.
We have mentioned in the classification the types of cancer and how to treat it with medications and the warning against treating it with iron lest it ulcerates, and we have mentioned the cancer that develops in the uterus and the warning against treating it.
And the ancients mentioned that when the cancer is in a place that can be completely excised, like the cancer that is in the breast or in the thigh and similar easily removed parts, especially if it is small and incipient, but if it has progressed and become large, then it should not be approached.
For I have not been able to cure anyone of it, nor have I seen anyone before me achieve that, and the procedure, if it is possible as we said, is to first purge the patient with black hellebore several times, then bleed him if there is clear fullness in the veins.
Then you position the patient in a position that allows you to work, then you apply to the cancer the appropriate hooks, then you scrape it from every side along with the skin completely until nothing of its roots remains, and you leave the blood to flow and do not stop it quickly.
Rather, squeeze the areas and draw out all the thick blood with your hand or with whatever instruments you can, and if you encounter in the procedure profuse bleeding from a severed artery or vein, then cauterize the vein until the bleeding stops, then treat it with the rest of the treatment until it heals.
Chapter fifty-four.
On the treatment of fistula.
We have already described in the classification the types of dropsy, how the accumulation of water occurs, and the signs of each type and its treatment with medication.  That which is treated with cautery is only the ascitic type alone, and the tympanitic type and the fleshy type are not at all approached with cautery.
For that is fatal, if you have treated this type of ascitic fistula with medications and your treatment has not been effective, then look, for if the patient is extremely weak, or if he has another illness besides the fistula, such as...
cough or diarrhea and the like, then beware of treating him with cautery, for that is perilous. But if you see that the patient is strong, without illness other than the fistula alone, and he is neither a child nor an old man, then the procedure is to have the patient stand before you. and an attendant behind him to press his belly with his hands and push the water downwards towards the pubis, then take a lancet in this manner:
pointed at both ends, long, with a sharp point like a lancet, except that it has a slight curve so that it does not go beyond the intestine during the procedure and injure it. Then look, if the fistula originates from the intestines.
Then it is necessary to make the incision three fingers' breadth below the navel, directly above the pubis. If the fistula originated from a liver disease, let your incision be three fingers' breadth to the left of the navel; if it originated from a spleen disease, let the incision be three fingers' breadth on the right side.
Also, the incision should not be on the side on which the patient wishes to lie down, lest the fluids flow to that weak place. Then pierce the whole skin with the instrument, then insert the instrument into the incision and raise your hand.
with the lancet between the skin and the peritoneum, as if you are flaying it, and the amount you flay should be about the size of a fingernail or so. Then pierce the peritoneum until the lancet reaches an empty place, which is the location of the water, then remove it and insert into the hole the instrument which is of this form.
It is like a small tube made of silver, or copper, or smooth, polished lead.  It has a small hole at its lower end and three holes in its sides; two on one side and one on the other, as you see.
And one may sharpen its end in the shape of a pen nib, as in this image:.
At its upper end is a ring.  If the instrument reaches the water, then it will immediately flow down the instrument and you will evacuate a moderate amount of water at a time. For if you evacuate more than is necessary at one time, the patient may die from the dissipation of his vital spirit.
Or he may experience a faintness close to death. But evacuate according to his strength and what the patient's condition indicates to you of the strength of his pulse and the good color of his complexion. Then remove the instrument and the water will be contained, for it will be contained immediately.
Because of the skin that holds the hole in the peritoneum, which I told you to suture in that manner. Then return the instrument another day if you see that the patient can tolerate it.
And you will also remove a small amount of water.  Do this for several days, while you are watchful and cautious against error, until only a small amount of water remains. If you fear for the patient and leave a large amount of water, then treat him by burying him in hot sand.
And profuse sweating in the bath and sun, and enduring thirst, and treat him with drying medicines until he recovers.  And cauterize the stomach, liver, and spleen after removing the water as I described previously.
Chapter Fifty-five.
In the treatment of children who are born with their urinary passages unperforated, or with a narrow perforation, or with the perforation in the wrong place.
Some boys may be born with their urethra imperforate; it is necessary to perforate it immediately upon birth.
With a very fine scalpel, in this manner:
Then place a thin lead nail in the perforation, and bind it and keep it in place for three or four days. When he wants to urinate, remove it, and afterwards replace it.  If you do not use the lead, it is possible that the urine passing through the place will not allow it to close.
As for him whose perforation is narrow, treat it with lead as we have said for many days until it widens.  And those whose perforation is in the wrong place—that is, some are born with the perforation at the end of the penis and are unable to urinate forward.
so that he lifts the urethra upwards by hand, and is not born before because the semen is unable to reach the uterus in a straight line, and this is a very ugly condition. The method of treatment in this case is that the patient lies on his back.
Then extend his penis with your left hand with a strong pull, and incise the glans penis with a sharp blade or scalpel, like a pen-knife, or as if you are carving something so that its middle is prominent, like a penis, and the perforation is in the middle as it should be.
And be careful during your work not to cause bleeding, for that often happens; so stop the bleeding with what cuts it off, and treat the wound until it heals.
Chapter fifty-six.
On the ulcer that appears in the foreskin and penis, and its blackness, corruption, and adhesion of the foreskin to the penis.
This ulcer often appears in the urethra; it is a fleshy, rough growth, and there is benign and malignant.  The benign one should be tied off with a fine ligature.
And cut it until you remove it all, then apply a cotton swab soaked in Egyptian ointment, then treat it afterwards with date-palm ointment until it heals. But if the ulcer is malignant and dark in color, you should cauterize it after cutting and removing it.
If the ulcer is in the foreskin of an uncircumcised man, and part of the ulcer is inside the foreskin and part outside, then you should remove the ulcer inside first, until it heals, then treat the outside.
Because if you treat both together, you risk perforation of the foreskin.  It may also appear in women and in the foreskin, blackness and corruption; then you should excise everything that is black and tending towards or already corrupted.
Then apply honey mixed with crushed and sifted pomegranate peel and  cress; then treat it with other treatments until it heals. If bleeding occurs, use cauterization with a crescent-shaped cautery of this form:
Cauterization is beneficial for both conditions, I mean bleeding and a wound if it becomes corrupted. If the cautery is consumed and disappears completely at some point, then you should insert a lead tube into the urethra so that the patient can urinate through it. As for the adhesion of the foreskin to the cautery...
This adhesion only happens in those whose foreskin is intact and who have not been circumcised.  It may also occur from a wound or swelling. Then you should incise it with a blunt scalpel until the bond is broken and the cautery is freed from all sides.
If it is difficult to distinguish them on close inspection, then you should incise a little of the cautery and of the foreskin.  Because the foreskin is thin, it may perforate quickly due to its thinness. Then separate the foreskin from the cautery with a thin linen cloth soaked in cold water, lest it adhere again.
Then treat it with an astringent drink until it heals.
Chapter fifty-seven.
On the cleansing of boys and the treatment of what may befall them of ailments.
Circumcision is nothing other than the separation of a connection like other wounds, except that since it is done by our will and especially used in boys, it is necessary that we describe the best procedure in it.
And the easiest path leading to safety, so I say that the ancients did not mention circumcision in any of their books because it was not used in their laws, but rather it is what we have acquired.
by experience, and that is that I found the majority of surgeons and barbers using purification with the scalpel and with scissors, and they use the cautery and ligature with thread and cutting with the fingernail. I have tried all these methods.
So I found nothing better than purification with scissors and ligature with thread, because purification with the scalpel often causes the skin to retract, because the skin of the foreskin is two layers, so perhaps the upper skin is cut and the lower layer remains.
so it is necessary to cut again and renewed pain, and cauterization does not prevent cutting the tip of the urethra because it might enter its hole, and as for purification with the fingernail, the skin might slip and your work is spoiled or the boy's skin is naturally short...
so they are often born like that and do not need purification, and I have seen that, and as for purification with scissors and ligature with thread, experience revealed its advantage to me because the cut of the scissors is proportionate, because the blade above is like the blade below.
so when you squeeze your hand with the proportion of the two blades, you cut with one measure and in one time, so the ligature becomes like a wall for the skin of the urethra from all sides, with no mistake at all, and the way of working is first to reassure the boy.
especially if he understands a little that you only tie the thread in his urethra and leave him until another day, then rejoice him and please him in every way you can, and what he accepts with his mind, then stop him before you standing upright.
and he should not be sitting, and hide the scissors in your sleeve or under your foot, so that the boy's eye does not fall upon it at all, nor on anything of the instruments, then you insert your hand into his urethra and inflate the skin and lift it up until the head of the urethra comes out.
Then you clean it from what gathers in it of dirt, then tie the marked place with a doubled thread, then tie a little below it a second ligature, then hold with your thumb and forefinger the place of the lower ligature a good hold and cut between the two ligatures. Then quickly lift the skin upwards and bring out the head of the urethra, then let the blood flow a little, for it is better and less swelling of the urethra, then dry it with a damp cloth, then sprinkle on it some ash of burnt dry gourd, for it is the best I have tried, or fine barley flour, for it is also excellent.
Then quickly lift the skin upwards and bring out the head of the urethra, then let the blood flow a little, for it is better and less swelling of the urethra, then dry it with a damp cloth, then sprinkle on it some ash of burnt dry gourd, for it is the best I have tried, or fine barley flour, for it is also excellent. Then apply on the sores from above in a piece of white cooked egg cloth cooked in rose water, beaten with fresh fragrant rose oil, and leave it on it until another day, then treat it with the rest of the treatment until it heals.
And this is the image of the scissors which are suitable for circumcision:
It should be sharp, cutting, not crooked, the nail smooth, and the length of the two blades should be equal to the length of the two handles. As for the mistake that occurs in circumcision, perhaps the entire inner skin or part of it is turned over during cutting, so you should stretch it with your fingernail before the place swells.
And cut it evenly; if you cannot hold it with your fingernail, then pull it with a thread and cut it. If three days pass and what is under the head of the urethra remains swollen and inflamed, then leave it until the acute inflammation subsides and then peel it gently.
And cut it as it suits you, and be careful of the head of the urethra, for if something of the head of the urethra is cut, it does not harm, so treat it with what heals the wound from the sores which we described in the chapter on sores.
And if some of the skin is cut above the amount and it retracts upwards, that also does not harm much, so treat it as we mentioned until it heals.
Chapter fifty-eight.
On the treatment of urine retained in the bladder.
Urine retained in the bladder is due to its obstruction by a stone, or clotted blood, or pus, or a growth of flesh, and the like.  If you treat that with what we mentioned of the types of treatment described in the classification.
And if the urine does not flow, and you see that its retention is due to a stone which has lodged in the neck of the bladder, then it is necessary that the patient be made to sit on his knees, then let a man mount his back and extend his abdomen towards his back, and let the patient restrain himself as much as he can.
Then the neck of the bladder will be forced to push the stone backwards, and the urine will flow.  If it does not flow by what we have mentioned, and the matter becomes severe for the patient, then it is necessary that you use its extraction with the instrument called a catheter, whose form is this:
It is made of silver, and it is thin, smooth, hollow like a bird's quill in thinness, long about a span and a half, with a gentle funnel at its head.  The way to draw the urine with it is to take a doubled thread and tie to its end wool or cotton tightly.
And insert the end of the thread into the lower part of the catheter and cut with scissors if any of the wool is in excess, so that it enters the tube and closes it like a button.  Then anoint the catheter with oil or butter or egg white, and seat the patient on a chair.
And cleanse his bladder and penis with moist ointments or oil and lukewarm water. Then gently insert the catheter into the urethra until it reaches the root of the urethra, then bend the urethra upwards towards the navel, then push the catheter inside it.
Until, when the urethra is completely bent, then push the catheter inside until, when it reaches near the rectum, incline the penis downwards with the catheter inside it, then push it until it reaches the bladder, and the patient feels that it has reached something empty.
This is done in this manner because the passage through which the urine passes is tortuous. Then pull the thread with the wool gently; then the urine will follow the wool. Then remove it, and the urine will flow out. Then replace the catheter.
Continue to do this until the bladder is emptied and the patient feels relief.
Chapter fifty-nine.
How to inject the bladder with a syringe.
And the instruments suitable for this are made of silver or hollow ivory, having a long tube as fine as pus.  And you wish to instill waters and medicines into it, which is done with an instrument called a syringe.  This is its image:
It is made of silver or hollow ivory, having a long tube as fine as a millet stalk, hollow throughout except for the tip, which is solid and has three holes, two on one side and one on the other, as you see.
And the hollow part in which the propellant is, is only as large as to contain it without excess, so that when you draw up something of the moisture, it is drawn up, and when you push it, it is pushed further, as the bellows does which throws oil in naval battles.
Then if you wish to place moisture in the bladder, you insert the tip of the syringe into the moisture and draw upwards with the piston; then the moisture is drawn into the body of the syringe. Then you insert its tip into the urethra as we described in the catheters, then you push the moisture with the piston.
Then that moisture reaches the bladder in the same place until the patient feels it. And this is the image of a gentle clyster also with which the bladder is injected:
It is made of silver or lead, its upper end resembling a small funnel, and below it a groove in which the ligature lies. Then take a camel's bladder and put in it the liquid you wish to inject into the bladder, then tie it tightly between the two grooves with a doubled thread.
And warm that liquid a little over the fire, then insert the tip of the clyster into the urethra, then press your hand tightly on the bladder with the liquid until the patient feels that that liquid has reached the bladder.
If you do not have a bladder, take a piece of thin leather and make a circle from it and pierce it all around. Then insert a strong thread into those holes as they are arranged, then gather the circle as you gather a bag after you have put in it what you want of liquids, ointments and water.
Then tie it to the instrument and do with it as you did with the bladder, pressing with your hand until the liquid reaches the bladder.
Chapter Sixty.
On the extraction of the stone.
We have mentioned in the classification the types of stones and their treatment with medicines, and mentioned the difference between the stone generated in the kidneys and the stone generated in the bladder, and the signs of all that, and mentioned the stone in which action by hand and incision is necessary.
This is the stone generated specifically in the bladder and that which becomes fixed in the urethra, and I am describing that with clear explanation and brevity.
So I say that the stone generated in the bladder most often affects boys, and among its signs is that the urine exits the bladder similar to water in its thinness.
And sand appears in it, and he scratches his penis and plays with it, and often it hangs down, then spreads, and the rectum protrudes with it in many of them.  It is easy to remove from boys until they reach fourteen years, and difficult in old men; as for young men, it is intermediate between the two.
And the one whose stone is larger, his treatment is easier, and the small one is the opposite.  So if we come to treatment, it is necessary first that the patient be given an enema to remove all the feces in his intestines, for it may prevent the finding of the stone upon examination.
Then the patient is taken by his feet, and he is shaken and jolted downwards so that the stone descends to the neck of the bladder, or he jumps from a high place repeatedly. Then you seat him between your hands upright, and his hands under his thighs, so that the whole bladder inclines downwards, then you examine him.
And palpate him externally. If you feel the stone in the space, act immediately to incise upon it. If it does not fall under your touch at all, then it is necessary that you lubricate the index finger with ointment from the left hand if the patient is a boy, or the middle finger.
If he is a mature boy, then insert it into his rectum and search for the stone, until when it falls under your finger, move it little by little to the neck of the bladder. Then press upon it with your finger and push it outwards towards the place you want to incise.
And you order a servant to compress the bladder with his hand, and you order another servant to stretch with his right hand the labia upwards, and with his other hand to stretch the skin under the labia away from the place where the incision will be. Then take the sharp scalpel, whose picture is this:
And incise between the rectum and the labia, not in the middle, but towards the left side of the buttock.  The incision should be upon the stone itself, with your finger in the rectum, and you pressing it outwards obliquely, so that the incision is wide on the outside and narrow on the inside, as much as is possible for the stone to exit from it.
Not larger, for perhaps the finger which is in the rectum will press the stone during the incision, and it will come out without difficulty. And know that there may be stones which have angles and edges, and therefore their exit is difficult.
And some are smooth, resembling an acorn and round, so their exit is easy. But those which have angles and edges, increase the incision a little.  If it does not come out like this, then it is necessary to devise a way for it, either by grasping it with a firm forceps whose end is like a rasp.
To hold the stone firmly so that it does not escape from it, or to insert under it a delicate instrument with a hooked end.  If you cannot manage it in this way, widen the incision a little. If you are overcome by blood, stanch it with alum.
If there is more than one, push the largest first towards the opening of the bladder, then incise upon it, then push the smaller ones afterwards.  And do likewise if there are more than two. If it is very large, then it is folly to make a large incision upon it, for this exposes the patient to one of two things.
Either he will die, or he will have a constant dribbling of urine because the place will not heal at all. But try to push it out, or devise a way to break it with forceps until you remove it in pieces.
When you have finished your work, dress the wound with mastic, aloes, and myrrh, and bind it. Place over it cloths moistened with oil and wine, or with rose oil and cold water, to calm the hot swelling. Then let the patient lie on his back, and do not loosen the bandage until the third day.
When it is loosened, wash the place with plenty of water and oil, then treat it with palm ointment and basil ointment until it heals. If excessive hot swelling, inflammation, or the like occurs in the wound, such as blood clotting in the bladder and preventing the passage of urine—which is known from the passage of blood with urine—
then insert your finger into the wound and remove that blood; for if it remains, it will lead to corruption and putrefaction of the bladder. Then wash the wound with vinegar, water, and salt, and treat each type with its appropriate treatment until it heals.
In all these treatments, it is necessary to bind and draw together the thighs to secure the medicines applied to the place. If the stone is small and has lodged in the passage of the penis, preventing the passage of urine, treat it as I have described before it necessitates incision.
Often I have dispensed with incision by this treatment, which I have tested; it is that you take a slender instrument of steel, which is of this form:
The end triangular, sharp, and fixed in a shaft; then take a thread and tie it around the penis below the stone, lest it return to the bladder; then gently insert the slender instrument into the urethra until the instrument reaches the stone itself.
And turn the instrument in the stone itself little by little, intending to pierce it until you pass through to the other side; then the urine will flow immediately. Then press with your hand on what remains of the stone from outside the penis, for it will crumble and come out with the urine, and the patient will recover.
If this treatment is not feasible for you due to some impediment, then tie a thread below the stone and another thread above the stone, then incise over the stone in the penis itself between the two ligatures, and then remove it.
Then untie the ligature and clean away the clotted blood which has formed in the wound.  The reason for tying the thread below the stone is to prevent it from returning to the bladder, and the other ligature above is so that when the thread is untied after removal of the stone, the skin returns to its place and covers the wound.
Therefore, when you tie the upper thread, you should raise the skin upwards so that it returns and covers the wound as we have said.
Chapter sixty-one.
On the removal of stones in women.
Stones are rarely generated in women; but if one of them should suffer from a stone, its treatment is difficult and impossible for many reasons. One is that the woman may be a virgin, and another that you will not find a woman who will allow herself to be examined by a physician.
If she is chaste or one of her relatives, and the third is that you will not find a woman skilled in this craft, especially the manual work, and the fourth is that the incision site in women is far from the stone, requiring a deep incision.
And in that there is danger. If necessity demands it, then a skilled female physician should be sought, but they are rarely found. If you cannot find one, then seek a chaste and gentle male physician, or engage a skilled midwife experienced in women's matters, or a woman who has some knowledge of this craft.
Then have her present and instruct her to do all that you order her to do, first examining for the stone.  That is, if the woman is a virgin, she should insert a finger into her rectum and search for the stone. If she finds it and presses it under her finger,
then instruct her to incise it. If she is not a virgin but a married woman, instruct the midwife to insert her finger into the patient's vagina and search for the stone after placing her left hand on the bladder and pressing it firmly.
If she finds it, she should push it from the mouth of the bladder downwards as far as she can, until she reaches the groin, then incise at the point opposite the middle of the vulva at the groin, whichever side she can reach and feels the stone on that side.
Her finger should not leave the stone, pressing it beneath. The incision should be small at first, then introduce the dilator into that small incision. If she feels the stone, enlarge the incision to the extent that she knows the stone will come out. And know that there are many types of stones.
Some are small, some large, some smooth, some rough, some long, some round, some branched.  Know their types so that you may thereby know what you seek. If the bleeding overcomes you, sprinkle powdered verdigris on the area and hold it for an hour until the bleeding stops, then return to your work.
until the stone comes out.  Prepare yourself with the instruments I mentioned for removing stones from men, to assist you in your work. If bleeding overcomes you and you know from the pulse of the blood that it is from a severed artery, apply styptics to the area.
Bind it tightly with bandages and leave it; do not disturb it, and leave the stone; do not remove it, for the patient might die.  Then treat the wound.  When the severity of the bleeding has subsided after a few days and the area has become infected, return to your work until you remove the stone.
Chapter sixty-two.
On the incision upon the watery bladder.
The watery bladder is an accumulation of moisture in the white peritoneum which is under the skin of the scrotum surrounding the testicle, and it is called the scrotum. It may be in a special membrane prepared for it by nature in a part of the testicle.
So that one thinks it is another testicle. It is between the skin of the scrotum and the white peritoneum that we mentioned, but this is rare. This bladder is generated from weakness that affects the testicles, so this matter settles there, and it may result from a blow to the testicles.
And this moisture is of many colors; sometimes it is yellowish, sometimes bloody red, sometimes black and purulent, and sometimes white and watery, which is most frequent. The signs by which it is known are:
Where the water collects, if it is in the white peritoneum that we mentioned, then the swelling is round, slightly elongated like the shape of an egg, and the testicle does not appear because the moisture surrounds it from all sides.
And if the moisture is in a special membrane for it, then the swelling is round on one side of the testicle.
Therefore, a person imagines that it is another testicle. If the moisture is between the skin of the scrotum and the white peritoneum, then it is palpable. But if you wish to know the color of the moisture, probe the swelling with the square probe whose image we have presented; whatever comes out in the wake of the probe, you judge it according to what is inside.
If we proceed to treatment with the cautery, then you should order the patient to be bled if he can tolerate it and you see that his body is full.  Then he should lie on his back on something slightly raised, and you place many cloths under him. Then you sit on his left.
And you order a servant to sit on his right, stretching his penis to one side of the scrotum's skin and toward the groin. Then you take a wide scalpel and incise the scrotum's skin lengthwise in the middle to near the pubis.
And make the incision straight, parallel to the line that divides the scrotum's skin in half, until you reach the white peritoneum which contains [the testicle]; then peel it back and be careful not to tear it. Your peeling should be from the side that adheres to the testicle more, and complete the peeling as much as you can.
Then you widely incise the peritoneum full of water and drain all the water. Then you separate the edges of the incision with hooks and stretch the peritoneum upwards, without touching the containing scrotum skin, and cut the peritoneum however you can cut it, either all at once or piece by piece.
Especially its thin side, for if you do not completely cut it, you will not be safe from the water returning. If the testicle protrudes outside its skin during your work, then, once you have finished cutting the peritoneum, return it, then close the edges of the scrotum skin with sutures.
Then treat it like any other wound until it heals. If the testicle is already damaged by another disease, then you should ligate the vessels in the spermatic cord for fear of bleeding, then cut the testicle from the spermatic cord and remove the testicle, then treat it as we have described.
If the fluid is collected in both sides, then know that they are two hydroceles. Incise the other side in the same way as you did the first. If it is better for you to do the procedure at once, then do so. Then wipe away the blood.
And you insert into the incisions wool that you have dipped in oil or rose ointment, and you place on the outside another wool that you have dipped in a potion and oil, and spread it over the testicles and the flanks of the abdomen, and place folded cloths on top, which are the compresses. And you tie them on top with a bandage which has six ends, in this manner:
Then two cloths are taken, sewn together, and stuffed with carded wool in this manner.  The ties used to fasten it are made of cotton or wet wool, and are gathered tightly in the waistband of the trousers, and one end is taken to the buttocks and towards the back.
And the second is taken towards the pubis, and the two ends to the thighs below, and the remaining two ends to the groins; and all the tightening is gathered in the waistband of the trousers. And this hydrocele may also be treated by cauterization instead of incision with an iron.
It is that you take a small, sharp cautery iron and with it you incise the skin of the scrotum while it is hot, as we have described, until the white peritoneum containing the water is exposed. Then take another cautery iron, in this manner:
It resembles the Greek eye, then you cauterize that peritoneum with it while it is hot until all the moisture comes out. Then you stretch the peritoneum with hooks and flay the containing layer with the cautery and cut it as much as you can, until you excise all of it, and you preserve the testicle, so that the fire does not touch it.
And know that this procedure is safer and prevents more bleeding, and is better than the procedure with incision.  If you are confronted during the incision or during the cauterization by a hot swelling or bleeding or something else, then you should treat whatever confronts you with the treatment appropriate for it, as previously explained.
If the patient is cowardly and does not endure this procedure because of its unpleasantness, then let him use cauterization as I describe, if it is confirmed for you by what we have presented of evidence.
That the hydrocele is watery, then the patient should sit on a raised chair, then cauterize the swelling at its base with a wide scalpel, and make the cauterization along the length of the body, and the opening should be wide so that all the water flows out. Then place cotton on the place and bandage it.
And leave it to drain the remaining water, then treat it until the wound heals. If the exit of the water is hindered during your cauterization, and it is only because the tunica albuginea obstructs the mouth of the wound and prevents the exit of the water,
then it is necessary to insert into the wound a goose feather or the instrument with which the water of hydroceles is extracted, the image of which has preceded, or enlarge the opening of the wound a little.
And know that the water may return and collect after six months or so. If it collects, cauterize it in the same manner, and the patient should protect himself during those days.
Chapter Sixty-Three.
Concerning the incision on the fleshy membrane and its treatment.
Know that an incision on this membrane is one of the dangerous things leading to death in most instances; therefore, we consider leaving it alone and being safe from it, although I will mention the treatment and types of swelling.
I say that many swellings may occur in the bodies which consist of the testicles, and this is due to many causes, either from a sharp humor that descends to the testicles, or from a blow; and the color of the swelling is the color of the body, and it contains no water.
And the swelling may be soft, and perhaps the swelling may be hardened, its color dark, and it has no sensation.  It may be from the entanglement and swelling of the arteries, as has been previously mentioned, or from the swelling of the veins.  That which is from the swelling of the arteries is known by the dispersion of the swelling.
If you press it with your fingers, it should not be dispersed at all. As for that which is from the swelling of the veins, nothing of it disperses when pressed with the fingers, and this type may possibly be incised, as with other flesh.
And that is to incise the skin of the testicle, then extend the testicle upward and remove it from the white peritoneum, and free the spermatic cord from the vessels, and ligate the vessels, and cut the spermatic cord after freeing it from every part of the testicle, if the testicle has adhered to that growing flesh.
Then it is necessary to remove the testicle and cut it. And if the adhesion is between some of the peritonea or between the vessels, then it is necessary to free all that adhesion and cut it with a circular incision.
If the growth of flesh is in the place of adhesion which is behind, then it is necessary to cut it all and remove the testicle as we said.  When your work is completed, dress the wound with wool soaked in rose oil and syrup, then treat it with other treatments until it heals.
Chapter sixty-four.
In the treatment of hydrocele with a hernia.
A hernia is a twisted tumor, somewhat twisted, resembling a bunch of grapes, with relaxation of the testicles and difficulty for the patient in movement, exercise, and walking. The treatment of this disease is similar to what has been mentioned before.
However, it is necessary to mention the procedure as the ancients did. I say that it is necessary to seat the patient on a high chair, then push the suspensory ligament of the testicles downwards, then grasp the skin of the testicle with your fingers along with the vessels which are near the penis.
And let your assistant hold it and stretch it tightly, then you incise with a wide, sharp scalpel an oblique incision along the vessels until the vessels are exposed, then dissect from each side as I described to you in the dissection of the arteries in the armpits, then insert a needle with a doubled thread.
And tie it at the first place where the hernia appeared and tie it also at its end, then incise it in the middle with a vertical incision along the body and remove what has gathered inside of it from the foul, putrid fluids.
Then treat the wound with the treatment of all other wounds so that a period of time elapses for the vessels that nourish one of the testicles to fall away, there is no harm in that.  If the hernia involves all the vessels, then it is necessary to remove one of the testicles with the vessels lest the testicle lack nourishment. Before cutting the vessels, they will wither and be of no use.
Chapter Sixty-five.
In the treatment of intestinal hernia.
This hernia occurs from a tear that appears in the peritoneum extending over the abdomen towards the groins from the abdominal cavity, and the intestine descends from that rupture into one of the testicles.
And this rupture is either from a tear in the peritoneum or from its distension.  These two types occur from many causes, either a blow, a jump, a shout, lifting something heavy, and the like.  Its sign, if it is from a distension of the peritoneum, is that it occurs little by little over a long time.
It does not occur suddenly, and the swelling is even towards the depth because the peritoneum constricts the intestine.  Its sign, if it is from a tear in the peritoneum, is that it initially causes great pain suddenly, and the swelling is uneven, appearing under the skin nearby.
That is by the exit of the intestine and its passage outside of the peritoneum.  And the omentum may exit with the intestine, and this hernia is called intestinal and omental.  It may be with gas. And the feces may pass into the intestine and be retained there, resulting in the death of the patient.
Because it causes severe pain and gurgling, especially if constricted.  Treatment of these types of illness with cautery is dangerous, so it should be avoided.  The method of treatment is to order the patient to return the intestine with his hand to the inside of his abdomen if it is possible to return it.
Then he lies on his back in your hands and raises his legs. Then you stretch the skin next to the groin upwards and incise the skin of the scrotum entirely lengthwise. Then you insert hooks into the edges of the incision as much as is needed to open the incision with them.
And the incision should be such that the testicle can come out of it. Then you dissect the membranes under the skin of the scrotum until the white, hard peritoneum is exposed on every side. Then insert your index finger into what is next to the testicle.
Between the white peritoneum which is under the skin of the testicle and the second peritoneum, and you loosen the adhesion which is behind the testicle. Then you pull with the right hand into the scrotum's skin and with this you stretch the white peritoneum upward with the left hand and lift the testicle with the peritoneum towards the incision.
And you order the assistant to pull the testicle upward, and you yourself completely loosen the adhesion from behind.  And you search with your finger lest there be something of the twisted intestine in the hard white peritoneum; and if you find some, push it down into the abdomen.
Then you take a needle with a thick thread, twisted from ten threads, and insert it at the end of the peritoneum under the scrotum's skin which is next to the incision. Then you cut the ends of the thread's fold until there are four threads. Then you place some on top of others in a firm manner and tie the peritoneum—which we said is under the scrotum’s skin—with it tightly on two sides. Then you also twist the ends of the threads and tie them tightly so that nothing from the vessels which nourish it can reach it. Lest a hot swelling result from that, and you make also a second ligature outside the first ligature, distant from it less than two fingers. And after these two ligatures you leave of the peritoneum which is under the skin of the scrotum the amount of a finger bone.
Lest a hot swelling result from that, and you make also a second ligature outside the first ligature, distant from it less than two fingers. And after these two ligatures you leave of the peritoneum which is under the skin of the scrotum the amount of a finger bone. And you cut all the rest in a circle and remove the testicle with it. Then you make an incision below the scrotum's skin so that blood and pus flow out as we described before. Then use wool soaked in oil and place it in the wound, and use the ligature that we described.
And the white peritoneum, which we said, may be cauterized after cutting it, cautiously against bleeding, for that often happens.  And leave the ligature until it falls off by itself; and if it is slow to fall off, then loosen it with warm water and apply something that sweats it until it falls.
Then treat the wound with all the treatments of wounds until it heals.
Chapter sixty-six.
Concerning the windy hernia.  I have not seen anyone dare to treat this windy hernia with iron. The ancients mentioned that it should be done as we described in the hernia with a stalk; that is, that the vessels should be gently tied below after the incision upon it.
Then incise in the middle and treat the tumor with that which opens it until the vessels fall and treat the wound as we mentioned until it heals.
Chapter sixty-seven.
Concerning the hernia which is in the groin.
A hernia may appear in the groin, as we said, so that the place swells, and nothing of the intestine descends into the scrotum; and if it descends, it is slight and returns at all times.
But if time elapses, the fissure in the peritoneum increases until the intestine and epiploon descend into the scrotum, and this occurs from the extension of the peritoneum which is in the groin, as we said. That is because the peritoneum extends, then relaxes and the place swells and protrudes, and its treatment is by cauterization, as I have previously described.
And it may be treated with the cautery in this manner: the patient lies on his back between your hands, then you incise the swollen, protruding hernia transversely to the extent of three fingers, then you separate the peritoneal layers which are under the skin.
Until, when the white peritoneum which follows is exposed, you take a probe and place it on the protruding part of the peritoneum and push it into the depth of the abdomen, then you sew the two protruding parts of the peritoneum to the edge of the probe, and you fasten one to the other with the suture.
Then you withdraw the probe, and do not cut the peritoneum at all, nor touch the testicle nor anything else, as I have informed you in the treatment of intestinal hernia, then you treat it with the treatment of wounds.
Then if the threads are cut, I clean them and dress the wound until it heals, for the peritoneum contracts and the protrusion of the hernia does not increase.
And cauterization is better in this place because it is closer to safety.
Chapter sixty-eight.
On the relaxation of the skin of the scrotum.
Often the skin of the scrotum relaxes in some people without the flesh inside relaxing, and its appearance is unsightly.  He who wishes to treat it should lay the patient on his back.
And he cuts all the relaxed skin over the thick skin, then gathers the lips together. And if you wish, first sew the excess relaxed skin with three or four stitches, and make sure of the stitching, then cut between the stitches.
Then treat it with the treatment of other wounds until it heals and the stitches fall out.
Chapter sixty-nine.
On castration.
Castration is forbidden in our religion.
Therefore, I should not have mentioned it in this book. However, I mentioned it for two reasons: the first is so that the physician may know about it if he is asked, and so that he may know the treatment of someone who has undergone it; and the second is that we often need to castrate some animals for our benefit.
Such as lambs, goats, cats, and similar animals.  I say that castration is of two types: either by crushing or by incision and cutting.  As for that which is done by crushing, its method is that we seat the animal in hot water until its testicles relax, soften, and hang down.
Then crush them with your hand until they dissolve and are not discernible to the touch. As for castration by incision and cutting, you should hold the animal and squeeze the skin of its testicles with your left hand, then tie the cords and make one incision on each testicle.
Until the testicles emerge, then cut them after you have skinned them, and do not leave any of the membranes on them except the thin membrane that is on the vessels. This type of castration is better than that which is done by crushing.
Because crushing may leave something of the testicles, so the animal may still desire mating. Then treat the wound until it heals.
Chapter Seventy.
On the treatment of the hermaphrodite.
The hermaphrodite in men is of two types. One is that which appears in the area following the scrotum, or in the skin of the scrotum between the testicles, in a form like a woman's vulva, with hair. Urine may flow from that which is in the scrotum's skin.
As for women, there is one type. It is above the vulva, on the pubis, like small male genitals, protruding outward. One is like a man's penis, and the two are like testicles.  And the treatment of the three types.
The two types in men and the one type in women: it is necessary to cut away that excess flesh until its trace is removed, then treat it with the treatment of other wounds until it heals. As for the second type in men, from which urine flows, which is in the scrotum's skin, there is no action in it, nor is there any benefit from removing it.
Chapter seventy-one.
On the excision of the clitoris and the protruding flesh from the vulvas of women.
The clitoris sometimes exceeds the natural amount, so that it becomes large and ugly in appearance. And it may grow large in some women, even spreading like in men and leading to sexual intercourse.
Then you should grasp the excess of the clitoris with your hand or with a hook and cut it.  Do not exaggerate in the cutting, especially in the depth of the root, lest bleeding occurs.
Then treat it with the treatment of wounds until it heals. As for the growing flesh, it is flesh that grows in the mouth of the womb until it fills it.
And sometimes it protrudes outwards like a tail. Therefore, some of the ancients call it the "tail disease."  Then you should cut it as you cut the clitoris, and treat it until it heals.
Chapter Seventy-Two.
On the treatment of prolapse.
Prolapse is when a woman's vulva is not perforated, or the perforation is small. This may be congenital or acquired.  The acquired type results from a previously existing condition, and may be due to an excess of growing flesh.
or a thin or thick membrane. This may be located in the depth of the uterus, or in its sides, or above or below it. It prevents intercourse, conception, and childbirth, and sometimes menstruation.  This is determined by examination by the midwife.
If the obstruction is visible and near, then if it is not visible, she should examine it with her fingers or a probe. If the obstruction is a thin membrane and is close to the labia, then proceed to rupture it. This is done by placing something like pledgets on the labia, then placing the thumbs of both hands.
The woman lies on her back with her legs spread apart. Then, forcefully stretch the labia until the thin membrane is ruptured and the obstruction is opened. Then take wool, soak it in oil, and place it on the area. The woman should have intercourse every day so that the area does not close again.
If the membrane is thick and dense, it should be incised with a wide scalpel resembling an aspen leaf. If the obstruction is from excess flesh, hang it with hooks and cut it. You should have styptics at hand that do not sting, such as acacia, myrrh, and frankincense mixed with egg white.
Then use a wide lead tube so that the wound does not close quickly. Keep it in place for several days and use a dry linen wick. Then treat it with the usual healing treatments until it heals.  Another growth of flesh may appear in the uterus; this should be cut in the same way.
Unless it is a cancerous tumor.  A cancerous tumor in the uterus should never be treated with iron.
Chapter Seventy-Three.
On the treatment of hemorrhoids, warts, and red pustules that appear in the vulva of women.
As for hemorrhoids, it is a swelling of the mouths of the veins, so that much blood constantly flows from them. If hemorrhoids persist, they become warts. I have already mentioned their types and signs in the classification, and we will mention them here.
What accepts treatment and what does not accept treatment. I say that hemorrhoids and warts, if they are deep in the uterus and do not appear to the touch, have no treatment with iron. Those that are in the mouth of the uterus and can be felt are those that are treated.
It is necessary that the woman enter a cold house, then she should stretch the warts with a scraper or a rough cloth and cut them from their roots. Then she should apply to them, when the blood flows, one of the styptics that stops blood without cauterizing.
Such as acacia,  Sumac, frankincense and the like. Then she should raise her legs to the wall for an hour, then lower them into cold water. If the bleeding persists, seat her in a decoction of sumac, pomegranate peel, gallnut, and the like. Then take barley flour, knead it with honey and vinegar.
And apply it as a compress to her back. If the blood stops, otherwise, apply cupping to the loins and breasts without condition. Then take wool, dip it in the juice of the thorny plant, or the juice of Plantago, or the juice of the branches of the bindweed with a gallic syrup, and apply it to the place.
When the swelling subsides, treat the area with ointment until it heals. As for the red pimples, they resemble the heads of the intestines, rough in appearance. It is necessary to cut what appears of them, according to what I mentioned in the case of warts, and treat it until it heals.
Chapter Seventy-Four.
Concerning the suppuration which occurs in the uterus.
Many kinds of tumors may occur in the uterus, such as cancer, the hardened tumor, ulcers, fistulas, phagedena, fistulas, hemorrhoids, fissures, warts, and the hot tumor. I have already mentioned all these diseases, their types, and their symptoms in the classification.
It is necessary that we mention in this section the hot tumor which occurs in the uterus, if it is one of the tumors which collect for a period, how its suppuration is with iron. It is necessary to look; if the pain of the tumor is initially severe with throbbing, inflammation, and fever.
And if you see it red when touched, do not hasten to its suppuration, and treat it with what helps in ripening until these symptoms subside. Then it is necessary that the woman sit on a chair with two seats and lean on its back.
And she gathers her legs towards the lower abdomen, and her thighs are spread apart, and her arms are under her knees, and they are bound with a suitable bandage.
Therefore, then the midwife sits on the right side and uses the instrument with which the mouth of the uterus is opened, whose image comes later. It is necessary, if you want to use this instrument, to estimate with a probe the depth of the woman's uterus, lest what enters from the instrument be more than the depth of the uterus.
Then it harms the patient. If the instrument is more than the depth, then it is necessary to place pledgets on the lip of the cervix to prevent the entire instrument from entering the depth of the uterus.  And it is necessary that the spiral which runs in the instrument be from the upper side.
And you hold the instrument and the assistant turns the spiral until the cervix is opened. Then, if the abscess appears and is touched by the hand and is soft and thin, then it is necessary to incise it at its head with a wide scalpel.  Then, when all the matter is evacuated,
it is necessary to place in the wound a soft tent soaked in rose oil or green oil containing some astringent. And the tent is placed outside the incision in the cervix, and clean wool soaked in water in which alum has been boiled is placed externally on the uterus and on the pubis.
Then, it is treated after two days with the ointment which is suitable for that until it heals.
And the uterus and wound may be washed by injecting with water and honey, then with water in which liquorice root or long rhubarb has been boiled, then return to the ointment. If the abscess is hidden inside the uterus,
then it is necessary to refrain from treating it with iron, but it is treated with what we mentioned in the classification.
Chapter seventy-five.
In teaching midwives how to treat live fetuses if they emerge in an unnatural form.
The midwife should first know the form of natural childbirth.  Its signs are: if you see the woman straining downwards and yearning to breathe the air, and what she is suffering from childbirth becomes easier for her.
And the emergence of the fetus is hastened. Know that this birth is in the natural form, and its emergence is head first with the afterbirth with it or attached to its navel. If you see these signs, then it is necessary to press her abdomen so that the fetus emerges quickly.
For if it descends head first, the afterbirth descends with it and is cleansed of those impurities well. Whatever is besides this birth is unnatural and blameworthy, because the fetus may emerge on its feet, and its hands may emerge before its head and feet.
And one of its hands or one of its feet, or its head and hand may emerge, or it may emerge folded, and perhaps it may turn onto its back, and similar blameworthy forms.  It is necessary for the midwife to be skillful and gentle with all of these things and to beware of slips and mistakes.
