And I am explaining each form and how the method is in it so that you may be guided by that and understand it. If the fetus emerges head first, the natural emergence, and that is difficult for the woman and labor is difficult for her, and you see her strength has weakened, then seat her on a chair and order women to hold her.
And compress her womb with cooked fenugreek and with moist ointments, then the midwife inserts a small scalpel between her fingers and cuts the afterbirth with it, or cuts it with her fingernail until the moisture inside it flows out, and she presses the woman's abdomen until the fetus descends.
If it does not descend, then it is necessary to inject the woman with fenugreek saliva with shirāj ointment, then after the injection, order her to strain and make her sneeze with a sternutatory and hold her mouth and nose, for the fetus will emerge immediately. If the hands of the fetus emerge first, then it is necessary to return them gently little by little.
If they do not manage to enter, then place the woman on a stool and raise her legs upwards, then shake the stool on the ground and the woman holds on so that she does not fall when shaken. If the hands do not enter and the fetus dies, then cut them off and pull out the rest of the fetus.
Or tie his hands with a cloth and pull them moderately, for then it will come out. The emergence of the fetus on its feet: if the fetus emerges on its feet, then it is necessary to push them upwards, then turn the fetus little by little until you straighten it, then take one of its feet and extend it little by little.
If they come out to the thighs, then order her to strain and make her sneeze with a sternutatory, for then it will come out. If it does not come out as we described, otherwise return the fetus little by little until you put it in its natural form, for then it will come out easily. If it is prevented by all that we have described.
Then take some saliva of the *khatmi*, and saliva of fenugreek, and shirāj ointment, and dissolved gum, and pound everything well in a mortar, then apply it to the vulva of the woman and the lower part of her abdomen, then seat her in lukewarm water until it reaches the sharasif.
If you see that her lower parts have become soft, then make a pessary for her from myrrh and let her bear it. If the pessary is held for an hour, then seat her on a chair, then make her sneeze and close her mouth and nose, and gently press on her lower abdomen, for the fetus will come out immediately. The emergence of the fetus on its knees and hands:
If it comes out in this manner, then gradually introduce your hands, then let the woman lie on her back and hang her hips downwards while lying on a bed, and let her clasp her hands, then press gently on her abdomen. Then remove the pillows from under her legs so that her lower parts are raised. If the fetus does not come out, then take both her legs and move them vigorously, then press gently above the sharasif until the fetus ascends upwards, then the midwife inserts her hand and adjusts the fetus little by little.
And order the woman to strain until the fetus comes out. The emergence of the fetus sideways, hanging by one of its hands.  Try to return its hand; if you are not able to return it, then make the woman stand and make her walk. If she is not able to walk, then let her lie down.
on her bed and shake her legs vigorously. Then use the poultice which I described from the saliva. If labor pains seize her, then seat her on a chair, then treat to return the hand and adjust the fetus into its natural shape, and make the woman sneeze and order her to strain until it comes out.
The emergence of the fetus on its back, extending its hands, and its face towards its mother's back. The midwife should grasp the hand of the fetus, then turn it little by little and adjust it gently.  When it is straightened, apply to its head what we described of ointment and saliva.
Then tell her to move little by little. If she does that, then seat her on the chair and tell her to lean to the right side. Then tell her to strain and make her sneeze with the khandas, then it will come out.  The emergence of the fetus upright on its side.
If it descends to the mouth of the womb in this manner with the afterbirth, then tear the afterbirth with your fingernail until it is emptied of moisture. If it is attached to the face and neck of the fetus, then cut it from the navel lest it suffocate and die, then push the fetus inwards until it is as it should be.
And tell the woman to strain repeatedly, then it will come out easily. The emergence of twins or many fetuses. Know that twins frequently are born, and three or four may be born and live, although this is rare. As for five, it is something outside of nature; they will not live at all.
And they are surrounded, even if numerous, by one afterbirth, and they are separated by a membranous partition between them, attached to the navel of each one. And they may emerge like a single fetus, naturally and easily, and they may emerge in a reprehensible manner as we described.
If some of them are difficult at birth and become as we mentioned, in unnatural forms, then treat them as we described. Let the midwife be gentle and do what she does with gentleness and slowness, and she should contrive for every form with whatever means is available to her that leads to safety.
Mentioning what is conceived in the womb of fetuses that are aborted. One, two, three, four, five, six, seven, and more than ten may be conceived in the womb. It has been confirmed for me that a woman aborted seven, and another fifteen, all of them conceived.
By the estimation of the All-Knowing and All-Powerful, all of this should be in the knowledge of the midwife, for perhaps such a thing may occur to her one day, so she may confront it properly.
Chapter seventy-six.
On the extraction of a dead fetus.
If you treat the dead fetus with what I mentioned in the section on treatment, and it does not come out, and the matter necessitates you to work with your hand, then you should look: if the woman is healthy and strong and does not have any of the symptoms that one fears may cause her harm, then the woman should lie on her back on a bed.
And her head should be tilted downwards and her legs raised, and a servant should support her from both sides, or she should be tied to the bed, lest her body be pulled when the fetus is extracted.
Then moisten her vulva with moist ointments, along with mallow sap, fenugreek, and flaxseed. Then the midwife should anoint her hand with these ointments and juices, and gently insert it into the uterus, then search for a place to hook the forceps onto the fetus, and the appropriate places for this.
Look: if the fetus is positioned to descend head first, then hook the forceps into its eyes, or its nape, or its mouth, or its palate, or under its chin, or its shoulder, or in places near the ribs, or under the clavicles.
But if it descends feet first, then [hook the forceps] in the pubic area and the middle ribs.  The forceps should be held in the right hand, its bend between the fingers of the left hand, and the hand should be gently inserted and a hook placed in one of those mentioned places.
Until you reach something firm, then insert another hook opposite it, or a third if necessary, so that the traction is even and does not pull to one side. Then pull with a steady pull, not only straight, but moving it in every direction.
So that its exit is facilitated, as is done with a tooth when it is extracted.  In between, the pull should be relaxed; if it becomes stuck on one side, the midwife should anoint some fingers with ointment and insert them from that side to move what is stuck. If part of the fetus emerges, then move the hooks to other places.
It is slightly raised, doing this until the entire fetus emerges. If a hand emerges before anything else and cannot be returned because it is compressed, then a cloth should be wrapped around it and pulled until it is completely out, then cut.
Either at the wrist joint or at the elbow.  This should be done to the other hand and to the feet. If the head of the fetus is large and experiences difficulty in exiting, or if there is accumulated water in its head, then a lancet should be inserted between the fingers.
And split the head with it so that the water comes out, or puncture it with the instrument called the *mushdakh*, the image of which appears with the images of the other instruments in the chapter that follows this.
Similarly, if the fetus is naturally large-headed, the skull should be split or crushed with the *mushdakh*, as we have said, then the bones should be removed with forceps.  If the head emerges and is compressed at the clavicle, it should be split so that the moisture in the chest pours out.
For the chest then becomes constricted. If the chest does not become constricted and it is not possible for it to emerge, then cut as much as you can. If the lower abdomen is swollen or has a tumor, it should be punctured until the moisture flows from it.
If the fetus is feet first, then pulling it out and straightening it towards the mouth of the womb is easy. If it is compressed at the abdomen or chest, then it should be pulled with a cloth wrapped around your hand, and the abdomen or chest should be split so that what is in them flows out.
If all the other members are extracted and the head remains behind and is retained, let the midwife insert her left hand. If the mouth of the womb is open, let her insert her hand into the neck of the womb and seek the head with it and pull it with her fingers to the mouth of the womb, then insert a hook or two hooks into it and pull it with them.
If the mouth of the womb has closed due to a hot swelling that has occurred in it, it should not be forced; rather, it should be treated with the application of moist, oily substances, and compresses should be used, and [the patient] should sit in waters that relax and moisten.
But if the fetus is on its side, if it is possible to straighten it, then let what we mentioned for a living fetus be used. If this is not possible, then let it be cut and removed.  Nothing of the afterbirth should be left inside at all. When you have finished your treatment, use what we mentioned for the treatment of hot swellings which occur in the wombs.
If bleeding occurs, let [the patient] descend into astringent waters and use all other treatments. I have seen a woman who had conceived, and the fetus died in her womb, and then she conceived again upon it.
Then the other fetus also died. After a long time, a swelling appeared in her uterus and it swelled until it opened and began to discharge pus. I was called to treat her, and I treated her for a long time, but the wound did not close, so I put some strong, drawing ointments on it.
Then a bone came out of the place, and after some days another bone came out. I was amazed by this, since the abdomen is not a place where there are bones. I judged that they were from the bones of the dead fetus, so I examined the wound and removed many bones of the fetus's skull from it.
I continued to do this until I removed many bones, and the woman was in the best of health.  She lived for some time after that, with a small amount of pus discharging from the place. And I have only related this unusual case here.
because it contains knowledge and aid for what the skillful physician attempts with his hand in treatment.
Chapter Seventy-Seven.
In it are images of the instruments needed for removing the fetus.
Image of a spiral instrument to open the mouth of the womb.
This is a binder with which books are bound, having two spirals at the ends of the two pieces of wood, except that this spiral should be finer than the binder and should be either of ebony or of boxwood, and the width of each piece of wood should be the thickness of two fingers.
And their breadth is about a finger and their length is a span and a half, and in the middle of the two pieces of wood are two strips of the same kind of wood, which are fastened in them, their length being half a span or a little more, and their breadth about two fingers or a little more, and these two strips...
are those which enter the mouth of the womb to open it by turning the spirals. Image of another instrument for the same purpose, finer and lighter.
It is made of ebony or boxwood in the shape of hooks, except that it has two strips at its end, as you see; the length of each strip is about a span, and their width is two fingers.  If you wish to open the womb with it,
then seat the woman on a bed with her legs hanging down, spread apart between her thighs, then insert these two strips together into the mouth of the womb, while you hold the end of the instrument below between her thighs, then open your hand with the instrument as you do with hooks, as much as you want to open the womb. until the midwife does what she wants.  Image of another spiral mentioned by the ancients.
Image of the instrument which pushes out the fetus.
Image of the forceps with which the head of the fetus is grasped.
It resembles scissors, it has teeth at the end as you see, and it may be made rectangular like hooks, in this image as you see, it has teeth like the teeth of a saw, to cut and tear with.
Image of a pusher also.
Image of a hook.
This hook should be somewhat thick so that it does not break when pulling the fetus with it. Image of a hook with two barbs.
Image of two wide scalpels for cutting the fetus.
All of these instruments, the more numerous their types and the better prepared they are by the craftsman, the quicker his work and the higher his standing among people. Therefore, do not despise any instrument that you have prepared, for there will certainly be a need for it.
Chapter seventy-eight.
On the removal of the afterbirth.
If the afterbirth is retained after childbirth, you should instruct the patient to hold herself, then have her sneeze with a handkerchief, and press your hand on her mouth and nostrils. If it comes out in this way, well and good; otherwise, take a measure.
And pierce a hole in the lid and place in it herbs that open the mouth of the womb, such as fumitory, rue, dill, chamomile, wormwood, marshmallow, and centaury – all of these drugs or some of them – and immerse them in water and place them over the fire, then place a silver tube on the hole in the lid of the pot and the other end in the mouth of the womb.
And hold it until the vapor reaches the bottom of the womb, then have her sneeze as we said; then the afterbirth will come out quickly. If it remains after this treatment and does not come out, instruct the midwife to immerse her left hand in sherbet oil or mallow sap.
Then she inserts it into the vagina and searches for the afterbirth; if she finds it, she grasps it and pulls it out little by little. If it is attached deep in the womb, insert the hand as we described until, when you find the afterbirth, pull it out little by little, carefully.
Lest the womb fall out with strong pulling; rather, it should be moved gently to the sides, right and left, then the amount of pulling is increased; then it will yield and free itself from the adhesion. If the mouth of the womb is constricted, we have described the treatment with sneezing, and the treatment with the pot and herbs.
If it does not come out with all that we have described, avoid violence in pulling it again; but it should be tied from what has come out of it to the mouth of the womb to the woman's thigh, then inject it with the quadruple ointment, for it will cause it to rot after a few days.
And it will dissolve and come out; however, if it rots, a bad smell will ascend from it to the stomach and head, and this will harm the patient; therefore, appropriate fumigation should be used.
Therefore, some of the ancients have tried the fumigation of rue and dried figs.  The image of the instrument with which the woman is fumigated when menstruation and the afterbirth are retained, and so on:
It is made of lead, resembling a funnel, or it is made of copper; the thin end is placed in the vagina, and the wide end over the fire, and the incense is placed on the embers; it is held until that incense is gone, and then another is added.
Chapter Seventy-Nine.
In the treatment of the non-perforated fistula.
Many boys are born with their rectums imperforate; a thin membrane may have closed it.  It is necessary for the midwife to pierce this membrane with her finger, otherwise let her incise it with a sharp scalpel, taking care not to touch the muscle.
Then she should apply wool soaked in wine and oil, and then treat it with ointments until it heals. If she fears that it will close up, she should place a lead tube in the opening for many days, and remove it when the child wants to defecate.
The occlusion of the rectum may also occur from the healing of a wound or a tumor; this adhesion should be incised, and then treated as previously mentioned, with treatment and the lead tube.
Chapter Eighty.
In the treatment of fistulas which occur in the lower parts.
Fistulas which occur in the lower parts are a complication and thickening which occurs near the rectum externally or in the space from one of the sides, and the fistula may be single or multiple.
If this complication becomes chronic, it opens and a watery white moisture or thin pus flows from it.  Some of these fistulas may penetrate to the perineum and to the intestine, and some may not penetrate.  Those which penetrate may be recognized by what comes out of them of feces and flatus when the patient defecates.
And perhaps worms come out of them.  There may be fistulas, if they are in the space, which penetrate to the bladder and to the urethra.  And there may be some which penetrate to the hip joint and to the sacrum. And what distinguishes the fistula which penetrates to the rectum from that which does not penetrate is also known.
That you insert your index finger into the rectum and insert a thin probe into the fistula of copper or iron, if the fistula is not tortuous. If it is tortuous, then insert into it a thin lead probe or a horsehair.
Until you feel the probe or the hair with your finger. If you do not feel it at all, and nothing of feces, nor flatus, nor worms, as we said, emerges from the opening, then know that it is not penetrating, so proceed to treatment.
And if the fistula penetrates to the bladder or to the urethra, the indication is the passage of urine from it and the inability of the place to heal with medication. And if it penetrates to the hip joint or to the sacrum, its sign is the arrival of the probe there.
If there is no tortuosity to the bone and the presence of pain in that direction and the constant discharge of pus from it, and no treatment is effective and it does not heal with ointment, these penetrating fistulas, all of them, are incurable.
And their treatment is a vain effort for those who attempt them among ignorant physicians.  As for those that are not penetrating and not chronic, recovery is hoped for with cautery as I describe and as I have tested.  It is that the patient lies on his back between your hands.
And he raises his legs upwards and his thighs incline towards his abdomen, then you insert a copper or lead probe if there is tortuosity in the fistula until you know where the probe ends. If the patient feels it towards the anus, then you should insert your index finger into the anus.
If you feel with your finger the probe having penetrated by itself, exposed without feeling between your finger and it any membrane or flesh, then know for certain that it is penetrating, so do not tire yourself with it, for there is no cure for it, as we said.
And they have said that it heals in some people rarely. And of the treatments for which benefit is hoped for is that you heat a thin cautery iron according to the size of the fistula as has been explained, and insert it hot into the fistula until it reaches towards the anus, then repeat it two or three times.
Until you know that all that excess, matted flesh resembling the tube of a bird's feather has been burned. Then treat it with lint soaked in butter until that burned flesh comes out, then treat it with healing ointments, if it heals.
And otherwise it will never heal with any other treatment. But if you insert the probe and it does not penetrate to your finger which is in the anus, and there was between it and the probe a thick partition of flesh or of membrane, and you saw the fistula in what is near the surface of the skin, then incise the skin from the beginning of the fistula. And you pass with the incision along with the probe while it is in the fistula until you reach with the incision where the end of the probe has reached, and the probe is released and falls out. Then you cleanse that matted flesh resembling a bird's feather tube, and leave nothing of it.
Then treat it with healing ointments until it heals. If blood overwhelms you and prevents you from your work of cutting that flesh, then the best and quickest remedy you can do is cauterization with fire or with a caustic medicine; for cauterization with fire combines two good things: burning that excess flesh.
and cutting the blood and drying the moisture. Then treat it with lint soaked in butter or sulfur powdered with oil until the place suppurates and that burned flesh comes out in the pus. Then treat the wound with healing ointments that grow firm flesh.
These are the medicines which have astringent and drying properties; for when it is closed, it has healed, and you do not fear a return. If the fistula has reached the depth of the anus and is far from the surface of the body, then insert your finger into the anus and explore with it.
If you feel the probe and between you and it a partition of peritoneum or flesh, and it is close to penetration, then the treatment is only by way of hope and expectation; that is, to use one of three methods: either to cauterize it as we said.
Or to incise it until you reach its bottom so that you can easily insert the lint and treat it from close by. Do not go too far with the incision lest you cut the muscle surrounding the anus and cause the patient involuntary evacuation of stool. Then treat it as we have mentioned; perhaps it will heal as we said.
Or to penetrate that partition and pierce it after incising it near the anus as we said, either with a probe or with another sharp-pointed instrument. Then cleanse all the hardened flesh in the incision as far as you can, completely.
Then sew up the whole wound, including the incision and the upper opening, until it is closed, and leave the fistula open inside the anus. This will be easier for the patient.  The fistula may perforate in this manner.
And if you insert the probe into the fistula and it is in a part of the anus towards the surface of the body with the skin and the circumference of the anus, then take a probe with a perforated tip like a shoemaker's needle, in this form:
And insert into it a twisted thread of five or similar threads, then insert the probe with the thread into the fistula until it reaches its bottom. If it penetrates into the margin of the anus from the inside nearby.
Then bring out the thread from that hole by inserting your finger into the anus and bringing out the end of the thread. Bring both ends together and tighten them, and leave it for a day or two. Every time the thread cuts into the flesh, tighten it well until the flesh between the ends of the threads is severed and falls away. Then treat the wound until it heals and is cured.
If the opening of the fistula is not perforated, perforate it however you can, except that if it is very deep, you should not do this because of the muscle, lest you cut it. Then do as we have mentioned until it heals.
The image of the hooked scalpel with which fistulas are incised.  The bend is very sharp, and the other side is not sharp, lest you cut what is not needed, as you see.
Chapter eighty-one.
Concerning the perforation of hemorrhoids from which blood flows, their excision, and the treatment of fissures.
Hemorrhoids are of two types: either they are inside the rectum, resembling red bladders or like grapes, and they are small and large, and blood constantly flows from them.
And there may be one or many, and they may be outside the rectum and at its edges, except that those which are outside the rectum are, in most cases, less moist; a yellow fluid or a little blood flows from them constantly, and they are the color of the body.
The treatment for those which are inside the rectum is that you order the patient to strain and bear down until the rectum protrudes and the hemorrhoids appear to you. Then you quickly grasp them with hooks or hold them with your fingernail and then cut them at their bases. If the hooks do not hold because of their moisture and laxness,
then rub them with a rough cloth or a piece of goatskin, or pull them with your fingers, then cut them.  Then, after cutting, sprinkle on them some sharp powders to act in place of cautery, or cauterize them as previously described in the chapter on cautery.
Then treat it with the rest of the treatment until it heals. If the rectum does not protrude, then inject the patient with an enema containing a little irritant to wash what is in the rectum and to facilitate its rapid protrusion when the patient strains. As for the hemorrhoids outside the rectum, their treatment is easy and simple.
That is, you take it with your fingernail or grasp it with a hook and cut it, then treat it as we have mentioned until it heals. And whoever dislikes cutting with iron should use a ligature in this manner: take a twisted thread and insert it into a needle.
Then pull the hemorrhoid upwards and pass the needle through its base from the other side, and wrap the ends of the thread below the needle while it is across, and pull the hemorrhoid tightly. Then tie the thread and remove the needle. Do this to all the hemorrhoids, leaving one unligated.
so that the excess blood flows out. Then place a cloth soaked in rose oil or kyrios on the rectum and tell the patient to remain still.  Then leave him until they fall off. When the hemorrhoids have fallen off, treat them with ointments and the rest of the treatment until they heal.
As for the treatment of fissures, they often occur from dryness of the stool and retention of the temperament. If it becomes chronic and medicine does not work, then it should be scraped with a scalpel blade or your fingernail until it becomes moist and the upper layer which prevents it from healing is removed.
Then treat it until it heals as it should. If it does not heal, repeat the scraping more severely than before until it bleeds well and is excised, then treat it, for it will heal.
Chapter eighty-two.
On the treatment of ingrown and non-ingrown nails, dry warts, and anthills.
A nail is a round nodule, the color of the body, resembling the head of a nail; it occurs throughout the body, especially on the soles of the feet and the toes, and causes pain when walking.
It is necessary to incise around the nail, grasp it with a hook or forceps, and remove it from its root, then treat it; and if you wish, cauterize it as described in its chapter.  As for dry warts, each one is a small, rough, hardened, round protrusion on the surface of the body.
They often appear on the hands of children.  Its treatment is to stretch the wart and cut it, or pierce it with a silk thread or hair until it falls off; and if you wish, cauterize it with fire or a caustic medicine as previously described.
Ingrown and non-ingrown warts may be removed with the instrument I mentioned in the treatment of anthills. As for anthills, they are also small, hardened, thick protrusions on the surface of the body, extending deep; and if they are affected by cold, a sensation similar to the stinging of ants is felt in them.
They occur throughout the body, most often on the hands.  Its treatment is to take a tube made from a strong goose or eagle feather, place the tube over the anthill so that the circumference of the tube surrounds it on all sides, then rotate the tube with your hand until you have incised around the anthill.
Then push it into the depth of the flesh, and the anthill will be removed from its root. And if you wish, you may do this with a copper or iron tube in this manner:
The top of the tube should be solid and twisted to facilitate gripping and turning it with the fingers. And if you wish, cauterize the anthill as previously described in the cauterization of warts in the chapter on cauterization.
Chapter Eighty-Three.
Concerning the instruments used for injections in cases of rectal disorders, diarrhea, and colic.
A syringe may be made of silver, china, or cast or beaten copper.  Small and large sizes of this instrument may be made according to the users. Those used in treating small children should be small, and those for patients with narrow or painful rectums should be very gentle. Image of a large syringe:
Its upper funnel, to which the bladder is tied, is wide in this manner; it has a partition where the bladder is tied, as you see, and its lower end, which is inserted into the rectum, is solid and very smooth, except that on one side there are two holes and on the other side one hole.
The size of the hole should be the thickness of, or slightly thicker than, an awl. The bladder, in which the medicine is placed, is made from an animal's bladder or sheepskin, shaped like a small pouch, its diameter being a span.
Many holes are pierced in it in a circular pattern, the distance between each hole being the thickness of a finger. Then a strong thread of twisted yarn, of ten or so strands, is inserted into these holes and the bladder is gathered together like a pouch. Then, when the medicine is placed inside, it is tightly bound at the head of the syringe between the stones, and then it is injected.  This is an image of the circular bladder and the holes:
The method of injection is to fill the pouch or bladder with warmed medicine, as much as the patient can bear, but beware of it being too hot or too cold. Then the patient lies on his back and raises his legs.
A cloth or blanket is placed beneath him. Then the end of the syringe is lubricated with oil, egg white, fenugreek saliva, or something similar, and gently inserted into the rectum. Then the attendant takes the bladder in both hands and squeezes it forcefully until everything in the syringe has emptied into the bowel.
Then the syringe is removed, and the patient raises his legs against the wall. If the medicine stimulates evacuation, he should restrain himself as much as possible; if he sleeps the whole night with it, it will be more beneficial.  If it is injected for diarrhea and evacuation is hastened, repeat the injection until he is cured.
If there are hemorrhoids or painful tumors in the rectum, the syringe should be very soft and smooth, smoother than those used to inject children. This is an image of a soft syringe with its skin stretched over it:
Chapter Eighty-Four.
In the treatment of wounds.
I mentioned in the classification the treatment of what resembles cauterization, and I will now describe its treatment by hand and by medication completely. I say that wounds differ according to the instrument causing the wound and according to the location where the wound occurs.
The instruments that cause wounds are numerous, such as a blow from a stone, a cut from a sword or knife, a stab from a spear or stick or arrow, and so on from many things.
As for wounds according to their location on the body, there are wounds that occur on the head, neck, chest, abdomen, liver, and other organs.
And I am describing the treatment of some wounds to make them a measure and standard for all other wounds, and I begin with simple head wounds, especially because complex ones will be mentioned at the beginning of the third chapter of this book.
So I say that when a simple wound occurs in the head and there is no fracture of the bone, I look; if it is from a blow from a stone or the like, and it has only torn the skin, and the wound is large, and I fear for the patient the occurrence of hot inflammation, then I hasten and open the temporal vein appropriately. And do not delay that, and let blood be drawn from him according to his strength, if nothing prevents bloodletting, especially if the blood coming from the wound is little, and let the bloodletting be from the opposite side of the injured area.
And warn the patient against fullness, drinks, and heavy foods; if something prevents him from bloodletting, let him reduce food and not approach meats or drinks.  And apply to the wound, if hot inflammation occurs in it, cotton soaked in rose oil alone or with an astringent drink.
And if you are safe from hot inflammation, apply to the wound, if it is fresh and the air has not altered it—the unguent which I will describe to you shortly—but if the air has altered it, apply some ointments to it.
Until pus forms, then treat it until it heals. If the wound is large and is from a sword cut or the like, and its edges do not come together by themselves, then join it by suturing, as I describe in the suturing of abdominal wounds.
If the skin exposes the bone and hangs, and its attachment is only by a small ligament, cut it, then treat the wound with medicines that produce firm flesh in place of the skin, and order the patient to eat foods that have firmness, such as heads and brains and the like.
If decay occurs in the skin and does not adhere to the bone, cut away all the decayed part and throw it away, then treat it. If in these wounds there is an artery or vein bleeding and the bleeding does not stop with medication, then search for the artery.
If you find it and it is not severed, sever it with the scalpel and tie it. If necessary, if what we have mentioned is not effective, cauterize it until the bleeding stops. If these wounds are small and superficial, their treatment is easy; it suffices to treat them by sprinkling this unguent on them while they are still bleeding.
Before the air changes them.  The preparation of the unguent is that one part of frankincense, two parts of mastic, and three parts of slaked or unslaked lime are taken.  All are ground, sifted, and the wound is packed with it and bound tightly until it adheres well.
It coagulates with the blood, then bind it from above with bandages and leave it; do not unbind it as long as it does not swell, for the medicine will not separate from it until it heals. The lime alone may do this if you do not have frankincense and mastic.
This action may also be done with alum in many large wounds if its application and binding are properly done, and the wounds are fresh and bleeding, before the air changes them, but if the air has changed them somewhat, especially in summer.
Then there is no point in applying this unguent; treat it with what we mentioned: apply some ripening ointments or apply a poultice of barley flour made with water and honey until the pus is drawn out, then treat it with other treatments until it heals.
But if a fracture of the bone occurs with the wound and it is slight, then reduce it with traction.  I have described the treatment of head fractures in what follows, in the surgery of the neck. If the wound occurs in the neck, there is no difference in treatment between it and a head wound if it is simple.
But if a nerve or artery of the neck has been severed, if it is a nerve, there is no remedy other than to avoid treating it with things that cause strong constriction, such as verdigris and vitriol and the like, because these harm the nerve and cause it to spasm.
Nor should anything cold whatsoever be placed on the wound because the substance of the nerve is cold and its connection is to the brain, which is the noblest of organs.  Treat it with soft medicines such as washed nutria with fresh water many times, moistened with oil or rose ointment and tutty.
If also washed with fresh water and other mineral stones in this manner and moist ointments. But if the wound is large, use sutures or bind its edges with compresses, then treat it until it heals.
If the wound has depth and a pocket forms in its lower part where pus collects, open it in its lowest place. If an artery has been severed in the wound and you see its blood flowing, ligate it or cauterize it if necessity demands it.
If the wound has severed some or all of the cartilages of the larynx and the trachea is intact, join the edges of the skin with sutures over the windpipe, but do not touch the windpipe; rather smooth it and return it to its natural shape, then tightly bind it and leave it for some days.
Then treat it with ointments that have astringent and drying properties until it heals. If the wound has affected the bone, examine it; if there are bone splinters, extract all of them or as many as you can; what you cannot reach, leave until the wound suppurates and heals and it is easy to remove them.
If the wound is fresh, sprinkle upon it the powder we described. If a bone becomes infected in the wound and it becomes fistulous, treat it with what will be mentioned in its chapter, in the surgery of the chest and between the shoulders.
If it is a wound from a spear or knife and you see that it has depth, look: if air comes out of it when the patient breathes, know that it is a mortal wound. If it does not have depth and is fresh with its blood, do not at first put powder in it, nor bind it tightly, lest the blood be retained in its depth.
It will then recede to the heart and kill the patient.  But put in it an attractive ointment and dress it twice a day. If you do not have ointment, put old cotton in the mouth of the wound to absorb the moisture that comes from it, and have the patient sleep on the wound.
so that what collects in it will flow out. And if three days or more have passed since the wounding, and the patient has not developed convulsions, a bad pulse, or shortness of breath, and you see that his other conditions are good, know that the wound is healthy, so treat it with cauterization and other treatments until it heals.
If its sight is difficult and the pus flows constantly, know that it has become fistulous, and treat it in its chapter. The patient lies on the wound so that what collects in it may flow out. If the wound is in the chest or back, treat it with the preceding stitching.
If it is large, use powder; if it is small, use cauterization. If it has affected the bone and broken off splinters, explore the wound and proceed to remove those splinters if they are clean. If they are not clean, leave them until…
the wound becomes septic, for then it is easy to remove them.  As for other wounds occurring in other members, their treatment follows the rule of what we have mentioned.
Chapter Eighty-five.
Concerning wounds of the abdomen, the protrusion of the intestines, and their suturing.
The wound which occurs in the abdomen may be large, or it may be small or medium.  A large wound may allow the protrusion of an intestine or several intestines, and returning them and suturing will be more difficult and arduous.
A small wound may also be difficult in another respect; returning the intestine may be more difficult, and if the intestine is not immediately returned to its place, it swells and thickens, making its return difficult. For this reason, a medium-sized wound is preferable, because returning the intestine is not as difficult with it as with these two types.
Know that the suturing of the abdomen is of four kinds: two are general, suitable for suturing the abdomen and for suturing other wounds of the body; the other two are specific, used in other wounds, but they are more particularly suited to the suturing of the abdomen.
And I will describe them to you one by one with explanation and clarification. I say that if the wound is small and some of the intestine protrudes and its return is difficult, this is for one of two reasons: either because of the smallness of the wound, as we said, or because the intestine has been swollen by the cold air.
If this is the case, then it should be warmed by dipping a sponge or wet cloth in lukewarm water alone, or in water in which anise, rue, and wheat have been boiled, and applying it to the intestine until the swelling resolves.
A styptic drink may also do this, and it is better than water alone in resolving the swelling. When you see that the swelling has resolved, moisten the intestine with water in which comfrey or mallow has been boiled, for this will make its return easier.
If its return is still difficult after this treatment, then make a small incision in the wound with the instrument used to incise fistulas. The first treatment, if successful, is better than incision. Incision is only resorted to after necessity and failure of other methods. This is an image of the instrument:
One side of it is curved and pointed, the other side is not pointed. The thin end should not be as sharp as a scalpel, but slightly blunt. It is an instrument resembling a small forceps, as you see.  If the wound widens and the intestine enters, then it should be returned to its natural shape.
And its proper place, if you can manage it, is best. But if the wound is large and in the lower abdomen, the patient should lie on his back with his legs raised higher than his head; if it is in the upper abdomen, his head and chest should be raised higher than his lower body.
Similarly, if the wound is on one side of the abdomen, always keep the side with the injury higher than the other. This should be done in large and medium-sized wounds.
As for small wounds, position him as you are able. Then have a skilled assistant ready, who holds the entire wound with both hands, bringing its edges together, then revealing it bit by bit to the one who is stitching.
This is the description of a single common stitch: take one needle, or several needles, according to the size of the wound. Then leave a space from the edge of the wound equal to the thickness of the little finger, and insert a single needle without thread into the edges of the skin. along with the edges of the peritoneum under the skin from the inside, until you pass it through from that side.  You have now brought together the edges of the skin and the edges of the peritoneum, forming four layers. Then, tighten with a doubled thread around the needle several times from both sides until the edges of the wound are firmly joined.
Then leave a space equal to the thickness of a finger, and insert another needle, then connect it with the thread as you did with the first needle. Continue doing this with as many needles as needed until you complete the entire circumference of the wound. Let the needles be of medium thickness, neither too thick nor too thin.
Because very thin needles quickly cut the flesh, and thick ones are difficult to insert into the skin; therefore, they should be of medium thickness.  And the needle should not be inserted into the edge of the skin too close to the wound.
lest the flesh quickly tear and the wound open before it heals.  Do not space the stitches too far apart either, lest the wound fail to unite. Then cut off the ends of the needles so that they do not injure the patient when he sleeps, and make him pads of linen cloth on each side to hold the ends of the needles.
And leave them until you know that the wound has healed. This type of stitching with needles is best suited for small wounds, because one or two needles, or so, may suffice for stitching them.
As for the method of stitching for the common [wound], it is to gather with the stitching the four edges—that is, the edges of the skin and the edges of the peritoneum—at once with a needle threaded with a twisted thread of moderate thickness, then when you have passed the needle through these four edges, return the needle to the same side from which you began.
So that the thread lies interwoven from the top of the wound, so that the stitching is like the stitching of a purse with which belongings are tied. And make the space between each stitch the width of a little finger. This stitching is known to all people.
With this stitching, a wound was stitched for a man in his abdomen, who had been wounded with a knife. The wound was more than a span long, and about two spans of the midgut had protruded. And the wound was in the middle of the abdomen.
And I replaced it after its gut had remained outside the wound for twenty-four hours.  The wound healed in about fifteen days, and I treated him until he recovered, and he lived many years thereafter, managing all his affairs. And the physicians had judged that he would never recover.
And it is remarkable that I did not treat him with an ointment, because I was in a place where there were no medicines.  So I applied worn cotton to the wound twice a day and would treat it by washing with honey water until it healed.
As for one of the two types of special stitching, I mention it according to Galen's words:  Begin the stitching from the skin, and insert the needle from outside to inside. Then, when the needle has penetrated the skin and the muscle going straight along the whole length of the abdomen...
...leave the edge of the peritoneum on the side where you inserted the needle, and pass the needle through its other edge from inside to outside, into the other edge of the peritoneum. Then, when you have passed it through, pass it again through this same edge of the peritoneum from outside to inside.
And leave the edge of the peritoneum on this side, and pass the needle through its other edge from inside to outside.  And as you pass it through, pass it into the edge of the peritoneum on its side, until it penetrates it completely.
Then begin again from this same side, and sew it together with the edge of the peritoneum on the other side. Bring the needle out of the skin nearby, then return it into that skin and sew the edge of the peritoneum on the other side together with this edge of the peritoneum.
Bring it out through the skin on its side. Do this again and again, one time after another, until you have sewn up the whole wound in this way. The complete description of this stitching is that it is sewn like the stitching of furriers for fur.
That you sew the peritoneum once from one side with the edge of the skin, and leave it once from the other side, until you are finished. As for the second type of special stitching, which Galen also mentioned, his words are literally:
It is that you sew it in the manner that some healers sew, by joining each part to its counterpart, naturally similar to it, so that they join the edge of the peritoneum to its other edge, and the edge of the intestine to its other edge.  This is as I describe to you.
You should insert the needle into the edge of the intestine nearest you from the outside, and pass it to the inside in it alone, and leave the edges of the peritoneum. Then return the needle and pass it from the outside to the inside in both edges of the peritoneum. Then return it again.
And pass it from the inside to the outside in the edge of the other intestine, which is on the opposite side. This type of stitching is better than the easy, general stitching, which is the stitching previously mentioned. These are Galen's exact words. Some have said that the stitching of the abdomen may be done with two needles. That is, you insert one thread into them, and begin by inserting the needle from your side and passing it to the other side, and insert the other needle from the opposite side, then pass it from your side, according to the stitching of the saddlebags.
Know that if the wound is in the middle of the abdomen, then its stitching is more difficult than in other places of the abdomen. As for the treatment of the wound, its ruling in its treatment is the ruling of all other wounds. That is, if you find the wound fresh with its blood before the air changes it, and you return the intestine and stitch it and make it tight, then apply to it the styptic powder.
If the wound has been changed by the air, then apply to it some of the ointments that you prepare until it suppurates and the stitches fall out, and the peritoneum and intestine unite. Then treat it as you treat all other wounds until it heals.
If you do not have medicines, then apply to it, from the time it begins to suppurate, old cotton, and change it twice a day, as I have taught you, until it heals. For you do not need further treatment in most cases if the wounds are simple. If you fear that the wound will involve the principal organs in pain,
Then it is necessary that you dip soft wool in moderately warm oil or rose oil and place it around the areas between the groin and the armpit. If he feels pain or putrefaction in his intestine—and that often happens—then inject him with lukewarm black astringent syrup.
Especially if the putrefaction has reached the intestine and become a penetrating wound into its cavity. And know that what is thick of the intestines is easier to heal, and what is thin of them is more difficult to heal.
As for the intestine known as the jejunum, it does not accept healing from a wound that befalls it at all, because of the multitude of its veins and their size, and the thinness of its substance, and its proximity to the nature of the nerve.  But if what emerged from the wound is the mesentery, and you find it moist, then return it in the same way as you would return the intestine.
If a period of time has passed and it has turned green or black, then it is necessary that you tie it with a thread above the place which has blackened, lest bleeding occur, for there are veins and arteries in the mesentery, then cut below that ligature.
And leave the ends of the thread hanging from below the wound, emerging from it, so that it is easy for you to pull and remove it when the mesentery falls off and the wound suppurates.  [He] mentioned the wound which occurs in the intestine.  But if a rupture occurs in the intestine and it is small,
it is possible that it may heal in some people, because I saw a man who had been wounded in his abdomen by a spear thrust, and the wound was to the right of the stomach, and the wound became chronic and became a fistula from which feces and wind emerged, so I treated him though I did not hope for his healing. I continued to treat him gently until it healed and the place rejoined. When I saw the place had rejoined, I feared for the patient that a bad incident might occur in his abdomen, but no bad incident occurred to him at all, and he remained in the best of health. He eats and drinks properly, and engages in sexual intercourse, and enters the bathhouse, and participates in his service.  Some experienced people have mentioned that whenever a wound occurs in the intestine and it is small, then it should be sutured in this manner.
And that is to take large-headed ants, then bring the edges of the wound together, then place one of them, with its mouth open, on the edges of the wound; and when it has grasped and tightened its mouth, cut off its head, for it will adhere and not become undone. Then place another ant near the first.
And you continue to do that with several ants according to the size of the wound, then you return it and suture the wound, for those heads remain stuck in the intestine until the intestine heals and recovers, and no harm at all occurs to the patient.  It is also possible to suture the intestine with fine thread.
That which is drawn out from the intestine of the animal adhering to it after it is inserted into a needle is that a strand of this thread from the intestine is taken, gently drawn out, then a thin, twisted linen thread is tied to its end, and that thread is then inserted into the needle. And in it is the intestinal thread, with which the intestine is sutured, then returned to the abdomen. This type of suturing with ants and intestine is only by way of hope and expectation.
But if the perforation is large and extensive, especially if it is in one of the thin intestines, then there is no remedy and no healing at all.
Chapter Eighty-Six.
In the treatment of catarrh and fistula.
Know that every wound or swelling, if it becomes chronic and longstanding and becomes an ulcer and does not heal, and constantly discharges pus that does not cease, is called in general, in whatever organ it may be, a fistula, and we call it a catarrh, and a fistula.
It is in reality a hardened, matted, white mass, with no pain, having a cavity like the cavity of a bird's feather.
Therefore, some have called it a feather.  And it is sometimes moist, constantly discharging pus, and sometimes the moisture may cease.  This moisture may be abundant or scanty, thick or thin.
And I have already stated in the classification, when mentioning wounds, that every ulcer which does not heal and in which flesh does not grow, is so because of one of nine reasons: one of which is either because of a deficiency of blood in the body, or because of its poor quality, or because inside it and on its edges there is hard flesh which prevents the growth of good flesh. Or because it is very foul and dirty; or because the ulcer itself is putrid and the matter it discharges is of poor quality; or because the medicine is unsuitable for its treatment; or because of a general epidemic corruption in the region; or because of a specific quality in the region.
Or because it is very foul and dirty; or because the ulcer itself is putrid and the matter it discharges is of poor quality; or because the medicine is unsuitable for its treatment; or because of a general epidemic corruption in the region; or because of a specific quality in the region. As was shown in the city of Zaragoza, where the maturation of diseases is difficult and the healing of tumors is naturally slow; or because there is one bone or several bones in it.  I have explained all these causes, their signs, and their treatments with medicines in the classification.
Here it is necessary for me to acquaint you with the treatment of fistulas and abscesses which is done by means of the hand. Know that fistulas may occur in all the members of the body; some of them end in large veins and arteries, or in a nerve.
Or to the peritoneum, or to the intestine, or to the bladder, or to a rib of the ribs, or to a vertebra of the vertebrae of the back, or to a joint of the compound joints, such as the hand and the foot; and some of them lead to the vicinity of a principal organ, and so on.
Now, those fistulas which are of this kind are among the difficult-to-cure diseases which do not tolerate treatment and the occupation with them is troublesome and ignorant; but if there are fistulas which do not reach these places which I have mentioned, and I have hoped for and expected their treatment,
and you wish to arrive at the knowledge of that, then take a probe of brass or iron; if the fistula runs straight, then explore it with it; but if there is a winding in the fistula, explore it with a probe of thin lead, because lead is soft in its body.
Then it will become smooth at the entrance and bend towards the winding.  If the fistula has many openings which you cannot discover with the probe, inject one of its openings; for the moisture which you inject into it will pass towards the other openings and flow out.
Then thoroughly investigate in whichever way you can, to find out whether there is bone or nerve, or whether the bottom of the fistula is far or near, or whether it is a single fistula with many openings.  Establish all this to the extent of your ability by examining the patient and his feeling of pain when you touch the place.
And in the same way from the signs and causes occurring in the swelling.  If you have established all this truthfully, then proceed to treatment with confidence; it is that you should see whether, if it is...
the fistula is apparent and near, or in a safe place far from a joint or nerve or artery or vein or one of the places I mentioned to you, then incise the fistula as I have described above and remove what is in it of the clot and the corrupted flesh and the excess flesh.
And whatever grows in its opening of flesh or wart and the like, and treat it until it heals. If the fistula is deep-seated and straight, then you should incise it deeply as much as you can, then cleanse it of all its corrupted flesh.
Then use a twisted tent of strong medications and insert it to the bottom of the fistula which you can reach with an instrument; do this repeatedly until that strong medication eats away all that remains in the bottom of the fistula of corruption.
Then treat it with ointments which cause healthy flesh to grow until it heals. If it does not heal with that, cauterize it as described above. If the cause of the fistula is bone and this is certain to you, then investigate it and incise it as I have told you, if nothing prevents you.
a nerve or a main organ as we said, then if the bone is revealed to you and there is some corruption and blackness in it, then scrape it until its corruption is gone, then treat it with what will heal it until it heals. If it does not heal and the pus continues as it was doing,
know that you have not reached the complete removal of the corruption; expose all of it again and completely scrape and cleanse it with the utmost effort, then treat it. If it heals, otherwise know that that corruption is in a deep recess; expose all of it again.
and completely scrape and cleanse it with the utmost effort. If the corrupted bone is small and you can pull it out, then pull it out with delicate forceps suitable for that.
If there are many bones, completely remove them all and do not leave any of them; exert your utmost effort. If something of the ligaments obstructs you and there is no danger in cutting them, then cut those ligaments and the flesh if it is there, and remove the bones.
If you cannot remove the bones at once, then apply to the wound something that will cause it to decay, and leave it for some days until the flesh and ligaments around those bones decay.  Keep the wound from closing; the incision you made during your work.
Rather, if you fear that, place on it cotton soaked in powdered sulfur with oil, or cotton soaked in Egyptian ointment, or one of the green ointments; for when they cause those ligaments to decay and the bones to loosen, it will be easy to remove and pull them out.
If it is a single large bone, like a bone of the leg or thigh, and so on, and only its surface is corrupted, then scrape it thoroughly until that blackness and corruption disappear, then dress the wound. If a large part of it is corrupted, and the corruption has reached the marrow of the bone, then it must be opened.
And cut it all away to where the corruption ends; then treat it until it heals. And I tell you of a gangrene that occurred in a man's leg, to make it an example and aid to your treatment; this man was young.
About thirty years old; a pain occurred in his leg, from a cause moving within his body until the humors reached the leg and it swelled with a great swelling; there was no external cause. Then time passed with the mistakes of the physicians until the swelling opened.
And much matter flowed from it, and he was treated badly until the leg became gangrenous and many openings appeared in it, all discharging pus and the humors of the body. A group of physicians treated him for about two years, and there was not among them one skilled in surgery.
Until they sought me out. I saw his leg and the matter flowing from those openings in great abundance, and the man was emaciated and his color yellow. Then I inserted a probe into one of those openings, and the probe reached the bone. Then I examined all the openings.
And I found them leading to one another from all sides of the leg. So I began and incised one of those openings until I uncovered some of the bone, and I found it corrupted, eaten away, black, putrid, and perforated until the perforation reached the marrow.  Then I excised what was revealed to me.
And I removed the corrupted bone, and I thought that there was no corruption in the bone other than what I had cut and excised, and that I had eradicated it. Then I proceeded to dress the wound with agglutinative medicines for a long time, but it did not heal. Then I returned and uncovered the bone again, beyond the first uncovering.
And I found the corruption continuous with the bone. So I excised what was also revealed to me of that corruption. Then I attempted to dress it, but it did not heal nor unite. Then I uncovered it again, and I continued to cut the bone piece by piece and attempt to heal it, but it would not heal, until I had cut away about a span of bone.
And I removed it with its marrow. Then I dressed it with medicines, and it healed quickly and completely.  This repetition in its operation and incision was necessary because of the weakness of the patient and his low tolerance, and my fear for his death, because he suffered from excessive vomiting and fainting at all times. He recovered completely.
And in the place of the bone grew firm flesh, and his condition in his body was improved, and his strength returned, and he managed his affairs, and in his walking he was not afflicted by anything whatsoever. If a projecting bone in a part of the body is broken…
Then it should be excised in this manner: you take a ligature and tie it around the end of the projecting bone, and you instruct someone to pull it upwards, and you make another ligature of thicker wool than the first ligature.
Then you tie it to the flesh beneath the bone and pull its end so that the flesh is drawn downwards, and you expose the flesh from the place which you wish to excise, lest the saw injure the flesh, and you place a piece of wood or a board firmly beneath the bone from below.
Because if you do that, the saw will not be prevented from cutting the corrupted bone.  The sawing should be slightly above the location of the corruption, lest there be corruption in the cavity of the bone which does not appear on its surface to the touch, thus necessitating sawing it again.
If the bone is corrupted and is not projecting, but its parts are connected to one another, and the corruption is in its middle or in some part of it, then expose the flesh from all its sides completely, then place the wood from below, then saw from the first side where the corruption is.
When you have finished sawing it, saw it from the other side, and let the sawing be a little distance from the corruption, as we said. If the corruption is in a joint, cut the corrupted joint itself and saw the bone where it connects from the other side.
If the corruption is in the connection of two joints, there is no recourse but excision. If the corruption is in the metacarpus of the hand or the metacarpus of the foot, the matter of its treatment is very difficult, but you should peel off the corruption however it appears to you, and excise it and clean it in whatever way you can.
And by whatever method it is straightened for you, provided that a vein or nerve does not obstruct you. And know that the saws and the instruments for cutting these bones are numerous, according to the position of the bones, their setting, their thickness and thinness, their size and smallness, their hardness and looseness. Therefore, it is necessary to prepare for each type of work an instrument similar to that work.
And know that the works themselves may indicate to you the type of instrument that you need, if you have long practice and knowledge of the arts of this craft, because it is from the skill of the craft and witnessing many types of diseases. He may devise for himself instruments similar to those for each disease, and I am depicting for you at the end of this chapter several instruments.
Make them examples to be imitated and a measure by which you measure others, God willing.  Image of a saw:

Chapter eighty-seven.
In cutting off limbs and sawing bones.
Limbs may become putrid, either from an external cause or from an internal cause. If you treat this corruption with medicinal treatment and the treatment is not effective, and you see the corruption spreading in the limb, nothing restraining it,
then it is necessary to cut off that limb to where the corruption has reached, so that the patient may escape death or a great affliction which is greater than the loss of the limb.  A sign of this occurring is that the limb blackens, until you think that fire has burnt it.
Or it may become putrid after the blackening, until that putridity spreads to what is next to that limb and takes hold in the body as a whole. Therefore, hasten to cut it off.  Similarly, if the cause of the corruption is the sting of some creature, such as a sea scorpion, a snake, a tarantula, or the like.
If the corruption or sting is in the tip of a finger, cut off the finger and do not allow the corruption to spread to the hand.  Similarly, if it has taken hold in the hand, cut it off from the origin of the forearm, and do not allow the corruption to spread until it takes hold in the forearm.
Similarly, if it has taken hold in the arm, cut off the arm at the elbow in the joint itself. If the corruption spreads and you see it taking hold towards the shoulder, do not cut off the shoulder at all, for in that is the death of the patient; rather use other treatments as much as possible.
And similarly you do with the leg: if the corruption takes hold in a toe, cut off the toe at one of the joints; and if it takes hold in the metatarsus, cut off the whole foot; and if it ascends to the knee, cut off the leg at the knee joint.
And if the corruption has reached above the knee, there is no recourse but to leave it and leave the patient to death. The method of cutting off or dissecting a limb is to tie a ligature below the place you wish to cut and tie another ligature above the place.
And one assistant pulls the lower ligature downwards, and another assistant pulls the upper ligature upwards, and you yourself expose the flesh between the ligatures with a wide scalpel until all the flesh is exposed, then it is cut or dissected; and it is necessary that you place linen cloths on all sides.
Lest the saw touch the healthy tissue and cause the patient excessive pain and hot swelling. If bleeding occurs during your work, staunch the place quickly or apply some styptic powder, then return to your treatment until you finish.
Then bind the injured limb with a suitable bandage and treat it until it heals. And I tell you of an example of a man who experienced this very condition in his leg, that is, blackness with burning like fire appeared in his leg.
And that corruption first appeared in his toe until it consumed the whole leg.  Then the man himself, seeing the corruption spreading in the limb along with the intense pain and burning, cut it off at the joint and recovered.
Then, after a long time, that same corruption appeared in the index finger of his hand.  He came to me, and I tried to stop that corruption with the medications I applied to his hand after cleansing his body. But the corruption did not stop and began to spread to another finger.
Until the corruption consumed his hand, and he asked me to amputate it. But I refused, hoping to stop the corruption, and I also feared for his life if I amputated his hand because the man was very weak. When he despaired of me, he returned to his town.
Then I heard that he himself cut off his entire hand and recovered.  I have recounted this story to be helpful in cases of this kind of disease and to serve as a guide to be followed.
Chapter Eighty-Eight.
On the treatment of fistulas and how to inject them with medication.
If a swelling occurs in one of the fleshy organs and the duration of the swelling is prolonged until it gathers pus, then it bursts or is lanced and all the pus inside comes out, and the place is left empty like a vessel.
And the skin over it is like a thin rag, not so severely damaged as to affect the bone, nerve, or ligament.  Therefore it deserves to be called a fistula, and not a sinus, unless its duration is prolonged until the damage affects one of these organs.
Then it is called a sinus or a fistula, and its treatment is to cut away all that skin completely, especially if it has thinned and become like a rag, and you are certain that it will not adhere to the place because of its corruption.
But if you hope that the skin will adhere and the corruption has not reached that point, and there is a thickness of flesh in the skin, then treat it with injection. This is done by examining the fistula; if it is large and the pus that flows from it is foul-smelling, then inject it with the Egyptian medicine.
which is vinegar, oil, honey, and verdigris in equal parts, gathered in a vessel and cooked over a fire until the medicine thickens and reaches the consistency of honey. Then take what you need and thin it with water and honey, and inject it into the fistula, and bind its mouth, and leave the medicine in it for about two hours.
Then remove it by pressing; do this for several days until the fistula is cleansed and the foul smell is gone.  You may inject it with ash water if this medicine is not available to you.  This is made by taking the ash of grapevine wood or oak wood and pouring water over it.
Then filter it and inject it until you are certain that the fistula has been cleansed. If the patient cannot tolerate the Egyptian medicine or the ash water, then inject it with water and honey in which some powdered verdigris has been mixed.
Or inject it with honey and syrup mixed together, for honey cleanses and washes, and syrup adheres to the fistula, especially if the syrup has a binding and drying property.  When you have done this several times and are certain that the corruption of the fistula has gone, then inject it with something that will grow flesh in it.
Such as taking some palm ointment and dissolving it in rose oil and an astringent syrup, or inject it with some of the other medicines and ointments whose descriptions we have given in the chapter on ointments. If the opening of the fistula is narrow and does not admit the injection tube,
widen it slightly with an iron instrument, or place in it a pledget soaked in Egyptian ointment or theriac ointment until it widens. Similarly, if the opening is wide, bring its edges together with stitches, leaving only enough space to insert the injection tube.
Similarly, if the opening from which the pus flows is high up, incise it in the lowest point so that the pus may flow downwards, for if the pus is retained in the depth of the fistula it prevents the growth of flesh there.
If you cannot incise the fistula downwards as desired, then raise the limb so that the pus may flow easily, as seems best to you, so that nothing of the pus remains at all.
As for the cataplasms which are applied externally to the fistula and the manner of their application, take one of the suitable cataplasms and spread it on a linen cloth, the cloth being large enough to cover the whole fistula. Then cut with scissors opposite the opening of the fistula a hole slightly larger than the opening of the fistula; then spread some soft ointment on another cloth, to the size of the hole, and place it there, spaced away from the opening of the fistula, so that the pus may easily exit, and do not remove the larger cloth until afterwards.
several days; but that which is on the opening of the fistula, you remove at all times so that what has gathered there of pus may come out, and you may know what flows from it of pus: whether it is much or little, mature or immature.
And with this also, examine the site of the fistula itself: does its owner feel pain in it or not, and is there swelling in it or not?  If your treatment continues thus, and you see the matter which comes out is little at the opening of the fistula,
know that the fistula has adhered or is nearing adhesion; then at that time resolve it on the morrow or on the third day. And when you have opened the wound, change the cloths to others and renew the ointment. If, after a long time, thin pus comes out of the fistula, do not despair of its healing.
Its healing is near, for some people are slow in the growth of flesh in their wounds, and others the opposite. If, after many days, immature pus comes out of the fistula, know that the fistula has not healed. If the healing of the fistula is slow,
and its matter is prolonged, increase the drying medicines, and let them be medicines that are moist in their consistency and dry in their strength, like the palm ointment if an excess of litharge has been added to it. And one of the most effective things to treat it with is that the palm ointment is taken,
and it is dissolved with rose oil and sprinkled upon it with old wine moderate in its consistency, then knead it with it and use it.  He may do a similar action if the palm ointment is not available to you: honey, if you cook it until it thickens and use it; or you may take myrrh, aloes, and frankincense, crush them all together, and sprinkle them onto the honey while it is on the fire. Then spread it on a cloth and bind it to the fistula; or it may be spread with the honey which you have cooked until it thickens, and sprinkle the drugs on it, and place the cloth on it and bind it.
Long zedoary, the root of the asphodel, the powder of costus, and centaury may be used in such a case, singly if you use them, or in a mixture: crush and sieve them and sprinkle them on the honey as described and use it. If the matter of the fistula is prolonged and it does not heal with this treatment, know that there is corruption in its depth.
And if it has affected the bones, or the other hard nerves, or the ligaments, treat it as a fistula, as has been explained above.
Chapter eighty-nine.
On the treatment of the felon, the injured nail, the excision of the superfluous finger, and the separation of adherent fingers.
A felon is a fleshy growth that develops under the nail of the thumb of the hand or foot, and it may also develop in the other fingers. If its condition is prolonged and its treatment is neglected, it swells into a hot tumor.
Or it becomes corrupted and putrid until it eats away the root of the nail, and perhaps destroys it entirely, and perhaps the corruption reaches the bone, so that it has a foul odor, and the tip of the finger becomes wide, and its color becomes livid. If you treat it with what we have mentioned in the division, and your treatment is not effective,
it is necessary to cut off with an iron instrument all the superfluous growth that grows from the nail, then cauterize the wound afterwards; for cauterization is very beneficial in this case.  And if the bone is sound and the nail is also sound,
and the corner containing the nail has pinched the flesh inwards and is causing it to be lacerated and injured, it is necessary to place a thin pledget under the corner of the nail which lacerates the flesh, and raise it upwards and cut that flesh gently.
And you should apply to what remains of the flesh some of the cauterizing, corrosive drugs until it is all gone, then treat it with ointments until it heals.  And if the corruption has affected the bone, it is necessary to cut away that bone and remove it, for it will not heal at all as long as there is corrupted bone in it.
If you see that the corrosion and corruption is spreading in the finger, cut it off at one of the joints as mentioned before, then treat it until it heals. If the nail receives a blow or a bruise and severe pain occurs in it, it is necessary to bleed the patient first.
Then you incise the nail with a sharp scalpel, making an oblique incision from above downwards, and be careful not to reach with the incision the flesh which is under the nail; for by so doing you would cause the patient severe pain and it would be a cause of the growth of superfluous flesh in the place. Then treat the place with what alleviates pain.
As for the superfluous finger which grows in some people's hands, perhaps it is entirely fleshy, and perhaps it contains bones in some part, and perhaps it has a nail, and the growth of some of them is at the root of the joint of some fingers.
Or its growth may be in some of the joints of the finger. Those which grow in some of the joints of the finger do not move, and those which grow at the joint of the finger may move.  As for that which is only fleshy, its cutting is easy; that is to cut it at its base with a broad scalpel.
As for that whose growth is at the root of the joint, its treatment is difficult, so avoid cutting it. As for that which grows in the finger at one of the joints, it is necessary to first cut its flesh in a circular cut down to the bone, then saw the bone with one of those saws suitable for that.
Then treat it until it heals. As for the adhesion which occurs between the fingers, this often occurs, and it is something with which a person is born, or which happens from the healing of a wound or a burn by fire or the like. It is necessary to incise that adhesion.
until the fingers return to their natural form. Then place between them a pledget or a piece of lint soaked in rose oil, lest it quickly adhere. Separate them, or place between them a thin lead plate until the place heals as it should.
Similarly, if adhesion occurs between some of the fingers and the palm, incise that adhesion according to what is suitable and improves the shape of the limb.
Chapter Ninety.
On the excision of polyps.
Polyps are thick, twisted, varicose veins, dark in color, which occur in most parts of the body, most frequently in the legs, especially in the legs of those who carry burdens and those who work with their hands.  First, one should apply vigorous rubbing of the body with black hellebore.
Then bleed the patient from the basilic vein. As for treating them with iron, there are two ways: one is to incise and remove the black blood; the other is to extract the vein entirely.  As for incision, the leg should first be bathed in warm water.
until the thick, turbid blood becomes liquefied. Then the patient's leg is bound from above the thigh to below the knee with a bandage, and the vein is incised in one, two, or three places with a wide incision.
Then you extract the black blood with your hand from below the leg upwards and from above downwards, until you have removed the amount you deem sufficient, or what the patient's strength can bear. Then you bind it and tell him to avoid foods which produce black bile; and repeat the purging and bloodletting.
When the veins are full and this harms the patient. As for extracting it completely, this is how it is done: shave the patient's leg if there is much hair; then take him to the bathhouse and bathe his leg in hot water until it turns red and sweats, or until he performs vigorous exercise.
If a bathhouse is not available, until the limb is warmed, then you incise the skin along the vein lengthwise, either at its end near the knee or at its lower end near the heel; then open the skin with hooks and peel away the vein from every side until it is palpable.
And when it appears, you will see it is dark red; and when it is freed from the skin, you will see it also like a tendon. Then insert a probe beneath it until, when it is raised and emerges from the skin, you hang it on a smooth, blunt hook, then make another incision near that incision by the amount of three fingers.
Then peel the skin from over the vein until it appears, then raise it with the probe as you did before and hang it on another hook as you did initially; then make another incision, or many incisions, if you need to.
