Then extract it and cut it at the end of the incision near the heel, then pull it and extract it until it comes out of the second incision; then pull it to the incision above it, and do this until you pull it from the third incision, the highest of all the incisions, until when it has all emerged, you cut it.
If it does not respond to pulling and extraction, then insert a needle with strong doubled thread, and tie it and pull it, and insert the probe underneath and twist it with your hand to every side until it emerges; and take care that it does not break, for if it breaks, then its extraction will be very difficult for you.
And harm will come to the patient from it.  When you have extracted it completely, place on the site of the incisions wool soaked in a syrup and rose oil or olive oil, and treat it until it heals. If the varicose vein is tense, with convolutions and twisting in various directions…
and it is not straight as we said, then it is necessary that you make an incision on it at each part of its convolutions and places of twisting, then hang it on hooks until you extract it entirely; and be careful when incising it that you do not cut or injure the vein, for that will make its extraction difficult for you, so take care.
Image of the incision used to incise the varicose vein.
Image of the blind hook.
It should not be hooked like other hooks, nor should it be sharp-pointed, lest it injure the vein; and it should be thick, smoothly curved, for if it is thin it will cut the vein with its thinness; rather it should have a considerable thickness, as we said.
Chapter ninety-one.
On the treatment of varicose veins.
This vein is generated in the legs in hot countries like the Hejaz and the lands of the Arabs, and in hot, arid lands with little fertility; and it may be generated in other places in the body besides the legs, and its generation is due to an infection that occurs under the skin.
Just as living creatures and worms occur inside the bodies, and the swelling of gourds and worms generated between the skin and flesh, the sign of the beginning of the occurrence of this vein is that a severe inflammation occurs in the leg, then the place suppurates, and then the vein begins to emerge from the place of that suppuration.
As if it were the root of a plant or animal, so when its tip appears, it should be wrapped around a small piece of lead weighing from one to two dirhams, and tied, and the lead is left hanging from the leg.
So every time something emerges from it to the outside, wrap it in the lead and tie it.  If it becomes very long, cut some of it and wrap the remainder; but do not cut it from its root before it all emerges, because if you cut it, it will contract and enter the flesh, causing a swelling, infection in the place, and a bad ulcer.
Therefore, it should be treated carefully and pulled out little by little until it emerges completely and nothing of it remains in the body.  From this vein, in some people, what emerges can be five to ten cubits long.
And it has reached me that from a man twenty cubits emerged. If it breaks for you while you are treating it, then insert a probe into its hole and cauterize it deeply with the body until all that is in it of matter is emptied, and try to treat the infection with medicines for several days.
Then treat it with the treatment of tumors. This vein may have many branches, especially if it appears in a joint of the leg or in the leg itself, causing many openings, and from each opening a branch emerges. Treat it as we mentioned in the classification and what preceded.
Chapter ninety-two.
Concerning the incision for the worm that develops under the armpit and is called the cow's disease.
This disease is called in some of our countries the cow's disease, because it often affects cows. It is a single small worm that develops between the skin and flesh, and moves in the whole body, ascending and descending.
It is perceived by touch as it moves from one member to another, until it breaks through the skin wherever it breaks through, and emerges.  Its formation is from the corruption of some humors, as happens with worms, snakes, and gourd-like swellings in the abdomen.
Its harm is expected to be that if it moves in the body and ascends to the head and reaches the eye, it may open it and emerge, thus destroying the eye. This happens frequently. If you wish to treat it and remove it, this is done only when it moves and becomes perceptible to the touch.
It is necessary to tightly bind above and below it with a strong ligature, then incise over it and remove it. If it has sunk into the flesh and you cannot find it, apply cautery with fire to the area until you burn it. The greatest harm to be expected from it is, as we said, its damage to the eye.
If you see that it has reached the head near the eye, then bind tightly below it on the forehead, then incise over it and remove it. The patient should be treated by purifying his body with purgative medicines for bad, corrupt humors, and should abstain from foods that produce putrefaction.
Chapter Ninety-Three.
Concerning the incision for the disease known as the *nafir*.
This disease, which in our country is called *nafir*, is a pain that appears in some members and then moves from one member to another. I have seen it as I describe to you.
I was called to a sick woman in the countryside. She uncovered her arm and showed me a slight swelling in a vein of the forearm. After a short while, I saw that swelling move along the ulna like a worm, ascending to her shoulder as quickly as possible, like mercury.
Then, as it moved from place to place, the pain left that place and settled in the shoulder.  Then I waited a while, and it ran throughout the body until it was in the other arm. Then she told me that it circulates throughout her whole body, as I witnessed.
I was amazed at the speed of its movement from one member to another. I had never before seen this disease with my own eyes in this manner, except that I had seen several people whose pain moved from one member to another. However, I had not seen it with my own eyes as I saw it in this woman.
And I judged this to be only because the woman was from the countryside, with a dry body and visible veins; therefore, that moving wind was apparent to the senses. It would not appear in this way in those of comfortable circumstances, with moist bodies and hidden veins.
If you wish to treat it, and the patient feels pain, and if it appears to you visibly as we mentioned, then bind above and below it tightly, and incise it until that congested wind comes out, and cauterize the place.
But if you do not see it with your eyes, then treat it by means of things that dispel the body's humors and disperse the winds, such as the seeds of rue and the seeds of spikenard, and similar medicines.
Chapter ninety-four.
On extracting arrows.
Arrows differ according to their types and according to the places in the body where they are located. As for their differences according to their types, there are large ones and small ones, hollow glass ones and solid glass ones, and some have three points and some four.
Some have barbs and some have splinters. As for those according to the organs in which they are located, they are of two kinds: either the organs are hollow principal organs, such as the brain, heart, liver, lung, kidneys, intestines, bladder, and so on. If an arrow is lodged in one of these organs and you see the signs of death which I shall describe to you later, then you should avoid extracting that arrow from it, for in most cases death will overtake the patient. But if these bad signs do not appear and the arrow has not disappeared into the depth of the organ, then extract it and treat the wound.  Among the signs of an arrow reaching the brain and penetrating the bone and wounding the membrane covering the brain are severe headache, dizziness, and stupor.
But if these bad signs do not appear and the arrow has not disappeared into the depth of the organ, then extract it and treat the wound.  Among the signs of an arrow reaching the brain and penetrating the bone and wounding the membrane covering the brain are severe headache, dizziness, and stupor. And redness in the eyes, redness of the tongue, convulsions, mental confusion, vomiting, and sometimes blood comes from the nostrils or ears; sometimes speech is lost and the voice is gone, and a white moisture resembling gruel comes from the wound, and a watery substance like flesh flows from it.
If these signs appear, abstain from treating the patient and from removing the arrow if it has not already been removed.  As for the signs if the arrow reaches the heart and is near the left breast, you will feel as if it has been driven into something solid, not into something empty.
And perhaps the arrow will have a movement resembling the pulse, and black blood will flow from the wound, followed by coldness of the extremities, cold sweat, and fainting. Know then that death is inevitable.  A sign that the arrow has wounded the lung is the discharge of frothy blood from the wound.
The vessels near the neck swell, the patient's color changes, he breathes heavily, and seeks to inhale cold air. If the arrow reaches the diaphragm in the chest, it will be near the lower ribs, and breathing will be labored with severe pain.
He will sigh and all the muscles of the shoulders will move. If the arrow reaches the liver, severe pain will follow, and blood resembling the liver in redness will come from the wound. If the arrow reaches the stomach, something undigested from the food may come from the wound.
His condition is clear. If the arrow reaches the abdomen and becomes embedded there, and something from the stool, fat, or intestine which has been perforated comes from the wound, there is no hope for treatment or for removing the arrow. If the arrow reaches the bladder and urine comes out.
and something protrudes from it outwardly and the pain intensifies for the patient, know that he is doomed. As for the other organs, such as the face, eye, throat, shoulder, arm, vertebrae of the back, collarbone, thigh, leg, and similar organs, they are mostly spared.
When the arrow does not encounter an artery or nerve and the arrow is not poisoned. And I will tell you some of what I have witnessed concerning these arrows so that you may thereby deduce your treatment. That is that an arrow once struck a man in the corner of his eye at the root of the nose.
I removed it for him from the other side under the lobe of the ear and he recovered, and no harm befell his eye. And I removed another arrow for a Jew which had struck him in the lobe of his eye under the lower eyelid; and the arrow had disappeared, and I only reached the small end which is attached to the wood.
It was a large arrow from the composite bows, square-headed, smooth, without barbs.  The Jew recovered, and no ill befell his eye. And I removed another arrow from the throat of a Christian; and the arrow was an Arab arrow.
and it is the one with two barbs; so I made an incision between his jugular veins, and it had sunk into his throat. So I gently worked it out until I extracted it.  The Christian recovered and was healed. And I removed another arrow for a man which had struck him in the abdomen, and we judged that he would die from it.
But when thirty days or so had passed, and nothing in his condition had changed, I made an incision over the arrow and maneuvered it and extracted it, and he recovered and no ill befell him. And I saw a man struck by an arrow in his back, and the wound had closed over it.
Then after seven years the arrow emerged at the root of her thigh. And I saw a woman who had been struck in her abdomen by an arrow, and the wound had closed, and the arrow remained; and nothing in her condition changed, nor did she find any harm from it in any of her natural functions.
And I saw another man struck by an arrow in his face, and the wound closed, and he felt little pain from it. And there are many such cases. And I removed an arrow for a man from the Sultan's guard which had struck him in the middle of his nose, inclining slightly to the right side.
And the entire arrow was hidden. I was called to treat him three days after the arrow struck. I found the arrow wound very narrow, so I explored it with a thin probe, but I did not feel it. And he felt a stinging pain under his ear on the right side of the incision.
I hoped that this stinging pain was from the tip of the arrow, so I applied a dressing with drawing and ripening power, hoping that the area would swell and the arrow's location would be revealed so that I could incise it. But nothing happened in the area to indicate that the arrow had reached that place.
So I continued the dressing for many days, but nothing happened. Then the wound closed, and the patient despaired of its removal for a time, until one day he felt the arrow inside his nose and told me.  So I applied a strong, corrosive medicine to the wound for many days.
Until it opened, and I probed it, and I felt the thin tip of the arrow which adheres to the wood. Then I increased the opening of the wound with that strong medicine until the tip of the arrow appeared clearly to us.  And I spent about four months with him.
Then, when the wound widened and I was able to insert forceps into it, I pulled and moved it, but it did not respond to removal. So I continued to gently manipulate it and devise ways with various instruments until one day I grasped it with strong forceps, as its image is shown at the end of the chapter.
And the physicians judged that the cartilage of his nose would not heal, but I healed it, and the wound closed, and the patient recovered completely, without any further harm. And I tell you the method of removing some arrows so that you may make this a measure and a guide to what I have not mentioned.
Because the parts of this craft and its details cannot be grasped by description nor encompassed by a book.  Rather, the skillful craftsman judges from the little to the much, and from what is present to what is absent, and he deduces a new action and a new instrument in strange emergencies.
If we descend from this craft, I say that arrows are removed from the members in which they are embedded in two ways: either by pulling from the place where they entered, or from the opposite direction, and those that are removed from where they entered.
Either the arrow is protruding in a fleshy place, and it is pulled out. If it does not yield to removal from the place where it lodged, then you should leave it for a few days until the flesh around it rots, so that it is easier to pull out and remove. Likewise if it is embedded in a bone.
And if it does not yield to removal, leave it also for days and return to pulling and moving it every day, for it will come out.  If it does not yield to removal after days, then you should bore around the arrow in the bone itself from every side with a delicate borer until you widen [the hole] for the arrow, then pull and remove it.
If the arrow embedded in the skull has penetrated deeply into one of the ventricles of the brain and some of those symptoms I mentioned to you appear in the patient, then refrain from pulling the arrow and leave it until its condition improves after some days.
then persevere in pulling out the arrow. If it is very deeply embedded and does not yield to pulling, use the borers around the arrow as I described to you, then treat the area until it heals.  But if the arrow has disappeared into a part of the body, and is hidden from sensation, probe for it with a probe. If you feel it, pull it out with some instrument suitable for pulling it out. If you cannot reach it because of the narrowness of the wound and the depth of the arrow, and there is no bone, nerve, or vein there,
then enlarge the wound until you can reach the arrow and remove it. If it has barbs, grasp them, and free the flesh embedded in them from every side by any means you can. Persist, and if you cannot free the flesh, break off the barbs and twist them until you are free.
And when you attempt to remove the arrow from any place, use the forceps to twist your hand in all directions until you free it.  Be extremely gentle, lest the arrow break and it becomes difficult for you to pull it out. If you cannot manage it at once,
leave it for a few days until the flesh around it decays, then try again; it will be easier then. If bleeding occurs, use the treatments we mentioned in that chapter.  Guard carefully against cutting a vein, nerve, or tendon, and use skill.
By any means you can free the arrow, and let this be done gently and carefully, and firmly as I have described to you. It is necessary that when you pull out the arrow, you position the patient in the same position as when the arrow struck, for this is most suitable.
If you cannot do that, use whatever positions you can. As for an arrow that exits on the opposite side, either something of it will have emerged outwards, or you will feel the tip of the arrow under the skin nearby and see it protruding.
Make an incision there, and let the incision be large enough for the forceps. Then pull it out; it will be easy to remove. If it is stuck in a bone, twist your hand around until the arrow affects the bone and widens a space for itself; then pull it out.
Otherwise, leave it for some days, then try again until it comes out. If the arrow shaft is still in place, push it with that. If the shaft has fallen off and you want to use pushing, insert the hollow instrument so that its hollow enters the arrowhead, then push it with that.
If the arrow is hollow, push it with an instrument that enters that hollow; the arrow will then easily come out. If the arrow is poisoned, you should, if possible, cut away all the flesh that has been poisoned, then treat it with what is suitable for that.
If the arrow is lodged in the chest, or abdomen, or bladder, or flank, and is close to where it can be felt with a probe, and you can incise it, then incise it, being careful not to cut a vein or nerve, and remove it.
Then stitch the wound if it needs stitching, and treat it until it heals.  Image of the forceps with which arrows are extracted:
Their ends are like a bird's beak; they are made like a file, so that when they grasp an arrow or anything, they do not let go.  Large, small, and medium sizes of these can be made, according to the size of the arrow, and the width or narrowness of the wound.
Image of the hollow pusher:
Image of the solid pusher:
This has a solid end like an awl, to facilitate its entry into and pushing of the hollow arrow.
Chapter ninety-five.
On phlebotomy.
The veins usually opened in the body number thirty; of these, sixteen are in the head: the two pulsating veins behind the ears, known as the "whispering" veins; the two arteries in the temples, which are visible; and the two veins at the inner canthus of the eyes, known as the "seeing" veins.
And the vein erect in the middle of the forehead; and the vein at the tip of the nose; and the two jugular veins in the neck; and the two veins in the upper lip; and the two veins in the lower lip—these veins are known as the "bleeding veins"; and the two veins under the tongue.
As for the veins opened in the arm and hand, they are five: one is the cephalic, which is on the outer side, and the common people call it the vein of the head; and the median cubital vein, which is the middle vein and is composed of a branch of the basilic and a branch of the cephalic, and the common people call it the vein of the body.
And the basilic, which is situated on the inner side, and is also called the axillary; and the common people call it the vein of the abdomen; and the vein of the forearm, which is situated on the ulna, and to which it adheres, and which appears clearly above the thumb; and the salem.
And this is the vein between the little finger and the ring finger; it has two branches. And in the leg and foot there are three veins: one is that which is below the hollow of the knee on the outer side; the second is the saphenous, and its place is at the heel on the inner side; and the vein of the sciatic nerve, and its place is at the heel on the outer side.
And in the other leg there are three similar veins. As for the two veins behind the ears, the benefit of opening them is for chronic catarrhs, migraine, and herpes, and chronic bad sores of the head; and the manner of opening them is as I describe.
And that is to shave the head of the patient and rub the back of his head in the place of the two veins with a rough cloth, rubbing well; then the patient's neck is constricted with a turban until the two veins appear, and their place is behind the ears in the two low places of the head; then you feel for them with your finger, and where you feel their pulsation under your finger, there you mark with ink.
Then you take a lancet-like scalpel, which is known as the *nashal*, then you insert it under the vein in the skin until the scalpel reaches the bone; then you raise your hand with the vein and the skin upwards and cut the vein with the skin a cut, amputating it; and the length of the cut is the amount of two fingers joined together.
Or something similar, and you let out the amount of blood you want, then you bind them with bandages and leave them until they heal.  And cutting them and cauterizing them has already been mentioned at the beginning of the book. As for the two arteries which are in the temples, the benefit of opening them is for chronic migraine, severe headache, persistent ophthalmia, and the flow of sharp humors directed towards the eyes.
And the manner of opening them is as I describe to you: the patient constricts his neck with a turban until the veins appear clearly to the touch, and their pulsation is apparent under your finger; then you mark with ink. Then you raise the skin from above the vein upwards with your index finger.
And you insert the *nashal* scalpel from below and raise the vein upwards and amputate it as you did with the other two veins, and you let out blood according to your need; then you loosen the patient's constriction and place your finger on the vein for a while.
Then you place cotton and a bandage on it and bind it tightly from above and leave it until it heals.  Their mention, cutting and cauterization have already been discussed at the beginning of the book. As for opening the vein of the forehead, its benefit, after opening the vein of the nape of the neck, is for chronic facial ailments such as ringworm, ulcers, and erysipelas.
And the manner of opening it, as I describe to you: the patient constricts his neck with a turban until the vein appears; then you take the instrument called the hatchet, and this is its image:
You place the projecting spike which is in the head of the hatchet on the vein itself and strike from above with a comb or something similar, and you leave the blood to flow to the extent that you desire; then you loosen the patient's constriction and bind it until it heals.
It may also be opened with a wide scalpel, except that the scalpel should not be sharp-pointed like other scalpels, but should be slightly wide-ended, and you incise it obliquely, because the bone is near, so the scalpel might break if it is thin.
As for the two veins which are in the temples, their benefit is for eye ailments such as ringworm, redness, and blepharitis, and facial diseases. As for the manner of opening them, it is that the patient constricts his neck with a turban, then you open them while you are standing over his head, and let the incision be slightly obliquely lengthwise with a small, slightly wide scalpel.
For the location has no flesh; if the scalpel is thin-pointed, it might break. Then you let out the amount of blood you need and place cotton on them and bind them for one night, then unbind them. As for opening the vein of the nose, it is beneficial for acute fevers.
And severe headache and facial diseases like erysipelas which appears in the nose, especially if it is chronic.  The method of opening it is that the patient constricts his neck, then you grasp his nose with your left hand and take a thin, long scalpel.
And you pierce it in the middle of the nasal dorsum itself, between the nasal septum, in a straight line, because the vein does not appear to the touch there; then the blood emerges immediately. You should move your hand with the scalpel a little and let out the amount of blood you need, then bind it for a night, for it heals quickly.
As for the jugulars, the benefit of opening them is for shortness of breath, the onset of leprosy, and melancholic diseases which appear on the surface of the skin such as black vitiligo, scabies, bad ulcers and cankers.  The method of opening them is that the patient constricts under them in his neck with a bandage, and the surgeon stands at the head of the patient.
And the patient sits on a chair, then you open the vein lengthwise with a slightly wide incision, then you let out a moderate amount of blood, or according to what you see as necessary. Then you do likewise with the other vein, then you untie the bandage.
And you tie the veins moderately so that the patient does not suffocate, and you leave him until the next day, for the wound will heal. As for the veins of the temples, the benefit of opening them, after opening the cephalic vein, is that it benefits from cankers in the mouth, corruption of the gums, bad ulcers, cracking of the lips, and bad ulcers.
which are in and around the nose.  The method of opening them is that you seat the patient before you and bind his neck with a turban, then you turn back his lips and look at the two veins, one of which you see to the right of the lip and the other to the left, and you ascertain them by their blackness.
And that is also because around them are small, black veins, so you cut them with a severed cut. If it is difficult for you and you do not know what they are, then aim to cut the largest and clearest of them.  Likewise, do with the two veins in the lower lip.
As for the two veins under the tongue, the benefit of opening them after opening the cephalic vein is for the inflammations that occur in the throat from the disease of the uvula and diseases of the mouth. The method of opening them is that you seat the patient in your hands facing the sun, and raise his tongue, and look under the tongue, on one side a vein and on the other side a vein, and their color is towards blackness.
Then you open them, and be careful not to go too far in cutting them, for beneath them are arteries, and perhaps bleeding from those arteries may occur. As for the three veins which are opened in the elbow, they are those which custom has established in opening them in all people, and their opening is in two ways.
Either by piercing with a wide, wind-shaped or olive-shaped lancet to the soft part, or by cutting with a knife-shaped lancet, which is the *nashal* [and this is its image], the image of the wide, wind-shaped lancet:
It is wide, as you see, so that it is suitable for opening hollow, full, prominent, apparent, thick veins, and those which contain thick, turbid blood. And this is the image of the olive-shaped lancet:
And this lancet is less wide and thinner at the point, suitable for opening thin veins which contain thin, yellowish blood. And this is the image of the *nashal* lancet:
This *nashal*, which is suitable for cutting, and there are types of it, wide and narrow, according to the width and narrowness of the veins also. And one may infer from this to others, and it is well-known among the artisans.
As for the basilic vein, which is one of these three veins, the benefit of opening it is that it draws blood from the illnesses which are under the throat and neck, from what is near the chest and belly.  It is necessary for the phlebotomist, when opening it, to beware of it and be careful about its neck, for under it is an artery. If he errs and goes too far in piercing with the lancet, he will cut that artery.
Then bleeding will occur; therefore, it is necessary that its opening not be done with a lancet for piercing, but its opening should be a cut with the *nashal*. If the basilic vein does not appear clearly, then it is necessary to avoid it and turn to another, or seek one of its branches.
Or open in its place the vein of the arm, for it is clear, and cut it with the *nashal* lancet as we said. If you wish to open it itself, then before tightening the arm, it is necessary to feel the place until you recognize the place of the pulse, then mark it with ink.
Then you tie the arm and cut the vein with a sharp cut with the *nashal* lancet, as we said, and be careful that the blow falls away from the place of the artery. And when you see, upon tightening the ligature, swelling in the place which you marked with ink, then that swelling is the swelling of the artery, so avoid it.
If you see the blood, upon opening, leaping as a boy's urine leaps, and the blood is thin and red, then know that it is from the artery. Then immediately place your finger on it for a long time, then remove your finger. If the blood stops, it often stops, then tighten the arm.
And leave it. Warn the patient against neglecting it, and let him be in bed and not move for days until it heals. If the blood does not stop and it overcomes you and you do not have medicine at hand, then amputate the artery if it appears to you, for its ends contract and the blood stops.
Or take a pistachio shell and split it, and take one half and bind it tightly on the place of the vein with a ligature and bandages until the next day. If the blood stops, otherwise treat it with what was previously mentioned of placing styptics, and stopping its blood is not difficult in most cases.
Because of the smallness of the wound and the tightness of the ligature in the arm, inform him of that. As for the cephalic vein, the benefit of opening it is that it draws blood from the top of the head and the lower body, because it is composed of a branch of the basilic and a branch of the cephalic, as we said.
It is necessary for the one who opens it to be careful in opening it, for there is a nerve beneath it. If he goes too deep with the lancet and injures the nerve, numbness occurs which is difficult to treat, and perhaps it will not heal at all. This nerve is often perceptible to the touch.
If it is hidden in some people and is thin and cannot be discerned, then you should make your incision in it with a lancet and avoid the nerve as much as possible. If the vein is between two nerves, then split the vein lengthwise. As for the cephalic vein, the benefit of opening it is that it draws blood from the head.
It is beneficial for diseases of the eyes, and it is necessary in this vein especially, if you wish to open it, to make the incision with the olive-shaped lancet or with the broad, basil-shaped lancet, because it is the safest of all veins, because there is neither an artery nor a nerve beneath it.
Except that it is necessary for you, when opening it, to avoid the head of the muscle only with the lancet and seek the soft place. It does not harm him if he is not struck on the first attempt, to be opened again repeatedly, except that it may swell in some people if it is not opened on the first attempt.
But that swelling does not harm him at all. As for the method of opening and its complications and what should be done beforehand in its treatment, the first thing is that you should know that opening is used either only to preserve health.
Or it is used in illnesses.  That which is used to preserve health and maintain it and to guard against the occurrence of illnesses is to open one of the three veins in the forearm, I mean the cephalic, the median, and the basilic, and to open it at the beginning of spring if signs of plethora appear.
And the opening should be on Sunday or Tuesday after three hours of the day have passed. As for the opening which is used in illnesses, it has no limited time, but whenever necessity and need dictate it, night or day.
And at every hour and at every time. Children should not be opened until they reach fourteen years, nor old people who have passed sixty years. If someone intends to open for any reason whatsoever,
he should cleanse his bowels before opening with a gentle enema if there is much retained fecal matter, lest putrid excess from the bowels be drawn into the veins during opening and harm the principal organs.  And do not open one who is feverish, drunk or intoxicated until that passes from them.
And also avoid opening after dysentery, vomiting, diarrhea, excessive coitus, exertion, exercise, wakefulness, fasting, and everything that weakens the strength, whether physical or mental. Then look to thinning the humors beforehand if the blood is thick.
with foods, drinks, and medicines if he is able to do so; then he should enter the bath if nothing prevents him, or engage in some exercise so that the blood is thinned, and make his opening in the middle of the day as we said, and he should aim to free his chest that day from all bad mental affections.
such as worries, anger, and fear, and from all physical affections like excessive exertion and toil, coitus, and the like. And he should have at his disposal the things that people are accustomed to using, such as various perfumes, aromatic plants, entertainment, and the like, each person according to his means.
Then the phlebotomist sits on a cushion higher than the cushion on which the patient sits, then he extends his arm and the phlebotomist rubs it with his hand two or three times, then he tightens the ligature with the fillet and twists it two or three times, and let the tightening be moderate.
For if the tightening is not moderate, either by excessive tightening, it prevents the flow of blood, or if it is relaxed, it also prevents the flow of blood. Then, after tightening, the patient should rub both his hands together.
until the veins swell and become perceptible to the touch. Then the phlebotomist wipes the lancet with a little old oil, especially, then he places the index finger of his left hand on the vein itself a little below the place which he wishes to open, lest the vein slip away and avoid the incision. For some veins are like tendons, they slip away during phlebotomy, and some are filled with wind, so when the lancet is placed upon them, they sink beneath the lancet, deceiving the phlebotomist, and the lancet does not open the vein, or if it does open it, it is only a narrow opening.
Therefore, the phlebotomist should be careful and deliberate in all these matters, then he lowers the lancet; if the vein is opened on that attempt, well and good; otherwise, he repeats it another time a little below or above that place quickly if the place is not swollen.
If the patient swells or is frightened, leave him for a day or two and do not tighten the ligature, for it may bring on a hot swelling, and he should not enter the bath, then he may repeat the phlebotomy if he wishes. If the lancet is inserted and the opening is small and the flow of blood is thin,
and you fear that the desired quantity of blood will not be drawn, replace the lancet in the same opening gently and straight, and increase the opening slightly, and do this quickly before the place swells, for in many people the place may swell with a small opening.
But if you see that it has swollen, do not repeat it at all, for it will not help you; put on it some oil dregs, for it facilitates the flow of blood, and it is better in this case than oil itself and other ointments as well.
Therefore, use oil dregs in all your phlebotomies when the flow of blood is difficult.  The theriac Pharun and the  shakhznaja may also do this if one of them is placed on the area, for the blood will thin and dissolve if it is thick.
If a large swelling occurs at the site of phlebotomy—and this often happens, especially to those who have been phlebotomized only once or whose opening of the vein was small—act quickly and place on the swelling a sponge soaked in slightly warmed salt water and bind it for an hour, for it will resolve.
And it is necessary that you do this after the blood has completely exited from the vein itself or from another vein. If something of blackness or greenness remains in the area after several days, it does no harm. If you wish, apply something of aloes and bitter substances which have been dissolved.
Or something of the juice of the foudanj and the like.  Often a swelling and a pustule occur when phlebotomizing the basilic vein; place your hand upon it. If you find that it softens when you gently touch it, then it is a bad pustule, and beware of putting anything on it of what we have mentioned.
For perhaps arterial blood will flow from it; but compress it with something astringent to constrict the area, then treat it with other treatments until it heals.  It is necessary that you extract from each person a quantity of blood according to his strength and what appears of the color dominating the blood.
For if the blood is black, let it flow until it becomes red; and likewise if you see it thick, let it flow until it thins; and likewise if it is sharp, until its sharpness is gone.  It is necessary for one who is full-bodied and strong, and needs to have blood removed at once,
to widen the incision in the vein and the lancet to be wide. And for one who is weak, the opposite. And it is necessary to extract it in stages, and the opening to be narrow. And the best thing to use in phlebotomy is a sharp, well-sharpened, incised, not pierced, instrument.
And this type of phlebotomy is safe from bleeding and from cutting a nerve; and it is better and safer than phlebotomy across or lengthwise. And if anyone is accustomed to fainting during phlebotomy, it is necessary that you feed him before phlebotomy something of bread soaked in pomegranate water or oxymel.
If he is feverish and blood is extracted from him in three or four times, then if he is of cold temperament, let him take before phlebotomy bread soaked in hydromel or in honeyed syrup seasoned with aromatics or in pleasantly-scented syrup.
If fainting occurs during phlebotomy and its cause is the copious outflow of blood, then it is necessary to give him meat broth and thin aromatic syrup, and to use perfumes of gālīyā and to rub his chest with it, and to use all that we have mentioned in the section on fainting.
which results from vomiting. And as for one who wishes to relieve his arm and to let his blood flow again, it is necessary for one whose phlebotomy was for copious evacuation and whose strength is weak, that the blood be let little by little according to his strength on consecutive days.
And as for one who wishes to relieve his arm and to let his blood flow again and his body is strong, let him do that after seven or nine hours from his first phlebotomy. And as for one who wishes to draw the blood from his body to the opposite side to which it inclined, it is necessary to relieve him on the second or third day.
And as for one in whose body the blood is abundant, has become hot and sharp and has caused fever, it is necessary to extract the blood from him in one go and to extract a large quantity and to widen the opening until fainting occurs, after you have checked all the conditions for phlebotomy. And that you place your hand on his pulse during the flow of blood, lest death occur instead of fainting, for that often happens if the phlebotomist is ignorant and negligence occurs. And it is not necessary, if you wish to loosen the arm and let the blood flow again,
and the mouth of the vein has closed and the exit of blood has become difficult, that you prick it forcefully or twist it strongly, for that is very bad; rather, either you leave it until you phlebotomize it again, or you remove with the blade of the lancet what has solidified of the blood in the mouth of the vein.
Or you apply to it something of salt dissolved in water, or you apply to it something of theriac faruq or shakhzanaya and prick it gently until the blood comes out; and if the vein has swollen, leave it.
And do not touch it until the swelling subsides; and if necessity dictates letting the blood flow again and it is unavoidable, either phlebotomize above that place or phlebotomize in the other arm or in another vein.
And as for phlebotomizing the vein of the arm, it is phlebotomized instead of the basilic and cephalic veins if they are not found or are hidden, because it is composed of them; and its method of phlebotomy is that the patient puts his hand in hot water until the forearm reddens and the vein appears clearly, then it is tied above it with a ligature slightly with moderate tightness.
Then you phlebotomize the vein at a slight angle, neither across nor lengthwise, and let the opening be wide, and let your phlebotomy be slightly above the joint of the hand; and if the exit of blood is difficult, return the hand to the vessel with the hot water and let the blood flow into the water.
Until it reaches your need; and if you are in the summer days, you may dispense with returning the hand to the hot water, and most of what causes the blood to flow in hot water is in the wintertime; and phlebotomizing this vein is safer than all veins. Because there is no intersecting vein or nerve under it; and as for phlebotomizing the vein of the right hand, it is beneficial for ailments of the liver; and its method of phlebotomy is that you tie the wrist with a ligature or with your hand after you put it in hot water until the vein swells.
Because there is no intersecting vein or nerve under it; and as for phlebotomizing the vein of the right hand, it is beneficial for ailments of the liver; and its method of phlebotomy is that you tie the wrist with a ligature or with your hand after you put it in hot water until the vein swells. And it becomes very apparent to the touch, then you phlebotomize it at a slight angle; and even if you sever it completely, it will not harm him at all; and be careful not to plunge your hand with the lancet, for under it is the nerve of the fingers, and the place is bare of flesh; then return the hand to the hot water.
And leave it to flow the blood in it; for if you do not return it to the hot water, the blood will congeal in the mouth of the vein and cease flowing; and when you have removed the amount of blood needed, place on the vein fat and salt lest it close quickly.
And similarly you should do with every narrow branch; and as for the benefit of phlebotomizing from the left hand, it is beneficial for ailments of the spleen; and similarly you do in its phlebotomy as you did in the second, the same; and as for phlebotomizing the saphenous vein, its benefit is for the diseases in the lower body, such as ailments of the uterus.
And retention of menstruation, and diseases of the kidneys, and chronic ulcers of the thighs and legs, and similar diseases; and its method of phlebotomy is that he puts his leg in hot water and rubs it until the veins swell, then you tie above the joint of the leg with a ligature, and the vein is located at the heel, apparent toward the thumb.
And from it branch out in the front of the leg many branches; so phlebotomize in its widest branch, or at the heel at its confluence, for that is best and safest; for if you phlebotomize in the front of the leg, then be careful of the nerves which are under it on the front of the leg.
And make your incision in it by twisting, as if you intend to amputate it, and let the scalpel be sharp; and if the exit of the blood is difficult, let him return his leg to the hot water, and leave the blood to flow in it until it is emptied; and if the phlebotomist misses the vein in the first attempt,
let him return with the phlebotomy a little higher, for the place is safe, and there is no fear of harm from it if you are careful of the nerve, as we said; and similarly you do with the saphenous vein of the other leg, the same; and as for the sciatic vein, its place is, as we said, at the heel from the outer side.
And the benefit of its phlebotomy is for hip pain, if that is from hot blood; and its method of phlebotomy is that you put the patient in the bath and hurry, and tie his leg from the hip to above the heel by four fingers with a thin long bandage, for it does not appear except by that.
Then, if it appears, phlebotomize it in any way that you please, either by twisting—and that is best—or by amputating it, or by incising it, for its place is safe, and it is in most people very hidden; and if you do not find it,
and it does not appear at all to the touch, phlebotomize some of its branches, which are those that appear on the back of the foot towards the little finger and the ring finger, and be careful of the nerves, and let out the amount of blood that you want.
Then untie the bandage and place cotton on the place of phlebotomy and bandage the place, for it quickly heals.
Chapter ninety-six.
Concerning cupping and its method of use.
Cupping instruments may be made of horn, wood, copper, or glass, and cupping is of two types: one is cupping with incision and the extraction of blood, and the other is cupping without incision.
And this cupping without incision is of two types.
And the cupping of the mastoids, which are the sides of the neck on both sides, and the cupping of the chin, which is below the lower jaw, and the cupping of the shoulders, and the cupping of the coccyx on the rump, and the cupping of the forearms, which are the middles of the arms, and the cupping of the legs, and the cupping of the ankles.
Cupping draws blood from the small veins scattered in the flesh; and for that reason, it does not weaken the body as bloodletting does, nor should cupping, whether with fire or without, be used in any disease caused by plethora until the whole body is purged.
If cupping is needed because of illness or habit, we use it at any time, at the beginning of the month, at the end of it, in the middle, or at any time.
That is because some people, when their blood increases to the point that it needs to be removed by cupping, feel heaviness and headache; and some feel fullness and redness in their face, head, and neck; and some feel itching in their face and sides, and darkness and burning in their eyes; and some itch at the site of their cupping.
And some laugh a lot; and some find the taste of blood in their mouth, and their gums swell, and they spit blood; and some sleep a lot; and some see blood, redness, pus, and wounds in their sleep, and things like that.  Whenever we see any of that...
especially if it is in the middle third of the month, we then order cupping after two or three hours of the day have passed. As for the benefit of cupping the temples, it benefits from heaviness in the head and water pouring into the eyes.
But it should be after emptying the whole body. This cupping can be a substitute for phlebotomy of the cephalic vein. It is cautioned against using it for those who are cold-headed or have a cold, for it harms them greatly.
Therefore, the elderly and those with cold diseases in their heads should not use it, and those who overuse it will develop forgetfulness.
Therefore, you should instruct the cupper to lower his hand with the cupping glass a little lower, for fear of generating forgetfulness. As for cupping the nape of the neck, it is a substitute for phlebotomy of the basilic and cephalic veins.
Therefore, it benefits from asthma, shortness of breath, rupture of the respiratory apparatus, cough, and fullness.  The cupping of the nape of the neck should be raised a little, for if it is lowered, it will produce weakness in the heart and stomach.
As for cupping the temples, it benefits from headaches, ophthalmia, migraine, angina, and toothache. It is a substitute for phlebotomy of the basilic vein. You should instruct the cupper not to deepen his hand in the incision, lest he cut a vein and cause bleeding.
As for cupping under the chin, it benefits from thrush in the mouth, corruption of the gums, and similar diseases in the mouth. It may substitute for phlebotomy of the labial veins. As for cupping the shoulders, it benefits from palpitations that result from fullness and heat.
As for cupping the inner sides of the forearms, it benefits from what benefits phlebotomy of the three veins, the basilic, cephalic, and median cephalic, because it draws blood from all those small veins in the flesh and draws those small veins from other thicker veins until the drawing reaches the three large veins.
You should instruct the cupper not to deepen the incision because the area is devoid of flesh and underneath are nerves and arteries. As for single cupping applied to the coccyx, it benefits from hemorrhoids and lower ulcers.
You should instruct the cupper that the cupping glass should be large and made of copper, because the area needs strong suction, and a glass cupping glass might break and require many incisions. As for cupping the legs, it visibly reduces fullness.
Because it draws blood from the whole body and benefits from chronic pains in the kidneys, uterus, and bladder, it promotes menstruation, and benefits from boils and abscesses. It substitutes for phlebotomy of the saphenous veins and the veins of the ankles, but it greatly weakens the body and causes fainting in most people. Cupping the ankles has benefits similar to cupping the legs.
The method of applying cupping glasses is to first place the empty cupping glass and apply moderate suction. Do not prolong the application of the cupping glasses, but place and remove them quickly so that the humors are drawn to the area evenly. Continue to repeat this until you see the area redden and open.
When the redness of blood appears, then make an incision and resume suction slowly. Then consider the condition of the bodies. If a person has loose flesh and porous skin, you should only make one incision so that the area does not ulcerate.
Instruct the cupper to widen the incision and deepen it slightly, and to adjust the suction gently and with a light movement. If the blood is thick, then two incisions should be made; the first to open a path for the thin and watery blood, and the second to thoroughly remove the thick blood.
If the blood is very thick, repeat the incision a third time to achieve the desired result. In general, if we want to remove a small amount of blood, one incision suffices; if we want to remove a large amount of blood, we make more incisions, especially if we consider the blood to be thick.
It is necessary to make a deep incision, and the moderate limit of the incision is only the depth of the skin. What ointments should be used when applying cupping glasses, and what waters, and what the cupper should avoid. As for someone whose skin is thick, hard, dry, and with tight pores,
the areas where the cupping glasses will be applied should be anointed with opening, softening, and resolving ointments. If it is summer, then use rose ointment, violet ointment, sweet almond oil, or pumpkin seed oil.
And if it is winter, then use narcissus oil, iris oil, chamomile oil, or lily oil, and the like. If the waste matter is thick and cold, then let the ointment be marjoram oil, balm oil, sesame oil, or rue oil, and the like.
If the patient has wide pores and soft flesh, then the ointment should be avoided. These people should wash their cupping sites after cupping with rose water, cold water, fox grape water, gourd water, or chickweed water, and the like. As for someone whose blood is very moist,
he should wash his cupping sites with vinegar, or with water of  asafoetida and sumac, and the like. As for someone whose waste matter is thick, he should wash his cupping sites with old wine, or with marjoram water, or a decoction of rue or chamomile, and the like. Cupping should be avoided in the bathhouse and immediately after bathing.
Rather, it should be done an hour or two after leaving the bathhouse. No one should sleep after cupping. What should be done for the patient before and after cupping.
First, one must look. If the patient is choleric and his blood is predominantly sharp and inflamed, then he should take cooling things such as pomegranate, dandelion with vinegar, lettuce, oxymel, and sharbat, and the like.  His food should include poultry, lamb stew, and sour things, and the like.
And if his temperament is cold, then he should be given honeyed drinks, or a drink of dates or seed oxymel. He should consume moderately sweet wine, that which is between old and new. He should be ordered to eat little, and his food should be poultry,  quails, songbirds, and squabs in a stew.  Drink should be more plentiful than food on the day of cupping and phlebotomy.
It may be necessary in some cases for some people to be given Mithridatium, or musk medicine, or theriac before cupping and phlebotomy, or afterward, to strengthen the principal organs and thin the blood.  Those with fever should not be given this.
As for cupping without scarification, this is cupping applied to the liver, spleen, breasts, abdomen, navel, kidney region, and hip joint, because these organs do not tolerate scarification.  Rather, the aim is to draw blood from one organ to another.
Such as placing the cupping glass on the breasts for epistaxis, or using it to draw out cold wind which has lodged in an organ, such as placing the cupping glass on the abdomen and navel. This loosens the organ, warms it, and removes the pain by resolving that wind.
It may be placed on the kidneys if there is an obstruction or stone there, for by its drawing power it may open the obstruction or dislodge the stone. Similarly, it acts if placed on the liver and spleen in the case of wind that troubles them.  These cuppings may be used empty, by suction alone.
It may be used with fire, or it may be used filled with lukewarm water for ailments of the chest.  That is, fill the cupping glass, and let it be a large one, with hot water alone, or with water in which some herbs suitable for this purpose have been boiled.
Then, filled, it is placed on the affected area, held, removed, and repeated as needed.  This is an image of the cupping glass used with fire:
Its mouth should be the width of two open fingers, as we have pictured, and its depth half a span.  There should be a small hole in its side, about halfway down, large enough for a needle to enter. It is made of Chinese copper or yellow copper, with a thick rim.
Smooth, even, and polished, so that it does not injure the member when placed upon it. In its center there is a transverse groove of copper or iron, where the burning candle is placed. This cupping glass may be made larger than we have described, or smaller, according to the disease.
And we will explain its use, for the cupping glasses of children and thin people are not the same as those of men and stout-bodied people. As for the method of applying this cupping glass with fire to the member, it is to prepare a burning wick of tightly-woven linen or a small candle of pitch, and to place it on the middle of the hardened rod which is in the middle of the cupping glass.
So that the fire ascends upwards towards the bottom of the cupping glass, so that the patient's body is not burned. Then it is placed on the member, with the finger on the hole we mentioned, so that when we have held the cupping glass as long as necessary, the finger is removed.
And the vapor escapes through that hole, and the cupping glass adheres to the place. Then prepare the wick in the same way and reapply it if necessary.  As for the cupping glass which is used in the treatment of phlegmon with water, it has neither a hardened rod nor a hole.
It is only used by being filled with water and placed on the member. This is its image:
And this cupping glass, the larger it is to hold much water, the better it is.
Chapter ninety-seven.
On the suspension of the leech.
Leeches are used mostly in cases where cupping glasses cannot be applied, either because of smallness, like the lip and gum and the like, or because the member is bare of flesh, like the finger and nose and the like.
And the manner of using them is that you obtain leeches which are in fresh, pure waters, free from impurities; then leave them a day and a night in fresh water until they are hungry and nothing remains in their bellies. Then the body is first purged by bloodletting or cupping, then the affected member is wiped.
Until it reddens, then it is placed upon it; and when it is full, it falls off. And if it is possible to suck the place with a cupping glass, that is more effective; otherwise, wash the place with vinegar, then with much water, and rub and squeeze it. If the bleeding continues after the leech falls off, and this is a trickle, then let a linen cloth be wetted in cold water.
And place it on top until the trickle stops. If the blood is profuse, sprinkle on it powdered vitriol or alum or the like of astringents until the bleeding stops, or place on the affected area halves of peeled lupins.
And leave it until the lupin adheres to the place, for then the blood will stop.  And it is preferable, if it is necessary to reapply leeches, that those leeches not be reapplied if others are available. If the leeches refuse to adhere, then let the place be wiped with fresh blood.
Or pierce a needle into the place until some blood comes out, then apply them; for when they sense some blood, they will adhere in place.  If you wish to remove them, sprinkle on them some aloes or salt or ashes, for then they will fall off immediately.
Chapter Three.
On setting (bones).
This chapter also contains what is necessary in the art of medicine, which is the setting of fractures and dislocations occurring in bones.
Know, my sons, that this chapter is claimed by the ignorant among physicians and the common people, and by those who have never perused any book of the ancients on it nor read a single letter thereof.
For this reason, this art of knowledge has become extinct in our country, and I have never encountered anyone at all who is skilled in it. Rather, I derived what I have derived from it through my long reading of the books of the ancients and my eagerness to understand them, until I extracted knowledge of it from them, and then I adhered to experimentation and practice throughout my life.
And I have drawn for you in this chapter all that my knowledge encompasses. And my experience has followed upon it after I have brought it close to you and freed it from the ramifications of prolixity, and I have abridged it to the utmost, and clarified it to the utmost, and I have depicted for you therein many pictures of the instruments that are used in it.
For it is an increase in explanation, as I did in the preceding two chapters. And there is no strength except in God, the Most High, the Magnificent.
Chapter One.
In it are summaries and compendia concerning the matter of fractured bones, and it is necessary to present them. And before we begin to mention the fractured and dislocated members one by one, it is necessary that we mention at the beginning of this chapter a summary of the discourse and sections. It compels you first to understand them and to stand upon their reality, you and others besides you who are eager to learn this noble craft.
So I say that when a fracture, dislocation, wound, or fall occurs to someone, it is necessary to hasten first to bleed him or to purge him, or both. Unless something prevents it, such as weakness of strength, or if the one to whom this has happened is a child or a very old man, or if the time is extremely hot or extremely cold.
Then he should restrict his diet to cold vegetables and the meat of birds and hares, and he should avoid drink, strong meats, and overeating, and all foods that fill the veins with blood, until the swelling is safe and the effusion of matter to the place is not expected.
Then let the patient return to his first regimen, to which he is accustomed. When the fractured bone begins to knit, the patient should be nourished with foods that provide abundant, thick, strong nourishment with viscosity, such as pottages, rice, heads, and gizzards, and cow stomachs. And eggs, soft fish, and thick drinks, and the like; for by this regimen the consolidation of the fracture will be quicker and better. And know that fractured bones, if they are in strong men and old men, cannot ever unite and heal to their original nature.
Because of the dryness and hardness of their bones.  And it may unite and heal where the bones were extremely soft, like the bones of small children.  But nature produces on the fractured bone from all its sides something resembling glue, thick, which adheres to it and binds it until one part holds to another.
And it binds one part to another until it comes to the utmost strength and firmness as it was at first, so that nothing hinders its actions.
For this reason, it is necessary to give the patient foods which contain firmness, viscosity, and thickness, as we have said. And know that fractures differ in their kinds according to the differences in the members, for a fracture of the leg bone is different from a fracture of the head bone, and a fracture of the chest bone is different from a fracture of the back bone.
And so are all the other members, each different from the other.  We shall come to the mention of each kind of fracture, explained in its chapter, detailed separately from the others.  And the kind of bone fracture may also differ in itself.
For it may be fractured by splintering without fragments being produced, and it may be fractured lengthwise with fragments and protrusions, separated and unseparated.  And the fracture may be with a wound and perforation in the skin, and the fracture may be a slight crack.
And for each kind there is a special method of setting it, as will be mentioned in detail in its place. And what makes a bone fracture known is its crookedness, swelling, its being apparent to the touch, and its creaking when you touch it with your hand.
Therefore, when there is no apparent crookedness in the place, nor creaking, nor do you feel any disturbance when you touch the bone, nor does the patient feel much pain, then there is no fracture, but it may be a sprain, or a slight fracture, or a slight crack; therefore it should not be moved by stretching and probing at all.
Rather, apply to it some of the medicines, the mention of which will come after a while, what suits the place, then bind it gently. And know that if the bone is broken and split in two without fragments being produced, except that each part has been separated from its fellow.
Then you should hasten at once to straighten and align it before a hot swelling occurs to it.  If a hot swelling occurs to it, then leave it for some days until the hot swelling subsides, then align it in whatever way you are able with gentleness and skill.
And know that its setting and alignment is easier than a bone in which fragments have occurred. Then it is bound as will be mentioned.  If the bone has fragments, then it is necessary to stretch the broken limb from both sides, whether hand or foot.
Either with your hands, if the limb is small, or with two ropes, or with a rope and hand. And let your placing of the limb be on a level surface, in its natural shape, until when the broken part of the bone is extended, then return those fragments to their places in whatever way you are able with skill and gentleness.
And strive with all your effort that you do not cause the patient pain or hurt by your action, and strive with all your effort to unite one of the bones with its fellow in the best way. And it is necessary at that time that you touch them and feel them with your hand.  If you see there anything contrary, correct and align it to the best of your ability.
And beware of strong extension and forceful probing, as many ignorant people do, and they often cause by their action a hot swelling or stiffness in the limb, as I have witnessed from their actions repeatedly.
Then, after the setting, perfection, and binding, keep that limb still and quiet, and warn the patient not to move it during his wakefulness and sleep, and during his turning and restlessness, and during his evacuation and all his movements, to the best of his ability, and that he should strive to keep the limb in a position where he will be safe from pain.
That is because, whenever he feels pain or ache in the position of the limb, he should move it to another position where he does not feel pain, and he should strive, moreover, that that position be level and straight.
Lest crookedness occur in the limb if it is set, and as for the method of binding the broken limb, it is as I describe to you. Know that broken limbs differ in their smallness and largeness and shapes; those that are small, such as the arm, finger, forearm, and the like.
Then it is necessary that the bandages be soft and gentle, and those that are thick, like the thigh, back, and chest, then it is necessary that the bandages be wide and firm, for a wide bandage is necessary for a large limb and binds it from every side with equal tightness so that no flaw interferes.
Then, after you have finished the setting, you begin to apply the appropriate plaster. With a soft, gentle movement on the place of the fracture, then you begin to wrap the bandage on the place of the fracture itself three or four times, according to what the limb requires, and you tighten your hand slightly with the bandage, then you take it to the upper side of the place of the fracture.
With less tightening than your tightening of the broken place, then you move the wrapping away from the place of the fracture a little and loosen the tightening little by little until you take from the correct place something suitable, then you take another bandage and wrap it also on the broken place.
Wrappings, then you take the wrapping to the lower side of the fracture, and let your action in tightening and loosening the wrapping be as we mentioned in the first upper wrapping, then you place between the bandages from the soft padding or rags what straightens the crookedness of the fracture.
If there is a crookedness, otherwise do not put anything in it, then you wrap around it another bandage, then you set on these bandages the firm splints at once if there is no swelling or hot inflammation in the limb.
If there is swelling or hot inflammation, then apply what calms that inflammation and removes the swelling, and leave it for days, then tighten the splints on it then, and let the splints be from wide, carved, skillfully prepared halves of reeds, or let the splints be from sieve wood.
Which are made from pine or palm leaves or  *khulanj* or *kalkh* and similar things which are available, and let the width of each splint be in this image and this shape exactly.
Except that the splint which is placed on the fracture itself should be a little thicker and wider than the other splints, and as for the length of the splint, it is made according to what suits the broken limb, in its size and smallness, image of the splint:
Then you tighten the splints with another bandage, according to your first tightening exactly, then you tie from above with firm threads according to what we mentioned of the tightening, which is that your tightening on the place of the fracture should be more. And the further from the fracture, the less the tightening.
And the threads should be medium in thickness and fineness, and let them be of wet linen especially, because if the threads are thick, as I have seen done by the ignorant who make their threads from twisted linen strips, it is a great mistake.
Because the tightening with them falls outside moderation, and thin threads are also unsuitable because you do not achieve with them the tightening you want, and there should not be less than a finger between splint and splint.
If the patient is harmed by the ends of the splints after tightening in the healthy places, then place under them soft tow or fluffy wool so that nothing harms him from that. As for if the fracture is with a wound and a rupture occurs in the skin, we will mention that separately.
And know that not every broken limb should be tightened with splints from the first day, and that is that if the limb is large, splints should not be placed on it except after five or seven days or more, according to your assurance of the avoidance of hot swelling.
And this is the description of the bandages with which the ancients used to bind fractures, dislocations, and wounds; a description of a general, brief bandage that binds fractures and is suitable for most temperaments, especially children and women, because it is not overcome by heat or cold.
And it is that you take from the dust of the mill, which is the essence of the flour that clings to the walls of the mill during the movement of the mill, then knead it as it is without sifting it with egg white, and make its kneading neither thick nor thin, then you apply it.
The description of another bandage that binds fractures, dislocations, and wounds: take from mastic, leadwort, acacia, resin, gum arabic, and sugar, ten drachms of each; and myrrh and aloes, five drachms of each; and from the lote tree, twenty drachms; and from Armenian or Roman clay, twenty drachms. All is pounded and mixed with lote tree water, or with egg white if the patient's temperament is hot, then this bandage is used, for it is good in composition, quickly binds broken bones, and is suitable for most people because of its moderation.
The description of another bandage that is also useful for fractures and wounds: take gum arabic, mastic, and white leadwort, ten drachms of each; myrrh and aloes, five drachms of each; acacia, six drachms; Armenian clay, twenty drachms.  All is pounded finely, sifted, kneaded with water or egg white, and used.
The description of a bandage for joints and bones that have shifted from their places, and it soothes the pain affecting them, and for bone cracks and fractures: take purified wool and soak it in vinegar and cooked oil, and apply it to the place. This bandage does not have the power to set [bones].
But it is excellent in soothing hot swelling and relieving pain in particular. The description of another bandage that sets broken bones: take the leaves of the mute fig and the leaves of wild poppy, pound them all together, and bandage with them while moist. The description of another brief bandage used when a bone fracture is knitting.
And you want to resolve the rest of the swelling, take of the root of leadwort, chamomile, violet flowers, and crushed acorns, one part of each; pound all together and knead it with egg white if the member is not inflamed; if it is inflamed, knead it with coriander water or water, and use it.
The description of another bandage stronger in resolving than the first, used when a hard swelling occurs when the bone is knitting: take of the root of leadwort, flaxseed, fenugreek, rue, germander, violet flowers, and chamomile, one part of each.
Pound all together and knead with vinegar water, or with fresh water, or with egg white, all according to the heat of the member and the quietness of its inflammation. As for Hippocrates, he did not mention in his book that it should be placed on the broken member when it is set.
Except for the cerate made from wax and oil only, and he described it as being moderate between thickness and thinness. As for Galen, he saw that things that have drying properties with some heat, such as myrrh, aloes, frankincense, and the like that we mentioned, should be placed on the broken member when it is set.
The description of a bandage that benefits weakness and pain: take  mugwort,  fenugreek, human hair that is clipped or bird feathers, leadwort and salt, equal parts; pound and sift, knead and bandage with it. As for the amount that the bandage should remain.
Then it is to be loosened; you should look, and if no pain, itching, or movement of the broken bone from its place occurs in the afflicted area, do not loosen it for many days.  If intense itching or disturbing pain or swelling occurs in the area.
Then hasten to loosen it at once and do not delay that at all, and remove the bandage from it. Then take a soft cloth or a wet sea sponge, dip it in lukewarm water, and wash the area with it until the itching and pain subside, then let the member rest for an hour.
Then apply carded wool soaked in vinegar and oil or rose oil and bandage it for a night until you are safe from hot swelling and the member's swelling subsides and its pain goes away. Then return it to gentle tightening and a light bandage, and do not tighten it as tightly as the first time.
And treat it gently until it heals. If you see that the swelling, redness, pain, and puffiness, and all the symptoms have completely disappeared and you need a bandage and tightening, then repeat as you did initially, the same. If nothing of what we have mentioned occurs in the member.
Then do not loosen it except after three, four, five, or seven days, and it may be left for twenty days; all this depends on what appears to you from the condition of the member, as we said, until the fracture is set and the flesh nears consolidation on it.
Then also increase the tightening more than your first tightening, and also increase the patient's nourishment as previously mentioned. If you see that the place of the fracture has dried and atrophied more than it should, then know that nourishment is prevented from reaching it.
Then bathe it with lukewarm water each time you loosen it, and let that be every three days, and loosen the tightening a little. For by this action, nourishment will reach the member and it will heal quickly. As for what ignorant bone-setters do of breaking the member again.
If it is not set properly initially, and it is set crookedly, then that is an error of their action and a great deception. And if it were correct, the predecessors would have mentioned it in their books and it would have been practiced, and I have seen no trace of it at all from any of them. And the correct thing is not to do it.
Chapter Two.
On accidental fractures in the head.
The types of accidental fractures in the head are numerous, their forms varied, and their causes diverse.  Some fractures are caused by a sword blow, and this may either sever the bone completely until it reaches the membrane under the bone. Just as the wedge does in wood.
Therefore, this type of fracture is called a wedge fracture.  It may also be that the sword cuts some of the bone and severs only its surface, without the cut penetrating completely through it; this type of fracture is called an absolute avulsion, and the wound of both of these fractures may be either large or small.
And there are fractures which are comminuted or contused, and their cause is a blow with a stone, or a fall onto a stone, or something similar. This fracture may be penetrating, nearly reaching the membrane beneath the bone, or it may be in the surface of the bone.
And the wound of these two fractures is also either large or small. And there is a fracture which is hidden in the bone, as thin as a hair, which is a slight crack.
Therefore, this type of fracture is called hairline.  There is also a fracture which results from a fall or a blow from a stone or the like, which drives a portion of the bone inward, making a depression in the area, just as happens to a copper pot when struck, causing a part of it to be driven inward.
This most often occurs in the soft bones of the head, such as the heads of children. All of these types of fractures may have loose and non-loose fragments; we shall describe the treatment of all of these in their proper place, and we shall identify all these types of fractures by examining them.
And examining them with probes and removing the corrupted flesh from them. As for the hairline type, it is identified by exposing the bone, wiping it, and smearing it with ink; then the fracture will appear black. As for the treatment of fractures, first look at the symptoms of the patient.
If you see in his symptoms that which clearly indicates danger, such as vomiting bile, extension, loss of consciousness, loss of voice, fainting, high fever, sinking of the eyes, and redness of the eyes, and similar symptoms, then do not approach the patient nor treat him, for death is inevitable in most cases with these symptoms.
But if you see symptoms that do not frighten you and you hope for his safety, then begin his treatment. And that is that if the wounded person comes to you immediately after being wounded, and that is in the days of winter,
then you should strive to remove the bone before the fourteenth day in any case. And if it is in the days of summer, then you should hasten to remove the bone before the seventh day, before the membrane under the bone becomes corrupted and those symptoms which we have described appear.
If the fracture of the bone has reached the membrane covering the brain and there is crushing and contusion, then you should cut away the crushed and contused part as I describe to you. And that is that you shave the head of the wounded patient.
And uncover the bone in whatever way is possible for you and according to the shape of the wound and what is easiest for the patient. If, when you uncover the bone, you find bleeding or a hot swelling, then treat that as is appropriate.
And that is that you pack the place with cloths soaked in a syrup and rose oil, and cleanse the wound until the swelling subsides and the bleeding stops. Then you begin to remove and extract the bone, and this is done in one of two ways.
As for the first method, it is that you cut the bone with a delicate saw with a narrow blade, and this is its image:
Then you use another saw after this one, a little wider, and this is its image:
Then you also use another saw, wider still than the second.  It is necessary that you have several different saws, some wider than others, and some shorter than others, and they should be extremely sharp at their edges, and let them be of Indian iron or good steel.
And use gentleness in striking the saw, lest you shake the head and injure it. If the bone is strong and solid, then it is necessary that you pierce around it before using the saws, with the drills which are called non-immersive drills, and they are called non-immersive drills.
Because they do not go beyond the limit of the cranial bone to what is beyond it, for the drill has a round edge below its sharp head, similar to a ring or small circle, preventing it from sinking and exceeding the thickness of the bone.
And it is necessary for you to take many of these drills, each suitable for the thickness of that bone, so that you have for each skull a drill whose sharp point is in length and shortness according to the thickness of that skull.  And this is an image of three types. Of the drills: large, medium, and small.
As for the method of piercing around the fractured bone, it is that you place the drill on the bone and turn it with your fingers until you know that the bone has been penetrated. Then you move the drill to another place, and you make the distance between each hole the thickness of an awl or similar.
Then cut between each two holes with the forceps, and do this with the utmost gentleness as we have said, until you remove the bone, either with your hand or with something else from among the instruments that you have prepared for that.
