Such as forceps and delicate hooks. And it is necessary that you take great care that the drill or forceps does not touch anything of the peritoneum. If the bone is cleaned and freed from the peritoneum, if it is adhering to it, and you have removed it, then it is necessary that you cleanse it.
And smooth the roughness that remains in the bone with another instrument similar to the forceps, except that it should be thinner and more delicate than the other forceps. If something remains of the small bones and fragments, then take them gently with the instruments that you have.
Then you treat the wound afterwards with sutures and ointments, the mention of which will come. As for the other method of operation, it is a very easy method, far from danger; Galen mentioned it and praised it greatly, and this is his saying. First, you should begin by uncovering the part of the bone where the fracture is most severe and evident, until, when you have uncovered that part, you place under it the end of these lenticular forceps, and this is its image:
The lenticular part of it is smooth, so that it does not cut anything, and the sharp part of it is on its sides going lengthwise, as you see, so that the lenticular part rests against the peritoneum and the face of the sharp cutting edge is on the bone. Then you strike the forceps on one side.
With a small hammer, until you cut all the bone gently as it rotates, and you are safe from the membrane, so that nothing frightening happens in it at all, even if the surgeon is the most ignorant and cowardly of people, yes, even if he were sleepy.
If anything of the membrane remains attached to the bone in some parts of the bone, then scrape it off with the tip of the lenticular forceps itself and free it gently, for it will be freed from it without harm or fear.
But if the fracture of the bone did not penetrate to the membrane, and what was broken from the bone was its surface, and roughness and small fragments remained in it, then it is necessary to remove that roughness and extract those fragments with fine forceps, which have been made in various sizes so that you can use the most suitable and appropriate ones in each place, according to what the work leads you to.
And the broken bone itself and its shape, and it is necessary that you use first in your scraping of the bone the widest of those forceps, then use after it a finer one, and continue to do that successively until you come to using the finest and thinnest of them all.
As for the other small, hair-like fissures and slight fractures, it is necessary to use for each one a treatment according to what leads to its healing, which is something that is not hidden from anyone who has the least skill in this craft and has studied what we have written and explained concerning large fractures.
If the membrane remains exposed when you extract the bone, then it is necessary that you take a linen cloth the size of the wound and dip it in rose oil and place it on the mouth of the wound, then take another folded or triangular cloth. And dip it in syrup and rose oil and place it on the first cloth, and do that as gently as you can so as not to burden the peritoneum, then use a wide bandage on top and do not tighten it except to the extent that it only holds the wound.
And leave it with this treatment for a day or two until you are sure of the hot swelling, then untie it and use afterwards medicines that have a drying quality, such as the roots of iris, and powdered costus, and powdered spikenard, and zedoary, and the like; and that is that you make powders from these medicines.
And sprinkle it on the wound as it is dry; and in general, use in that a medicine that cleanses and does not sting, and strive hard during your treatment of the wound that it be clean, free from the filth of oil and dirt, and do not allow pus to collect in it at all.
Because if pus collects on the membrane of the brain, it corrupts and rots it, and from that great harm befalls the patient; and it may occur in the peritoneum of the head when the bone is exposed, especially if its treatment is neglected.
Blackness on its surface; if the patient shows the symptoms that we have mentioned, know that he is inevitably doomed, unless the blackness occurred due to a medicine applied to it, and it was in the power of that medicine to cause that blackness.
It is necessary that you take one part of honey and three parts of rose oil, and mix them well, and smear them on a cloth, then place it on the peritoneum, then treat it with the various treatments that are necessary until it heals.
Chapter Three.
On the setting of a broken nose.
Know that only the upper two parts of the nose, or one of them, can be broken, because they are bones, for the lower part of it is cartilaginous and does not break, but rather it is subject to contusion, crookedness, and flattening.
If one of its parts is broken, then it is necessary that you insert your little finger into the nostril and straighten that fracture from the inside with your index and thumb fingers from the outside, until you return the nose to its natural shape. Let this be done gently by you, and be careful that your actions do not cause the patient pain.
If the fracture is at the top of the nose and the finger does not reach it, then it should be straightened with the end of a small, slightly thick probe. If the fracture is on both sides, do likewise, and let him hasten to treat it on the first day of the fracture.
If possible, otherwise after the seventh or tenth day when the hot swelling subsides, then insert into the nostril a tent of linen rags if the fracture is on one side, or insert two tents if the fracture is on both sides.
Let the thickness of the tent be such that it fills the nostril. Some of the early bone-setters mentioned that the tent should be moistened with fat and changed every day, but I do not see this, rather the tent should be moistened with egg white mixed with powdered alum.
Then leave the tent until the bone sets and the cartilage hardens.  Goose quill tubes may be inserted into the nose at the site of the tent after wrapping them with soft rags, this will make the retention for the nasal fracture stronger and so that the patient does not have difficulty breathing.
This is not necessary; you may do it or you may make tents. If a hot swelling appears in the nose during your work, apply a compress to the nose with quince or cotton soaked in vinegar and rose oil or some diachylon ointment.
If no hot swelling appears, then you should dress it externally with finely ground semolina and finely ground myrrh mixed with egg white, then put a soft bandage on it, and do not bind the nose at all, if the nasal bones are fractured with a small fracture.
Or if it is shattered, then it should be incised and removed with the appropriate instrument, then suture the incision and treat it with appropriate ointments that unite and heal. If a wound occurs inside the nose, then it should be treated with a tent and lead tubes should be used. Until it heals.
Chapter Four.
On the setting of the lower jaw if it is fractured.
If the lower jaw is fractured and the fracture is not accompanied by a wound, then if the fracture is only external and it is not fractured in two places and is not depressed inwards, then its identification is easy.
It is necessary that if the fracture is in the right ramus, you should insert the index finger of the left hand into the mouth of the patient, and similarly if the fracture is in the left ramus, you should insert the index finger of the right hand and gently raise the prominence of the fracture from the inside to the outside.
And with your other hand from outside the bone, you control it to straighten it. If the fracture of the jaw has been fractured in two places, then it is necessary to use traction from both sides to straighten it until you are able to straighten it, if a displacement has occurred in the teeth.
Or if they are separated, then tie what you hope will remain with a gold, silver, or silk thread, then place a pledget on the fractured jaw, then place a folded cloth over it, and place a large, tight splint or a piece of leather the length of the jaw over the cloth. Then tie it from above according to what seems suitable to you to tie it and agrees with its binding so that it does not become undone, and order the patient to rest and be still, and make his food soft liquids. If you think that something has changed in its form in any way, then hasten to untie it on the third day.
Then tie it from above according to what seems suitable to you to tie it and agrees with its binding so that it does not become undone, and order the patient to rest and be still, and make his food soft liquids. If you think that something has changed in its form in any way, then hasten to untie it on the third day. Then repair what you have bandaged with mill dust mixed with egg white or semolina flour after removing the pledget from it, and place a soft compress on the bandage. As long as that bandage adheres to it and the condition of the bone has not changed, leave it.
Do not untie it until it heals and the fracture becomes firm. Often this fracture becomes firm in three weeks. If a hot inflammation occurs during that time, use what we have repeatedly mentioned to soothe it until that inflammation goes away.
And if the fracture is with a wound, look: if a splinter or splinters have separated from the bone, then be gentle in removing those splinters with whatever instrument suits you for their removal. If the mouth of the wound is narrow, then widen it with a scalpel to the extent of your need.
Then, when you have removed those splinters and nothing remains of them, stitch the mouth of the wound if it is wide; otherwise, apply one of the ointments suitable for that. And stitch the wound until it heals.
Chapter Five.
On the setting of the clavicle if it is fractured.
The clavicle is most often fractured in front, at the end of the shoulder, and its fracture occurs in one of three ways: either it is broken and split into two without splinters, which is easiest to set;
or splinters occur in the fracture, which is more difficult to set; or the fracture is accompanied by a wound.  The procedure, if the fracture is without a wound, is that you prepare two assistants, one to hold the arm next to the fractured clavicle, and the other to extend the neck toward the other side.
Then you set the fracture with your fingers until its shape is as it should be, and there is neither protrusion nor irregularity. If you need more extension, then you should place a ball of rags or wool under the armpit of the patient.
The size of the ball should suit your needs.  You extend and raise the clavicle and press the ball with your hand until the fracture is set as it should be. If you are unable to pull the end of the clavicle outwards because it has gone inwards, then the patient should lie on his back.
A moderately sized pillow is placed under his shoulder, and the assistant presses his shoulder downwards until the clavicle which is deep rises upwards. Then, set the fracture and smooth it with your fingers.
If you feel that a splinter of the clavicle has broken off and is moving, then you should make an incision over it and remove that splinter gently. If the splinter is embedded in the bone, then try to cut it with one of the cutting instruments which you have prepared for this purpose. After you place under the clavicle the instrument which protects the peritoneum, which is an instrument of wood or iron, and this is its image:
It resembles a spoon without a bowl. Its width should be according to the size of the bone, large or small, and its length also according to what is possible for the work. Let it have two ends, as you see, one end wider and the other narrower.
If the wound opening which you incised when removing the bone splinter is wide and you are sure there will be no hot inflammation, then join the edges of the wound with sutures. If the opening is small or you fear hot inflammation, then treat the wound with lint and pledgets according to the size of the incision.
If hot inflammation appears, then moisten the lint in rose oil and vinegar or wine and apply it. As for setting the bone if it is without a wound or incision, it is to apply to the bone a plaster made from mill dust with egg white. And you place the soft splint upon it, then place the ball under his armpit if he needs it. Then take a bandage which is very long, and let its width be a cubit or thereabouts. Then apply a folded pledget upon the splint and the plaster, then take a splint from a thin board. Let its width be three fingers and its length likewise. Then enclose it in a cloth, then enclose that cloth with the splint in the part of the bandage which lies on the place of the fracture, then tighten the bandage on the fracture as you wind it. And pass it over his neck and under his sound armpit and under his injured armpit, and pass it repeatedly on each side, and as you see that the tightening fixes the fracture firmly, which is not hidden from you.
And the aim of all this is that the splint does not move from upon the broken bone. Therefore, it is necessary to examine the patient every day; whenever the bandage loosens and you see that the splint has moved, correct that and tighten the bandage. Then let the patient sleep on his back, and place a small pillow under his armpit when he sleeps at night.
So that his arm may be raised from his side, and the fracture of the clavicle may be raised with the raising of the shoulder, or tie his arm to his neck. And do not loosen the bandage unless an incident occurs in the area, such as itching or swelling, until twelve days.
Then renew the dressing if you see a reason for it. And leave it bandaged until the fracture of the clavicle heals and consolidates, and it becomes stronger and firmer; this takes about twenty-eight days. And in some people it may be in less than that.
Chapter Six.
On the treatment of a fractured humerus. The humerus rarely fractures in its wide part; rather, it fractures at its extremities.  When a part of it is fractured, or it is fractured in its middle, this is known by touch. Then the treatment depends on the nature of the fracture.
Straighten it and restore it to its natural shape by any means you can, then apply to the area mill dust with egg white and soft tow, and place on top a pad of folded cloth, then apply a wide splint of wood.
Thin enough to cover the whole shoulder or slightly wider. If there is a lump under the splint at any part of the shoulder, smooth out that lump with soft tow until the splint lies evenly, then bind it from above with a long bandage.
Bind it tightly and make sure the splint does not move from its place. Check the bandage every day; whenever the bandage loosens, tighten it and straighten the splint if it has moved from its place. Let the patient lie on his sound side. The shoulder heals in twenty [days].
days or twenty-five days. When this time is complete, unbind the bandage, for you are safe, for it is among the bones which one does not fear will be crushed or dislocated. If a fragment of bone protrudes and is embedded under the skin, incise over it and remove it. And do what was mentioned in the fracture of the clavicle to soothe the hot swelling if anything of that happens.
Chapter Seven.
On the reduction of a fracture of the chest.
The chest may be fractured in its middle, and this rarely happens; but its extremities are more often fractured and shattered. Among its symptoms, if its middle is fractured, is that it inclines downwards, and there is intense pain, difficulty in breathing, and coughing.
And sometimes blood is expelled, and the depression in the broken bone is evident and not hidden from the touch.  Its reduction is that the patient lies on his back, and a pillow is placed between his shoulders, then his shoulders are compressed, and the ribs are brought together with the hands from both sides, and gentleness is used in straightening it in every way that is possible.
And continue until the shape of the bone is restored as it should be, then apply a bandage and a splint, and place over it a splint of a thin board of willow or elm or something similar that is light, after wrapping it in a cloth. Then gently bind it to the fractured bone lest it shift, and pass the bandage around to the back several times and tighten it firmly, then examine the bandage at all times, and whenever it loosens, tighten it.
And if necessity demands its removal because of inflammation that occurs at the site, or pain, or swelling, then proceed to remove it and take off the bandage, and treat what has occurred by its appropriate treatment, then reapply the bandage if you see reason for it. And face it and maintain the binding until it heals.
Chapter Eight.
Concerning the setting of ribs if they are fractured: Know that fractures of the ribs occur in the thick parts near the back, and their ends in front are subject to contusion because they are cartilaginous, and the knowledge of this is not hidden from the sense when examined with the fingers.
And set it by straightening the fracture with the fingers in the accessible way until the shape is straightened as it should be, then bandage and bind the broken bone with a splint if it needs that.  If the fracture of the ribs is angled inwards, then severe pain occurs to the patient.
And it penetrates like the penetration which occurs to someone with a puncture wound, because the bone penetrates the diaphragm, and shortness of breath, coughing and the ejection of much blood also occur to him, and this is difficult to treat, and the ancients devised many strategems for it.
And some of them said that it is necessary to make the patient's food that which produces flatulence and wind so that the abdomen swells and expands and the fracture is pushed outwards, and we dislike this lest it confirms the occurrence of hot inflammation if it has not occurred, and if it has occurred then it increases and confirms it.
And some of them said a cupping glass should be placed on the area then strongly sucked, and this is more similar to the measurement, except that it is feared that the cupping glass will draw impurities to the area due to its weakness, and some of them said that the area should be covered with wool dipped in hot oil.
And make a bandage between the ribs until it is full, so that the binding is even when you wrap it around, then treat the patient with the treatment of a puncture wound in food and medicine, if the patient is burdened by a severe matter that he cannot endure. And the bone penetrated the diaphragm with a harmful penetration, and we feared for the patient, therefore it is necessary that we incise the area and uncover the fractured rib, then place under it the instrument which protects the peritoneum, which was previously described, and gently cut the bone and remove it.
Then we bring together the edges of the wound if it is large by stitching and treat it with ointments until it heals, and if a hot inflammation appears in the meantime, then quickly wet bandages in rose oil, place them on the area and treat the patient with what calms the inflammation from the inside as well.
And he lies on the side on which sleep is easier for him until he recovers.
Chapter Nine.
In the reduction of the vertebrae of the back and neck.
As for the bones of the neck, if a fracture affects them—and this rarely happens to them, and what most often affects them is bruising, and likewise the vertebrae of the back also—if this happens to someone and you want to know whether he will recover or not. Then look, and if you see that his hands have become relaxed and numb and dead, and he is unable to move them, nor to extend nor to flex them, and if you pinch them or prick them with a needle he does not feel it and finds no pain in them, then know that in most instances he will not recover, and he is dying.
And if he moves them and feels the pinching and pricking in them, then know that the marrow of the bone is safe and that the patient will recover with treatment.  If a similar thing affects the vertebrae of the back and you want to know whether he will recover also or not,
look at his legs; and if you see that they have become relaxed and what happened in the hands has happened in them, then if he lies on his back, wind and excrement come out involuntarily, and if he lies on his stomach, urine comes out involuntarily, and if he lies on his back and wants to urinate, he cannot do so, then know that he is dying, so do not trouble yourself with his treatment; but if nothing of this happens to him, the matter is less serious. And the treatment of what has happened is that you try to alleviate the hot swelling.
by placing on the injured vertebra rose oil alone, or with roasted egg yolks; you should apply this three times a day, until the hot swelling subsides; then apply to the place one of the strengthening, drying compresses, and bind it tightly, and order him to keep still and quiet.
And let him not sleep except on the side where he feels no pain, until he recovers.  If, at the time of the injury, a splinter or something has broken off from the bone, then it is necessary to incise the skin and extract that bone. Then, if the wound is large, bring the edges together with sutures, then treat it with healing ointments until it heals. If the last bone of the coccyx, which is the rump bone, is broken, then you should insert the thumb of the left hand into the anus.
And set the broken bone with the other hand, as far as you are able to achieve the setting, then apply the compress and splint if you need it, and then bind it.
If you feel a broken splinter in it, then incise it and remove it, and treat the wound with the previously mentioned treatment until it heals.
Chapter Ten.
On the reduction of a fracture of the hip.
Hip bones rarely fracture; but if they do fracture, it is in such a way that the ends are shattered and split lengthwise, and incline inwards.  The patient experiences pain in the area, and weakness and numbness in the leg on the side of the fracture.
Its reduction is that you pass your hand over it until you ascertain the nature of the fracture. If the fracture is only at the ends, then set that fracture according to what seems best to you in terms of setting, until its form resembles its natural form.
If the fracture is lengthwise, or if it has inclined inwards, then lay the patient on his stomach so that the reduction of that fracture becomes possible for you. When you have set it, apply a bandage to it, then apply a splint of wood or leather, and bind it tightly.
Do not fear for him that the fracture will shift or the splint will come loose.  Fill any hollows in the loins with material until the binding is even.  Instruct the patient to lie on his back or on his sound side. If he develops a hot swelling…
…then cease from setting and binding it until the hot swelling subsides. Apply something to soothe it, as previously described, then return to setting and binding it as is appropriate. If there are fragments or shattered pieces of bone at the ends, they should not be removed.
They should not be touched but smoothed from the outside, as we have said, and the binding should be left in place until healing occurs.
Chapter Eleven.
On the setting of a fractured humerus.
The humerus is that which is between the elbow and the shoulder. If it is fractured, it is set in one of two ways: one is to take a smooth, curved, moderately thick stick, in this shape:
And you tie two bandages to its ends, then it is hung from a high place, and the patient sits on a chair, then his broken arm is placed on the stick until his armpit is touching the middle of the curve of the stick, then something heavy is hung from above it.
Or a servant stretches it downwards, then the physician sets the fracture with his hands until he returns the fracture to what is appropriate.  The other method is that the patient lies on his back and his hand is hung from his neck with a bandage.
Then you order two servants, one to hold above the fracture with his hands and the other to hold below it, and each stretches to his side. And if you wish the stretching to be stronger, then tie below the fracture with a bandage and above it with a bandage, and each of the servants stretches to his side.
If the fracture is near the shoulder joint, then the middle of the bandage should be under the armpit and the other under the fracture towards the elbow.  Similarly, if the fracture is near the elbow, then the bandage should be in that place and on the elbow itself.
Then you set the fracture gently without violence, until the fracture is set as it should be and unites well. Then you bandage it if there is no hot swelling. If hot swelling appears, then leave bandaging until the seventh day and apply moistened wool soaked in vinegar and rose oil.
Until the swelling subsides, then bandage it. The method of bandaging is to apply the compress to the fracture, then apply a bandage of new cloth over the compress, then bring the arm towards the upper arm itself and place the hand open on the shoulder.
And you apply the cloth and bandage to the upper arm and forearm so that the arm acts as a splint, if nothing prevents you and nothing changes in the broken bone. If you fear that something might change, then use splints.
This is to place on the fracture itself a splint that is wider and stronger than the other splints, and let there be the width of a finger between each splint.  Let the length of the splints be according to the fracture with the addition of three fingers on each side.
Then you bandage the splints with the bandage mentioned at the beginning of the chapter.
And that your bandaging of the fracture site should be tighter.
And the further from the fracture, the less tight the bandage should be. If you see that the placement of the splints and the bandaging as we have said is appropriate when you set the limb immediately, then do so.
And if you fear a hot inflammation, leave off the bandaging and splints until the seventh day, as we have said. Then examine the bandage every three days, lest there be chafing or swelling at the site, or the nourishment be prevented from reaching the limb because of excessive tightness.  Correct all this as we have mentioned.
But if you are confident that none of this will occur, do not untie the bandage except after many days.  And let the patient lie on his back with his hand on his stomach, and a moderately full cushion of wool be placed under the arm.
And examine at all times, night and day, lest the shape of the broken limb be disturbed or the bandage loosen. Correct all this with your effort, and let the patient's diet be of the kind we have described previously, that the food be light at first until the bone begins to harden.
Then it is necessary to make his food more substantial. For it is the nature of the arm and leg to harden in forty days. Then it is necessary to untie [the bandage], and he should use the bath and be treated with ointments suitable for this. If the fracture is severe and comminuted,
then do not untie the bandage from him until fifty days or even two months.
Chapter Twelve.
On the setting of a fracture of the arm.
The arm is composed of two bones, called the ulna. One is small, and it is that which is next to the thumb; and the other is large, and it is situated under the small one from below.  Perhaps the larger ulna is broken alone, or the smaller one alone, or perhaps both are broken together.
And when the smaller upper ulna is broken, then its setting is easy, and its healing is quicker. And when the lower ulna is broken, then its fracture is bad, and its healing is difficult. And the worst is if the two bones are broken together. If the bone which is broken is the smaller upper ulna,
then it is necessary for the physician, when setting it, to make its extension easy gently until he sets it straight.  And if the large ulna is that which is broken, then it is necessary to make its extension stronger. And if both ulnas are broken, then it is necessary to make the extension much stronger.
And it is necessary that the shape of the hand, when it is set and extended, is extended on a cushion, and the thumb of the hand should be higher than all the fingers, and the little finger should be lower than the other fingers, and the patient should sit squarely on his seat.
And let the cushion be opposite him in height, lest the patient suffer difficulty.
Then a servant extends the arm from below, either with his hand or with a bandage, and another servant extends from above likewise. Then the physician sets the bone until he returns it to the best shape he can.  If there are splinters in the fracture of the bone, then they are removed.
Return each splinter to its place to the best of your ability. If a splinter appears that is separated and is piercing the skin and there is no hope of setting it, then make an incision upon it and extract it in the manner which we mentioned previously. If the fracture is with a wound, then it has been given a separate chapter.
Then take its treatment from there. If a hot swelling appears at the beginning of your setting, then apply a cloth with a poultice made from rose oil and white wax, and let it be moderate between thickness and thinness, and bind the cloth upon it gently until the swelling subsides.
Then remove the poultice and apply the prepared bandage of mill dust with egg white. Then apply the splints, and let the splint which is placed upon the fracture itself be a little wider and stronger. And know that the number of splints for the arm is six in most cases.
Whether the fracture was in one ulna or in both ulnas together, then make your binding at the place of the fracture stronger and tighter, and whenever you move the binding upwards or downwards, make the binding a little looser, as previously mentioned at the beginning of the chapter.
And let the cloths which are wrapped around the fracture be soft and moist, and not very hard. And let the thread with which it is bound be of fine linen, moderate between thinness and thickness, as we described. And examine the limb and the bandage after a few days.
If something happens that needs to be corrected, such as itching that appears in the limb, then the limb should be bathed with warm water until that itching subsides, and the limb should be left unbandaged for a night so that it rests, then the binding should be resumed.
If the binding has loosened and the bone has shifted or something like that, then repair all that to the best of your ability. And look also, if nourishment is prevented from reaching the limb because of excessive binding, then it should be loosened a little and left for a few days until nourishment flows to it, then bind it.
If nothing of what we mentioned occurs to the patient, then it should not be unbound except after twenty days or so. Then suspend the patient's hand to his neck, and let his arm be straight. He should take care to avoid restless movements, and let him sleep on his back.
And know that this fracture of the arm heals in thirty days, or in thirty-two, and perhaps it heals in twenty-eight days; all that according to the conditions of the temperaments and conditions of strength.
Chapter Thirteen.
On the setting of a fracture of the hand and fingers.
Fractures of the metacarpus and phalanges rarely occur, but contusions frequently do.  Therefore, when a fracture or contusion occurs in the hand…
The patient should sit cross-legged, with a chair directly in front, level with him.
Then he should place his hand upon it, extended. Then the bones are extended by the assistant, and the surgeon sets them until they are properly joined. Then, if no hot inflammation occurs, a bandage and splint should be applied; and then a further splint is applied over it according to the location.
And it is placed in a soft cloth. If the fracture is towards the palm, make a kind of ball from a cloth and have the patient grasp it with his broken hand, then bind it tightly with a long cloth.
The splint should be of soft leather, so that it conforms to the skin with the flexion of the whole hand, and is bound as necessary. If the fracture is outward, the splint should be placed above and another below, in the hand, so that the hand remains open and straight.
Then the binding is applied as the hand turns, and the fingers are interwoven with the bandage. If the fracture is in one of the finger joints, if it is the thumb, it should be set properly and then bound with the hand; and if you wish, you may make a small, upright splint to support the fracture.
It should not be moved. If the fracture is in the other fingers, such as the middle, index, little, or ring finger, it should be set and bound with the next sound finger, or all bound together, which is best, or a small, upright splint is applied.
As we said for the thumb, and observe during and after your setting for hot swelling, and counter it appropriately when anything of that occurs, as has been repeatedly described.
Chapter Fourteen.
On the setting of a fractured thigh.
The thigh bone is frequently fractured and is apparent to the touch because it moves forwards and backwards.  Its treatment is to tighten a bandage above the fracture and another below it, the patient lying on his face.
Then each bandage is extended to its side evenly. This is if the fracture is in the middle of the bone, but if it is near the head of the femur, then a soft bandage should be applied, either of wool or similar.
At the head of the femur towards the pubis so that the extension is upwards, and the other bandage below the fracture.  Similarly, if the fracture is near the knee, let the bandage be near the knee so that the extension is downwards. Then the physician straightens the bone with both hands until he restores it to its natural shape.
And the bone unites well. Then the bandage and tightening should be maintained, unless a hot inflammation occurs in the limb. If inflammation occurs, leave it for some days until the hot inflammation subsides, then return to its treatment.
As for its tightening, a firm, wide turban should be wound twice or thrice around the fracture, leaving the excess. Then the leg is bent until the heel reaches the ischium, and a long thread is inserted between the thigh and leg near the knee from below, the ends of the thread being above on both sides.
Then you wind the remainder of the turban around the leg and thigh. Then you place splints on the thigh at the site of the fracture itself, and one splint on the leg bone. Then stuff the space between the thigh and leg with soft rags so that the tightening is even.
Then begin strong tightening from the middle at the site of the fracture, three or four turns.  The further the bandage is from the site of the fracture, the less tight, softer, and looser should be the tightening.
Then take the ends of the thread that you inserted between the thigh and leg, and tie them to the splints above. Then pass the ends of the thread downwards until you reach the heel, and with them also tighten the ends of the splints on the other side.
So that the bandage does not move from its place. Then leave the tightening as long as no pain, swelling, or puffiness, etc., occurs in the limb. If any of that happens, quickly untie it and correct what has occurred, as we have repeatedly mentioned.
If there is a protruding bone splinter, it should be aligned if possible. Otherwise, incise over it, remove it, and treat the wound as previously mentioned until it heals.  This fracture of the thigh may be set without adding splints to the leg.
As we mentioned in the case of the humerus and forearm, except that setting it in this way does not cause lameness for the patient. But if it is set alone, without adding the leg, the patient will inevitably be lame.
Know that the thigh heals in fifty days, or a little more or less, all depending on the differences in temperaments and other conditions.
Chapter Fifteen.
On the fracture of the kneecap.
Fracture of the kneecap is rare, but contusion is frequent. If a fracture occurs, it is either a fissure or a shattering into fragments, and this may occur with or without a wound.
You will ascertain all this by touch, and you will reduce it by aligning the separated fragments with your fingers until they are joined and united, as far as the setting, gentleness and precision allow. Then you will apply a bandage, and a circular splint if necessary.
You will tighten it with a suitable bandage above, and then you will treat all the conditions which we have described in other fractures, such as hot swelling, etc., by counteracting each symptom with the appropriate remedy until healing occurs.
Chapter Sixteen.
On the reduction of fractures of the leg.
The leg has two bones; one thick, called the tibia, and the other thin, called the fibula.  They are subject to the same types of fracture as the bones of the arm. Therefore, its treatment is the same as that of the arm, and the procedure is identical. If both bones are fractured, the leg will be displaced in all directions; if the thinner bone is fractured, the leg will be displaced forward; and if the thicker bone is fractured, and this happens lower down, then it is something that is not hidden from you.
Therefore use extension and reduction, and apply splints accordingly, except that if the fracture of the leg is severe with many fragments, the extension should be less and gentler, and you should take great care in its treatment.
And in the case of the leg, there is something additional to the treatment of the arm: when you have straightened the limb and completed all your work, take two thin strips of pine wood, the kind used in smoothing rooms, which are placed between the cracks in the planks, or they can be made from palm leaves or the like.
Choose those which are of moderate thickness, not too thin, and let their length be equal to the length of the leg from the knee downwards.  Then wrap each one with two layers of cloth along its length, and place one on one side of the leg and the other on the other side.
Let them extend from the knee to the bottom of the foot. Then bind the splints in three places, at the ends and in the middle; by this means the leg is prevented from tilting to the right or left, and is well supported. A wooden gutter along the length of the leg may also be used.
It is placed within it to keep it from moving; this is especially necessary in fractures accompanied by a wound. Then examine the leg each day, and give it the most careful attention in regard to swelling, inflammation, or anything else. If anything of this sort occurs, counteract it appropriately until it heals.  The bone of the leg unites in about thirty days or so.
Chapter Seventeen.
On fractures of the bones of the leg and toes.
As for the heel, fracture never occurs; as for the bones of the leg, fracture may occur; and as for the toes, fracture rarely occurs, but contusion is more common.
If a fracture occurs in the bones of the leg, and you see that some of those bones overlap, then let the patient place his foot on the ground upright as if walking, and then you place your foot on the elevated part of those bones and press down on it and straighten it.
until they return to their places; then apply a bandage and splint from above, and place a small board with two flat ends under the sole of the foot; then bind the foot tightly after wrapping it with cloths and whatever else is needed.
If three or four days pass, then untie the bandage, for you will find the bones straight, whether they were fractured or dislocated.  The procedure is as you see.  As for if some of the toes are fractured, set them and straighten them according to what I described to you in setting the fingers of the hand.
Then make a splint for the fractured toe, along the length of the toe and slightly wider than it, and place under the foot the board I described to you and bind it tightly. If two, three, or more bones are fractured,
then make for each toe a splint of appropriate size, placed in soft cloth, and tighten the board at the bottom of the foot, with each end of the board projecting beyond the sole of the foot for a good hold.
You should not forget to attend to all that we mentioned to you in other fractures, from the symptoms we mentioned, and treat each symptom appropriately.
Chapter Eighteen.
On the fracture of the woman's pubis and the man's penis.
When a woman's vulva is fractured, seat her cross-legged, then gently bend her backwards. Let her be held from behind, then let the midwife pack her vulva with cotton until it is full and forms a ball within her vulva.
Then the woman is shaken and her loins are raised little by little.  The cotton will then come out until it is at the opening of the vulva like a ball, and the fractured bone will return to its place. Then place a pad on her back. When she wants to urinate, remove the cotton gently so that she can urinate.
And replace it in the way it was initially inserted, then return to the first pad. Do this for seven days or so, for then it will heal. If you wish, you can take a sheep's bladder, tie a reed tube to its mouth, and insert the whole bladder into her vulva.
Then blow forcefully into the tube until the bladder inflates inside the vulva; the fracture will then return to its place. Then pack it with cotton and proceed as described until it heals. As for a fracture of the pubic bone in a man or woman, the same treatment is used for its reduction.
And its treatment is as we described for a fracture of the hip. The correct treatment for these unusual fractures, which rarely occur, especially for someone experienced and who understands this book well, is not hidden, because the fracture itself usually indicates the treatment.
Understand the correct way to reduce and bind it.  As for a fractured penis, take a goose's windpipe, insert the penis into it, then apply a bandage of cloth, bind it, and leave it for three days or so until it heals.
Chapter Nineteen.
On the reduction of fractures when accompanied by a wound.
If a fracture occurs with a wound, especially if the bone is large, such as the femur or humerus, or similar, one should immediately perform bloodletting if the conditions for bloodletting, as we have previously explained, are present.
If the wound is bleeding, one should immediately stop it by applying powdered vitriol if nothing else is available. Then, reduce the fracture on that same day and do not delay.
If no hot inflammation occurs, then leave the reduction until the ninth day until the hot inflammation subsides. Do not attempt it on the third or fourth day at all, for this will lead to bad consequences.
If the fractured bone is protruding through the skin, one should try to reposition and straighten it gently by hand with a slight pull. If you are unable to reposition and straighten it by hand, reposition it with this instrument; it is an instrument made of iron, seven or eight fingers in length. And its width should be according to the wound.
Therefore, the physician should have three or four of them, according to the need for treatment in each type of fracture. Let them be round, with a slight thickness, lest they bend when pressed during use.
And let the tip be sharp, with a curve at its end; the upper part should be thicker, and from the middle to the lower part, much thinner.  This is its image:
And it is called in Greek *peron*, meaning a small lever.  Its sharp, curved end should be placed against the end of the protruding bone and pushed with it until the bone returns and becomes somewhat straight; then, align the ends of the fracture with each other.
If the broken end is thin and the instrument does not grasp it well, then the end of that bone should be cut until the instrument can grasp it. If you are unable to reset the bone as we described, then cut it with a similar cut to those which we have mentioned.
Or saw it with one of the saws, however you can, then remove any roughness and thin flakes remaining on the bone. If, after resetting it, the patient feels severe, troublesome pain, know that the bone has not returned to its natural position.
If you can reset it to its natural position, do so, for you will greatly benefit the patient. When you have completed the setting of the bone, dip a cloth in a black astringent drink, especially if it is summer, and do not apply a poultice or anything containing fat to the wound.
Lest putrefaction and corruption occur in it. Then, apply splints when you have finished setting the bone, and leave the wound uncovered by cutting a hole in the bandages with scissors, to the size of the wound.
And be very careful not to bind the wound tightly with the fracture, for many ignorant physicians have done this, causing their patients either death, or gangrene, or tetanus. Let your binding be loose and relaxed, unlike the binding of other fractures.
If the wound is bad, or is a large wound, and you fear some of the bad symptoms we have described, and he feels troublesome pain in the place, then you should not apply splints, but make him bandages of firm cloths in place of the splints, and bind him with them.
If after a day or two you see that pus has begun to form in the wound, remove the cloth that you placed on it with the drink, then use lint and ointments with which we are accustomed to treat wounds, such as the tetrapharmacon and the like, and you should loosen the bandage.
And examine the wound every day, evening and morning, until it heals and is cured.  It is necessary to position the limb so that the pus flows down easily. If many days pass and the wound does not unite, and the pus does not cease, then know that there are small fragments of bone.
Then you should probe the wound.  Whatever of those fragments is loose, extract and remove it; and whatever is not loose, and is piercing the limb and causing pain, then strive to cut and remove it by any means you can.
If the wound shows signs of inflammation, or ulceration, or another type of corruption and putrefaction, then you should counter each symptom with a similar treatment to that previously described in its chapter.
And what you should pay attention to and keep in mind is that if a large bone is broken and protrudes from a limb, such as the femur, humerus, and similar large limbs, do not attempt to pull it or extract it. For death often results from such an action. Rather, leave it until it putrefies, for perhaps it will fall off by itself after twenty or thirty days.
Then treat the wound if you see a place for treatment; otherwise, leave it.
Chapter Twenty.
In the treatment of complications that occur after some fractures.
This complication often occurs after the healing of a fracture, especially those near the joints, making the limb unsightly and perhaps preventing the limb from its natural function.
Examine it; if the complication is soft, use astringent medicines such as aloes, frankincense, myrrh, gum tragacanth, acacia, and the like. Take some or all of these and mix them with an astringent syrup, egg white, or vinegar.
Apply it to the complication in a bandage and bind it tightly. Leave the bandage on for many days, then remove it and reapply another until the complication is gone, God willing, or bind a tight lead plate on it.
For lead has the property of removing whatever is superfluous in the limbs. If the complication has become hardened and severe, and removal is necessary, incise it from above and cut away the excess growth, or shave it off with some instrument until it is gone, and treat the wound until it heals.
Chapter Twenty-One.
On the treatment of a fracture if it heals, and the limb remains thin and not as it was originally.
If a bone fracture heals and the limb remains thin and weak, this is due to many causes. One is excessive loosening and tightening of the bandages in an improper manner; another is excessive tightening of the bandages to the point that nourishment cannot reach the limb; another is excessive and repeated manipulation; another is excessive movement at the wrong time; and another is a deficiency of blood in the patient's body and his weakness.
The treatment of this is to nourish the patient and strengthen his body so that his blood increases, and to use baths and to make him happy and joyful and so on, then applying pitch to the limb so that the pitch draws much nourishment to it, or repeatedly manipulating it with lukewarm water. until nourishment flows to it and it returns to its natural form.
Chapter Twenty-Two.
In the treatment of fractured bones which have healed crookedly and are prevented from functioning properly.
When a member that has healed after being set shows a crookedness or a projection of the fractured bone, or a complication, and the appearance of the member is thereby disfigured. However, if the member does not impede its natural function, then one should not accept the opinion of those who claim that the member should be broken from the head, as many ignorant physicians and bone-setters in our country have done.
And this action is utterly reprehensible, leading to great danger, easily resulting in harm; but if the crookedness and complication are recent, then it should be softened with water in which emollient herbs have been boiled, such as mallow leaves and root, and royal crown, and the like.
And it should be bandaged with emollient poultices, like skillfully made diachylon, or the sap of mallow root should be taken and pounded with chicken fat and goose grease and applied as a bandage, or ripe figs should be taken and pounded with pigeon droppings and similar medicines which are called inhibitors of consolidation.
And the complication may be resolved by constant, gentle rubbing with the hands, and the movement of the member in every direction at all times should be employed; but if the crookedness is old and severe and hardened, and treatment with iron is necessary.
Then it should be incised above and the connection of the bone released, and what remains of the complication or bone should be cut with delicate incisions, and gentleness should be employed with effort and care, then the wound should be treated as previously mentioned until it heals.
Chapter twenty-three.
A dislocation is the displacement of a joint from its place, which impedes movement, disfigures the member, and causes the patient severe pain and suffering.  Therefore, when a dislocation occurs, one should immediately proceed to reduce it.
One should not delay at all, for if he delays, the place will swell and become inflamed, making reduction of the dislocation difficult. Therefore, one should not delay, nor move nor stretch it while it is swollen, because this often causes the patient spasms and harmful pains.
But if that happens, one should immediately bleed the patient, then leave him until the swelling subsides somewhat, then bathe the member with warm water and oil, then reduce it gently, and treat each member as described in its place.
The chapters on dislocations are also arranged, as in fractures, from the upper part of the body to the lower.
Chapter Twenty-Four.
On the treatment of dislocation of the lower jaw.
Rarely do both jaws dislocate; their dislocation occurs in one of two ways: either they are slightly displaced and relax a little, or they are completely dislocated so that they relax. toward the chest, so that the saliva of the patient flows and he cannot retain it, nor can he close his jaw, and his tongue stumbles in speech. But if the dislocation is slight, it often returns of its own accord very easily.
But if the dislocation is complete, it should be reduced quickly and not delayed at all.  A servant should hold the patient's head, and the physician should insert the thumb of one hand into the base of the jaw inside the mouth, if the jaw is dislocated on one side.
Or he should insert both thumbs if the jaw is dislocated on both sides, and the rest of his fingers from outside to level them. He should instruct the patient to relax his jaw and release it to go to each side, and the physician should level the jaw and push it until it returns to its place.
If its reduction is difficult, especially if both jaws are dislocated, use a warm water and oil compress until their reduction is facilitated. Do not delay their reduction at all, as we said. When they return and are level, and the patient's mouth is closed and does not relax, then apply compresses with a mixture of wax, oil, and rosewater.
Then bind gently with a loose bandage. The patient should sleep on his back with his head elevated between two pillows, so that he does not move it to the right or left, and he should not strain to chew anything, but his food should be soft gruel.
Until the pain subsides and the jaw is set, let him eat whatever he wants, but let him use it gently and not strain to open his mouth when eating, drinking, or yawning, until the jaw is set and healed.
If the reduction of the jaws is difficult when they are dislocated at some time and do not return to their places, fevers and persistent headaches often result from this, and perhaps the patient's abdomen will become distended, and perhaps he will vomit pure bile. If you see this, know that it is corrupted.
And many die from what has befallen them within ten days.
Chapter Twenty-Five.
On the reduction of a dislocated clavicle and shoulder point.
As for the clavicle, it does not dislocate from the inner side because of its connection to the chest.  It may, however, dislocate outwards, and this is evident to the touch. Its treatment is that the patient should lie on his back and extend his arms.
Then press the area firmly with your hand, and it will return. Then apply a bandage and splints and bind it tightly. As for the end which is next to the shoulder and connected to it, it rarely dislocates; but if it should dislocate sometime, then it should be reduced and set as we have mentioned and as seems best to you.
Then apply a bandage and splints and bind it tightly, and instruct the patient to remain quiet and still until it heals. By this same treatment you reduce the shoulder point if it also leaves its place.
Chapter Twenty-Six.
On the reduction of the shoulder joint.
Know that the shoulder dislocates in three ways: one, that it dislocates toward the armpit, downward; the second, that it dislocates toward the chest; and sometimes it dislocates above the shoulder, and this is rare.
It does not dislocate to the rear, because of the shoulder blade, nor does it dislocate to the front, because of the nerve.  Most often it dislocates and comes out downwards toward the armpit, especially in those whose flesh is thin, because it comes out and goes in quickly in them.
But as for those whose flesh is abundant, it is otherwise, I mean that it comes out with difficulty and goes in with difficulty.  And sometimes a blow or a fall happens to some people, and the shoulder swells with a hot swelling, so he thinks that it has dislocated.  It is necessary to examine that until you ascertain its truth.
Then you proceed in its treatment.  And the dislocation is recognized if it is downward toward the armpit: if you compare the dislocated shoulder with the sound shoulder, you will find a clear difference between them, and you will find the head of the shoulder in it crooked, and under the armpit, upon touching, the head of the shoulder as if it were an egg.
And the patient is not able to raise his hand to his ear, nor to move it in all movements.  Likewise, if it dislocates toward the chest or upward, you find that apparent to the touch, it is not hidden.
And this dislocation is easy to reduce if it is recent or if the patient is a child.  Its reduction is that a servant raises his hand upward, then you place the thumbs of your hands under his armpit and raise the joint forcefully upward to its place, and the servant raises his hand and extends it upward.
Then he lowers it downward, for it returns quickly. If it does not return by what we have mentioned, and the dislocation has been for many days, then it is necessary that the patient bathe in hot water and use things that relax and soften, such as cooking the root of marshmallow, fenugreek, and rue in water, and use it.
Then the patient lies on his back, and you place under his armpit a ball of wool that is moderate between softness and firmness. Then the physician places his heel on the ball and grasps the patient's hand with his hand and extends it downward.
And he pushes the ball with his foot, then it returns to its place. And if you wish, reduce it in this way: you bring a taller man than the patient and station him at the side, and he puts his shoulder under the patient's armpit and raises his armpit upward until the patient is suspended in the air. And another servant pulls the patient's hand down to his belly. If the patient is light, then it is necessary to suspend something else on him to weigh him down, for then the jaw returns immediately. And it may also be reduced in another way.
And that is to firmly place in the ground a long piece of wood, its head round in shape like the pestle of a mortar, neither thick nor thin, then it is placed under the patient's armpit, after placing a soft rag on the head of the wood, and the patient stands along the wood.
Then you extend his hand downwards from the other side, and he extends his body also from the other side forcefully, then the joint returns to its place quickly. If it is difficult to reduce it by all that we have mentioned, then use this treatment: you take a piece of wood about two cubits long. And its width is the size of four fingers and its thickness the size of two fingers. It has a round head to facilitate its entry into the depth of the armpit in this manner:.
Then a soft rag is tied to the round head, lest the wood harm the patient. Then it is placed under the armpit, and the whole hand and arm are extended on the wood downwards, and the wood is tied to the upper arm, forearm, and hand.
Then the arm is placed on a crossbar.  The body is supported horizontally, and the hand is extended downwards, and the rest of the body is left hanging from the other side. Then the joint enters immediately. If it enters in any way possible, then a moderately sized ball of wool should be placed under the armpit.
Then the prepared bandage of mill dust, frankincense, and egg white is applied to the whole shoulder as it rotates from above. Then the ball is firmly tightened from under the armpit, and the bandage is rotated over the bandage from above, and his hand is hung around his neck.
He is left without moving his hand for seven days, and the patient's food should be reduced so that the limb may strengthen, for this is quicker for its healing. Then, after seven or five days, it is untied and exercised. If it is firm and does not relax, then it is healed.
If the joint dislocates repeatedly due to dampness or another cause, then cauterization with three cautery irons should be used, as previously described in the chapter on cauterization. If all this is done and the bandage is removed after seven days, and the joint is not stable,
the bandage and tightening were repeated many times, but it did not stabilize, and it fell and relaxed, and you were unable to raise it upwards, then know that the nerve in the head of the humerus has been severed, stretched, or relaxed. Then know that the joint will never be stable in its place.
As for the fracture that occurs towards the chest and breast and to the back, its reduction is by pushing and pulling with the hands until it returns.  All the binding and treatment are used until it heals. If, after healing, there is stiffness in the limb and slowness of movement,
let the patient use the bath many times until that stiffness softens and returns to its former nature.
Chapter twenty-seven.
In the treatment of fracture of the elbow.
The elbow joint is difficult to dislocate and also difficult to reduce.  It dislocates in all directions, especially forwards or backwards. Its dislocation is not hidden from you, because it is visible and palpable, whichever way it is dislocated.
And if you compare the dislocated elbow with the sound one, this will be clearly apparent to you by the deformity of the joint; and he cannot bend the arm nor touch his shoulder with it. You should act quickly and reduce the dislocation at once, before a hot inflammation occurs.
For if a hot inflammation occurs, reduction is difficult, and it may not heal at all, especially if the dislocation is backwards; for this is the worst of all types of dislocation, and the most painful, and often death follows.
And its reduction, if it is possible to reduce it, is done by an assistant extending his arm with both hands, and the physician's hands above and below the elbow, he pushing the joint with both thumbs, or with the heel of his hands, until it returns to its place.
But if the dislocation is forwards, it may be reduced by bending the arm forcibly until the heel of the hand strikes the opposite shoulder. If the dislocation does not reduce, employ strong traction, that is to say, let two assistants extend the arm.
And let two assistants also hold the patient, lest he move during the traction. Then the arm is rotated in every direction after a folded cloth or a broad bandage has been wrapped around the hand; and when the physician touches the joint with his hands, let him anoint them with oil, so that the joint may slip easily.
Then he pushes the joint forcibly until it returns. After its reduction, a bandage should be applied which produces constriction and drying, with white of egg, and it should be tightly bound, and the arm suspended from the patient's neck, and left for some days.
Then it is unbound; if the joint is firm in its place, unbind it and leave it.  But if you see that the joint is not yet firm, reapply the bandage and binding, and leave it also for some days until it becomes firm, then unbind it. If a swelling occurs after the reduction of the joint and its unbinding,
and there is difficulty in movement, let him use fomentations in the bath, and gentle rubbing and manipulation until it softens; or apply to the joint a piece of fat mutton, then bind it and leave it on for a day and a night, then remove it and put him in the bath; when he has perspired, rub the joint moderately.
Then reapply the fat mutton once, twice, and three times, with the bath, until it softens. And if you wish to apply to it fresh, light cow dung with fat and bind it on, do so repeatedly, for it softens and returns to its former condition.
Chapter Twenty-Eight.
On the treatment of a dislocated wrist.
The wrist is frequently dislocated, and its reduction is easier than that of other joints; however, it is necessary to hasten its reduction at the time it is dislocated, before the part becomes swollen or a hot swelling appears.
Its reduction is to place the patient's wrist on a board, and the attendant extends his hand.  The physician places his hand on the prominence of the joint and pushes it until it returns; however, it is necessary to see if the dislocation has gone to the inside of the hand.
If the dislocation is towards the outside of the hand, let the patient place the palm of his hand on the board during extension and reduction; and if the dislocation is prominent towards the back of the hand, let the back of his hand be placed on the board so that the physician's hand falls on the same prominence of the joint. If it returns immediately, well and good; otherwise, bind it with a bandage that soothes the swelling.
And leave it; do not manipulate it again, for it will not tolerate it, and you will not be able to reduce it after a few days have passed, except that the joint will remain dislocated, and this will not harm the patient, except if the hand becomes weak and unable to grasp anything.
Then you know that the nerve has been severed or bruised; there is no remedy for this except cauterization, which may or may not be effective.  When the reduction of the wrist is complete, apply the bandage we have described, then bind it tightly and leave it for five days.
Then unbind it and exercise the hand. If its movement is difficult and there is any stiffness, soften it with warm water and massage repeatedly until it becomes supple.
Chapter Twenty-Nine.
On the treatment of dislocated fingers.
Fingers may be dislocated in any direction. If a finger is dislocated towards the back or palm of the hand, extend the finger and push the dislocation with your thumb until it returns to place. Then bind the end of the finger and suspend it towards the direction in which it was dislocated.
Leave it for two days, then release it and extend it until it is straight that day. If it is at night, bind it in the same way; continue to unbind it during the day, exercising it with movement, and bind it at night. Do this for several days until it strengthens.
Do the same if it is dislocated towards the palm of the hand; bind it towards that same direction and do as you did before until it heals.  Do likewise if it is dislocated in any other direction.
Chapter Thirty.
On the treatment of the dislocation of the vertebrae of the back.
If a vertebra of the back or neck is completely dislocated, or if many vertebrae are displaced from their positions, there is no treatment, for death quickly overtakes the patient.
The sign of this is that the patient's stool is involuntarily passed; he cannot retain it, and often some of his limbs, either his legs or arms, or one of them, become relaxed. But if only one vertebra is displaced from its position, it often returns to its place.
And its displacement is in four directions. That which is displaced backward is called a hump. As for its treatment, if the hump has occurred from childhood, there is no treatment for it, nor any cure at all.  But that which occurs from a fall, a blow, or the like…
The ancients have mentioned it with various treatments in lengthy descriptions, most of which are not beneficial. I have shortened this, and what little I have included suffices for much of what they brought, by my approximation to the meaning and my explanation of it, and I have depicted the instrument differently from what they explained and described.
I say that the hump which occurs in the front of the chest has no remedy nor cure, and the same applies to that which occurs to the sides as well.  Only that which occurs in the back is treatable, in the way I describe, which is that you extend the patient on his face.
on a level bench near a wall, and spread a wet mat beneath him so that his chest is not harmed. Then place an upright plank, fixed in a hole in the ground at the head end of the bench towards his head, and another plank towards his feet at the other end of the bench.
And a servant holds the plank—let it not be firmly fixed in the hole—and another servant holds the other in that manner. Then wrap around the patient's chest and under his armpits with a soft, tight bandage, and extend the end of the bandage to the plank at his head and tie it there.
Then tighten with another bandage above his hips, above his knees, and at his ankles. Then gather all the bandages and tie them to the other plank at his feet. Then each servant pulls on his plank with the bandage, and the planks do not move from their fixed positions.
Except that they are not fixed, as we said. And the physician places his hands on the hump forcefully until it returns, or he places a board on it, then he leans on the board with his feet until it returns.  If it does not return with this treatment, then take a board about three cubits long.
and make a hole in the wall, which we said should be near the patient, into which the end of the board is inserted. Then place the middle of the board on the hump, and the physician places his feet on the other end and pulls strongly until the hump is compressed.
and it returns to its place. And if you wish to make it with a hand-twisted screw, then plant two planks in the ground at the head of the patient at the far end of the shop, a cubit long, and at his feet at the far end of the shop also two planks.
Let the distance between each plank be a span. And a hole is made in each plank through which the screw passes. And all the wood is firmly fixed in the ground so that it does not move at all. And insert a round rod, which is the screw in which the bandage is twisted, into the holes of the two planks.
And at its end is a hole in which a rod a cubit long is fixed with what is twisted. And in the other two planks similarly. Then tighten the bandage which you tightened on the chest of the patient in the screw which is at his head, and the bandages which you tightened on his legs in the screw which is at his feet.
Then a servant stands at each screw, twisting his hand with the instrument which twists the screw, and the physician straightens the hump as we have explained. And this is an image of the screw, the shop and the patient:
Then after the vertebra returns and the place is straightened, it is necessary to apply a drying compress with egg white, then a splint, and then to place on top of the compress a splint of wood, its width being three fingers or so, and its length being sufficient to cover the place of the hump.
And on some sound beads, then it is bound with the appropriate bandage, and the patient uses light food until he recovers. If some swelling remains in the place at the end of the healing, then it is necessary to use softening and relaxing medicines along with the use of the splint which we described for a long time.
And a lead plate may be used in that. And a swelling may appear at the end of the vertebrae of the back, and it is thought to be a dislocation, and it may be an extra bone that has grown. Do not treat this with this treatment, for death may result.
Chapter Thirty-One.
In the treatment of the dislocated hip.
Know that the hip joint and the shoulder joint are only subject to dislocation, and they are not subject to what happens to other joints of slight displacement and depression, and the hip joint is dislocated in four ways.
That is, it is dislocated inwards, outwards, forwards, and backwards, and most often it is dislocated inwards, and rarely forwards or backwards, and the sign of its dislocation inwards is that if you compare the sound leg of the patient with the affected one, it will be longer.
And the knee will be more prominent than the sound one, and the patient will not be able to bend his leg at the groin, and the area next to the groin will be clearly swollen because the head of the femur has gone there.
And the sign of that which is dislocated outwards is that its symptoms are the opposite of these symptoms, and the sign of that which is dislocated forwards is that he extends his leg completely, but he cannot bend it without pain in the knee, and if he tries to walk, he cannot do so forwards.
And he retains his urine, and his groin swells, and when walking, he treads on his heel, and the sign of that which is dislocated backwards is that he cannot extend the knee, nor can he bend it before bending the groin, and his leg is also shorter than the other.
And the groin is relaxed, and the head of the femur is clearly at the position of the loin, and as for the reduction of the types of this dislocation, it is that you should look, for if the dislocation is old, having remained with its possessor for a long time and he has not attempted to reduce it and it has remained in its state, then there is absolutely no treatment for it.
Then it should not be treated, and as for the one whose dislocation is recent, and whatever type it is of the four types of dislocation, then hasten to twist the joint and stretch it inwards and outwards, and move it right and left, perhaps...
it returned and did not need any other treatment. If it does not return like this, then you should prepare a strong and helpful assistant who stretches his leg from below, either with his hands or with a bandage tied around his leg above the knee, and another assistant who stretches it from above.
so that he puts his hands under his armpits, then he tightens a soft bandage around the base of the thigh, and another third assistant holds the end of the bandage, and the stretching is either from the front from the groin to the clavicle, or from behind to the back.
And their stretching is all at once until the patient is raised with his body from the ground and remains suspended.  For this type of stretching is a common type for the four types. If the dislocation returns as we said, otherwise, each type needs what I mention of specific treatment.
As for its specific reduction, if the dislocation is inwards, then the patient should lie on his sound side, then a bandage is applied to the base of the thigh between the head of the thigh and the area under the groin.
Then the bandage is stretched upwards from the groin to the upper body towards the clavicle, then another strong and helpful assistant takes it with his arms and embraces the thick part of the affected thigh and stretches outwards with a strong pull, then it returns to its place.
And this type is easier than the other types of treatment by which this member is reduced. If it is difficult for you and it does not respond to this type of treatment at all, then the patient's legs should be tied together with a strong, soft bandage on the heels and knees.
And the distance between each one and its neighbor is four fingers, and the affected leg is extended more than the other by two fingers. Then the patient is suspended by the head from a wooden beam in the house, and his distance from the ground is two cubits.
Then you order a strong boy to embrace the head of the femur, and another boy hangs onto the patient, and the other boy embracing the femur pushes strongly, then the joint returns to its place quickly. As for its specific reduction if the dislocation is outwards.
The patient should lie on the bench as we described for the hunchback, and a bandage is tightened on his affected leg and chest. Then two wooden beams are placed, one at his feet and the other at his head, then an additional beam is placed in the middle of the bench, very securely fastened.
A wet cloth is wrapped around it so that it does not harm the patient, so that the wooden beam is between his thighs so that he does not slip downwards when extended. Then each servant pulls to his side and the physician sets the hip with his hands, if it responds to reduction.
Otherwise, place the board on him and compress it as we mentioned in the case of the hunchback, except that the patient should lie on his healthy side. And if the dislocation is forward, then the patient's leg should be extended while he is in this same position on the bench.
The physician places the palm of his right hand on the affected groin, then presses it with the other hand, and with that extends the pressure downwards towards the knee. And if the dislocation is backward, then the patient should not be extended downwards.
And it is raised from the ground, but rather it should be placed on something solid, as it should also be if his hip is dislocated outwards, according to what we mentioned concerning his lying on the bench, and he is on his face and the bandages are tight.
As we said before, and the compression with the board should also be used on the place to which the joint has gone.  Then, if the hip joint is completely reduced as it should be, and the sign of its reduction will not be hidden from you, which is that you extend the patient's legs.
Then if you see them level and the patient can flex and extend the leg without difficulty, know that the member has returned as it should. Then, bring the thighs together, apply the bandage, and bind it with a turban tightly so that the hip does not move in any direction.
And the patient must remain still for three or four days. Then untie the bandage and compare the leg with the other. If you see them equal in size, know that the reduction has been successful, and allow the patient to walk. And if you see any laxity in it…
then return and bandage it and tighten it according to your first tightening, and also leave it for three days, then untie it, and he should walk slowly on it for days until it strengthens, God willing.
Chapter thirty-two.
In the treatment of the reduction of the knee.
The knee dislocates in three ways: inwards, outwards, and downwards, that is, backwards; it does not dislocate forwards at all. And the sign of its dislocation is that you order the patient to press his leg to his thigh.
If it does not adhere to the thigh, then know that the knee is dislocated.  The treatment of all types of its dislocation is that you seat the patient sitting with his legs extended if he is able to do so, and a servant sits behind him to hold his waist and twist him slightly backwards, then you sit on his thighs.
And you place your back against his face and place his leg between your legs, then you grasp his knee with your hands and interlock them between your fingers on his knee, then you press with your hands the sides of his knee strongly, and another servant extends his leg until the knee returns to its place.
And the sign of its return is that the leg adheres to the thigh softly without force. Then bandage it and adhere the leg to the thigh, then bind them both with a bandage for three or four days, then release them. And he should only use a little walking for some days until it strengthens.
If it is difficult for you to return it by what we have described, then use strong traction with the bandages that I previously described in the treatment of the hip, until it returns.
Chapter Thirty-Three.
In the treatment of the dislocation of the heel.
The heel may be dislocated slightly, and it may be completely dislocated.  Its dislocation is either inward or outward, and the sign of its dislocation is that you see the heel swollen and prominent in the direction in which it dislocated.
As for the treatment of its slight displacement, it is easy to return it;  he should gently stretch it with his hands and straighten it until it returns. As for its treatment if it is completely dislocated, then it is necessary that you seat the patient sitting, and a strong servant holds him from behind his back at his waist.
Then you grasp his foot with your right hand from above and with your left hand from below his foot at the place of the heel. Then pull the foot towards you with your right hand, then with the left twice, then straighten it with the left and push the front of the foot with the right towards the leg without force.
Do this twice as we have described, then push the front of the foot to the leg on the third occasion while you pull at the heel. If it returns in one or two times in this manner and you see the foot straight, otherwise repeat the action on it, for it will return.
If it refuses to return as we have described, then lay the patient on his back on the ground and firmly plant a wooden peg in the ground so that it lies between his thighs, having wrapped a cloth around it lest it injure the patient. Then a servant should hold his thigh.
Then another servant stretches the leg, either with his hands or with a rope that he ties around the neck of the leg. Then each servant stretches in the opposite direction to his companion, the peg remaining between the patient's thighs, holding it so that his body is not pulled downwards during the stretching. Then the physician straightens the joint with his hands.
And another servant holds the healthy leg below, for the joint returns quickly. When the joint returns and you see that its return is correct, apply the compress and bandage and tighten it with firm bandages and bind the foot with the bandage below. It is necessary to protect the nerve which is above the heel from behind.
lest the bandage be too tight and injure him. Then leave it for two or three days. If the bandage loosens, tighten it, then loosen it on the third or fourth day. And the patient must abstain from walking for forty days.
If he attempts to walk before this time, you cannot be sure that the joint will not become dislocated and spoiled, and will not then accept treatment. If a hot swelling appears, then you should use what we have previously described in other places for its treatment and poulticing until it disappears.
Chapter Thirty-Four.
In the treatment of the dislocation of the toes of the foot.
You should set what has been dislocated with a gentle pull without force; this is not difficult, but easy. If the dislocation is in some of the joints of the instep, then the patient should place his foot on a level surface.
Or on a board while he is standing like one walking; then you place your foot on the dislocated joint, and then press down on it firmly until it returns and you see that it is straight, and no prominence appears in the place.
Then place a board under the sole of his foot which takes the whole foot, having two ends, then bind it tightly and firmly for three days, then unbind it and keep him from walking for many days until it strengthens and you are safe from recurrence, God willing.
Chapter thirty-five.
On the types of dislocation which occurs with a wound, or with a fracture, or with both together.
When something of that happens and you attempt its treatment and setting, it frequently results in death.
Therefore, no one should undertake the treatment of such a thing except one who is skillful in the art, long experienced, gentle, compassionate, careful, not rash nor impetuous, and that he use in the beginning only those medicines which calm hot swellings and leave the patient alone.
By God's power, except that for which you hope for safety from harm with mildness of the disease, and some hope appears to you in it, then return it at once in the first instance before the hot swelling occurs.
If the member returns as you wish, then use the treatment which calms hot swellings and treat the wound with what is suitable for it of drying ointments; if the dislocation is with a fracture and there occur in the bone separated fragments, then remove them. And observe in that what we mentioned in the simple illnesses, what preceded in their places.
And strive your best and refrain from entering the path of risk, as I have previously advised you, for that is more lasting for your reputation and safer for your honor, God willing.
